1 1 BRETHREN,—The
1 / text upon which lam to
preach to you this morn-
ing will be found in the Second
Book of Samuel, the sixth chap-
ter, portions of the 20th and 21st
verses—
And Michal . . . said, How
glorious was the king to day, who
uncovered himself to day in the
eyes of the handmaids of his ser-
vants, as one of the vain fellows
shamelessly uncovered himself! And David said
unto Michal, It was before the Lord, which chose
me before thy father, and before all his house, to

BEFORE THE LORD,
You may be aware, dear brethren, that many
have declared that it is inconsonant with
the profession and dignity of a minister of the
gospel to interchange the sacred gown of his office
with the costume of a footballer. I therefore
propose to show you that the game of football
makes for righteousness no less than any other
pursuit in which it is lawful for men to engage,
and that it is many times warranted and defended
in the Sacred Writ.
I will divide my discourse under six heads
and a conclusion, to wit-
Firstly, op Football Generally.
Football is a bodily exercise, and though bodily
exercise profiteth little, godliness profiteth much
(I. Tim. iv., 8). Yet surely we can rightly com-
bine little and much, endeavouring to keep the
unity of the Spirit (Eph. iv., 3). Indeed, the vir-
tue of combination cannot be praised too highly ;
and it is woe to him who is alone when he falleth
(Eccl. iv., 10) and loseth (Matt, x., 39). Wherefore
it is nothing inconsistent with a minister’s
caUing to lead to you a team of footballers,
seeing that we are told if thou bring for-
wardfs) on their journey after a godly
sort, thou shalt do well (111. John, 6). And
in divers places we are assured that the sport of
football, when righteously conducted, is accounted
no evil. For when Abner said to Joab, Let the
young men now arise and play before us, Joab
said, Let them arise (11. Sam. ii., 14), and Joab
was a lieutenant of David, who was a man after
God’s own heart. And the attempt of some to
say that football is kin with the worship of Baal,
who was a false god, has no warranty. So it is
a pattern worthy of imitation, forgetting those
things which are behind,, and reaching forth Unto
those things which are before, to press toward the
mark for the prize (Philip, iii., 13, 14).
Secondly, op Running and Kicking.
We are exhorted to - “so run that ye may ob-
tain ” (I. Cor. ix, 24) the goal; to be sober, be
vigilant, because your adversary is the devil, and
as a roaring lion walketh about (I. Pet. v, 8); for
the race is not necessarily ,to the swift or the battle
to the strong ; but time and chance happeneth to
them all (Eccles. ix, 11); wherefore let him that
thinketh he standeth take heed lest he fall (I. Cor.
x, 12). As for me, I will walk in mine
integrity, and- when my foot standeth in
an even place, in the congregations will I bless
the L*ord (Ps. xxvi., 12). And remembering that
Abram only armed his trained servants (Gen.
xiv,, 14) let us avoid the pernicious example of
Jeshurun, who waxed fat and kicked (Deut. xxxii.,
15), for I doubt not that falling headlong he
burst asunder in the midst, and all his bowels
gushed out (Acts i., 18). But when you kick, re-
member that it is said I will make the place of
my feet glorious (Is. lx., 13), and when David
prepared a place (I. Chron. xv., 1) we
are sure that it was fulfilled as it was
spoken by the prophet, saying, Behold, I
will raise them—to wit, goals—out of the place
(Joel iii. 7). It is a redeeming feature in an evil
character that Judas knew the place (John xviii.
2), and possibly his comrades said of him as we of
brother Adamson, “Thy foot for the place”
(Josh. v. 15).
Thirdly, of Passing.
Here I may truly declare to my adversaries that
yourselves know how ye ought to follow us, for we
behaved not ourselves disorderly among you (I.
Thess. iii., 7). But I-justly say it is woe to those
who cry Pass, Pass, when there is no pass, and,
being our enemies, shout, Pass to us (Luke, xvi.,
26), saying to one another Mehe, mene, tekel
upharsin (Dan., v., 2*5), which in the vulgar
tongue may be rendered, Men, men, tackle
your parson, and whispering “As the Lord
live'th, I will run after him and take
somewhat of him ” (11. Kings, v., 20), For it is
impious for an opponent to act like those of whom
it is said, Now the young men did diligently
observe whether any thing would come from him,
and did hastily catch’it, and they said thy brother
Ben-hadad (I. Kings, xx., 32) seeing that I have
been deceived and passed the ball, and my brother
Ben' had not that, nor any of my side. For God
requireth that which is passed (Eccl. iii., 15) and
who is on the Lord’s side let him come to me
(Exod, xxxii., 26). Apd for this cause we do not
cease to praY for you (Col. i., 9).
Fourthly, op the‘Scrum and Other Matters.
It is hard for thee to kick against the pricks
(Acts ix. s),‘for 1 give thee charge in the sight of
God, who quiokeneth'all .-things (I Tim. vi. 13).
And to those of'mine I say “Vos estis scrutam
terrre ’’ —which, being .interpreted, means “You
are the scr-um of the earth.’” f Hast thou marked
the"'old way ? '(Job xxii. '15); mark the perfect
man '(ll. Sam. ii. 14). When thou passes* ....
I will be with thee ’ (Is. xliii. 2); foot for
foot (Deut. xix. 21); but there was no
place for the beast that was under me
to pass (Neh. ii., 14). And I poured out
my complaint (Ps. cxlii., 2), saying “ He breaketh
me with breach upon breach, he runneth upon me
like a giant.” Yet let enemies know of me, while
they reel .to and fro and stagger like a drunken man,
and are at their witY end (Ps. cvii., 27), that
‘He will surely violently turn and toss thee like
A ball into a iarge country ”,(Exod. xxxiii., 19).
For to their power I bear record, yea and beyond
their power they were willing (11. Cor. viii., 3).
They parted my raiment among them (John, xix.,
24), they came upon me as a wide breaking in of
waters . . . my skin is black upon me (Job, xxx.,
14, 31); lam escaped by the skin of my teeth
(Job, xix., 20). Wherefore I pray, Make the
heart of this people heavy . . . and shut their
eyes . . . lest they convert (Is., vi., 10). For I
am the flower of the field (Cant., ii., 1) ; and the
pi-iest’s lips should keep knowledge, and they
should seek the. law at his mouth (Mai. ii., 7).
Fifthly, of Costume.
We are told that “ they shall have linen bonnets
upon their heads, and shall have linen breeches
upon their loins; they shall not gird themselves
with anything that causeth sweat ” (Ezek. xliv.
18). Let it not be said that “thy tacklings are
loosed ” (Is. xxxiii. 23); and hold fast that which
thou hast (Rev. iii. 11).
Sixthly, of Barrackers.
These are persons I would gladly condemn, were
it not that we read an implied approval of their
habit in the holy writings, for when Barak pur-
sued Sisera (Judges iv. 22) Sisera was discomfited,
and the loss of him was an occasion of rejoicing.
In Conclusion.
In conclusion, dearly beloved brethren, I say
unto you whether therefore ye eat, or drink, or
whatsoever ye do, do all to the glory of God
(I Cor. x, 31). Be not forgetful to entertain
strangers : for thereby some have entertained
angels unawares (Heb. xiii, 2). Though Ibe rude
in speech, yet not in knowledge; but have been
thoroughly made manifest among you in all
things (II Cor. xi, 6). Wherefore, my beloved, as
ye have always obeyed, not in my presence only,
but now much more in my absence, work out your
own salvation in fear and trembling (Philip, ii, 12).
Amen.

Newer French Poetry.
VI.-STEPHANE MALLARME (1842-1898).
outer life of Rimbaud and Verlaine is
| picturesque and adventurous :of Stephane
JL Mallarme there is nothing to record be-
yond his devotion to poetry, an absolute
devotion undisturbed even by the human
and excusable desire for contemporary fame, a
heroic devotion that even imperilled his posthu-
mous glory. To his lot fell the heavy duty of
thinking out afresh the whole poetic problem, of
envisaging the poet’s task in its rarest form, of re-
fining the poet’s medium till it should be adequate
to that task. His was the exceptional destiny to
conceive the perfect work, to labour at it for more
than 20 years in the intervals of ungrateful drud-
gery, and to die, exhausted by the tension of those
years, when freedom at last had come. Thus the
work of his life, unfinished, yet even so surely
more precious to humanity than any iEneid, was
burnt, as he had the r courage to order : and he has
left us but a few fragments in verse, a few disser-
tations in pro?e, -the conquered borders at least of
a new domain.
Mallarme himself would have been the first to
remind us that each human destiny must be
judged strictly by what it has achieved. There is
a unity in his. It is good that we should have
poetry, and it is therefore good that the poet
should live by a compromise between his ideal and
his practical limitations. But it. is also right and
good that a poet should occasionally anse who
stakes himself on accomplishing, or at least bring-
ing nearer, the impossible. Mallarme did not
achieve what was probably not possible to him or
tp any other man. But there is a curious accord
between his theory that style is more valuable for
what it suggests than for what it states and the
fact that his ideal work remains a hint. Hinted
at least it is, and that in every line of his writings,
an ineluctable presence, brought definitely nearer
to human vision, and nothing that he has left can
be understood witho'ut reference to it.
The poems, for instance, are, up to a certain
date, a prelude to this work, after that date, when
his labour on it had begun, footnotes to it.
In the eleven poems contributed to the Parnasse
of 1866 we find him already meditating on the
destinies of that human instinct whereof it is the
poet’s glory to be the living symbol; we find the
Parnassian form lending impersonal breath to an
intense lyric aspiration towards the anterior and
paradisal beauty, to an exasperated sense of
the sufferings imposed by the world on the
captured spirit. Eor these were the days
when Mallarme had not yet found his place
in a life that was in truth a grinding between the
upper and nether millstone, between the “ cruel
Ideal ” and stepmother Circumstance; the days
when Manet painted him, as Rodenbach describes
the picture, with his tragic face “livid as with
the lightnings of an inner tempest.” The crisis of
this struggle is marked by the Herodiadc of the
second Parnasss, a variant of the Narcissus myth,
a mysterious expression o*f the solitary anguish of
the soul that has retired from life to dwell in the
house of its contemplation, too often a crypt chill
with despair of
That /,/ lory for whose sake of old she fled
Adorable youth of roses glowing red
Under the native blue.
The beginnings of serenity are, seen in the piece
herewith translated, the Funeral Toast to ThAophile
Gautier. The poet has now reached the secret of
the spirit’s destiny: to give the earth a sense,
creating of it an Eden; then to vanish in the
universal Idea, of which it is a fiction. This
Platonist strain has come into English with
Adonais. Mallarme continues it in the famous
A fternoon of a Faun, rejected by the Parnasse in
1875,‘ itself the first note of the new universal poem
that now’arose before him.
The leading idea of all Mallarm4’a poetry,
written or unwritten, the ground-idea of all poetry,
was humorously expressed by him in conversation,
thus: “You can’t get along without Eden.”
Eden must be realised in art, apart from life ;
a possession not for one, since the individual
here is marred and broken, but for all :
a promised land into which only the type
may enter. Poetry realises for us this type, this
Eden-life, by its fusion of natural and spiritual
fact, by the union of all the divers faculties of
man, intellectual, passional, and sensual, in the
one imaginative act. For the imaginative act is
not, as vulgarly held, the irresponsible creation of
unrealities : imagination is a faeulty that perceives,
outside of the dusty life of outer weariness, the
adequacy of our spirit to those only perfect things,
the things of beauty; that unites all hints thereof
into a perfect life, the only real one because alone
worth being real. For Mallarme the guide
to this free and adequately beautiful life was
an unparalleled insight into the very stuff of
poetry, the analogies that obtain between things
outside their dreary mechanism. He saw in the
movement of an eddy, the dance of waterflies, the
sweep of a planet, and the movements of thought,
one rhythmical idea; ‘ a soul, ’he said, ‘is the inner
aspect of a form.’ The universe is one vast rhythm
and language exists to give it a soul. The perfect
work would be the notation of just that rhythmic
quality, that ‘ musicality ’ of all things, the hier-
archic manifestation of all analogies. But this
implies a revision of poetic form.
The early poems, that drew from Gautier the
remark ‘somewhat wilful extravagance,’ are
marked already by an extraordinary fusion of
music and developing logic. The verse is
dense with richness; every syllable is in its
right place as regards significance and as
regards sonority; and none goes by without
yielding its due tribute (it is noted of Mallarme
that, in his talk, he seemed to dwell on every
syllable). The later poetry, from H6rodiade
onward, holds in every word a complexity of
super-posed signification, in every line a com-
plexity of concerted melodies. Mallarme had
begun to obey the law that for the poet no thought
exists except as an image, an inseparable unity of
significance and material beauty; and that the
image should be directly expressed, without intro-
duction of unnecessary matter, Of course, if you
adopt a scientific procedure, you can, of two har-
monious elements, take each separately, state the
fact that there is a harmony between them, and
lay bare the process by which they are harmon-
ized. A great many—l will not say how many
—poets put all this into their poetry, the
passages that have really passed through the
crucible of imagination being borne up on a dense
stratum of explanatory affidavit. This certainly
conciliates the reader who wants to run. Mal-
larme conceived that verse should present only
the completed work of the imagination. How he
acted up to this belief the accompanying transla-
tion, if it is what it was meant to be, will show.
There is no separate enunciation of the idea, no
separate description of the object, and then sud-
den fusion of the two. The logic of the poem is
that of the image : the handling is similar to that
of music, the art most closely akin to poetry. Sets
of images are chosen, containing their own signi-
ficance, and harmonious among themselves, like
the chords of music : instead of description, suc-
cessive aspects of the image are presented. As
the chords of a musical piece progress in relation
to a certain melody, so are these image-complexes
led by the emotion, which determines the sonority
of the words: as the music chords wax and wane
in complexity so the images : counterpoint being
paralleled by the manner in which an analogy will
summon up distant and kindred analogies, by the
manner in which the significations lie within each
other, progressively more general.
The result of all this, we are told, is obscurity.
Who is it that tells us so ? The Parisian jour-
nalist, promoted critic, pontificating in the name
of “ national clarity.”
’Tis a fable which we should be wary in accept-
ing. Itecent events have shown that the French
mind in general enjoys no special prerogative of
clearness in the formation and association of
ideas : it can confound an ideal abstraction with
certain individuals not to be named here and
attribute to these the respect supposed to be due
to that. Examination of its literature shows that
“clarity ” is just a convention enabling the reader
to run ; the demand of an easily fatigued mind
that dislikes being stirred anywhere but on its
rhetorical surface, that hates being shown any
other than the immediate, professional, official
aspect of the world. “Social” in its essence,
French literature, as Brunetiere confesses, has
been obliged to forego the higher lyrism. The
Frenchman loves a formula and his “clarity” is
generally just neat expression of the obvious and
inessential. Mallarme refused the inessential. —
Parallel with this, one may observe how his poetry,
synthetic and concentrated, has been charged
with “distance from life,” “cryptic chill,” etc.,
by those who, accustomed to verse which prepares
its poetic moments not merely by gratuitous ex-
planation but also by exciting a generous wealth
of extra-poetic emotion, moral or sensual, simply
cannot assimilate the unadulterate ecstasy of
song.—Yet the poet, working a rarer life out
of what we choose to call “ life, ” cannot remain
on our fatigued level of intelligence or sensitivity.
Transposition is the prime necessity of his art.
All that his people have a right to demand of him
is that he respect their language; not that he
adopt their diurnal or journalistic defilement of it.
He will, on the one hand, reach backward to its
historic genius, on the other forward, to new and
natural developments.
Now a large part of Mallarme’s effort was
directed to a reform of French syntax. Hitherto,
he remarked, the language had been at its best cn
ner/lu/d, in memoirs or correspondence. New deep-
going methods of imagination demanded new
methods of expression, deep-going change. If you
reject all but the refined analogy, not merely must
the word reveal underlying harmonious senses,
not merely must the rhythm be a complex
of melody, the movement of the sense must also
be determined by the image, and your syntactical
forms jtmist be sought in the repertory of nature.
Thus in the Afternoon of a Faun the design of the
sense, suddenly flashing here, there veiled, is the
very play of the afternoon light amid the forest
that, in all its leaves, sings with the faun. Again,
he who tracks beauty to its lair finds mystery, and
he who will sing essential beauty must sing mys-
teriously. Of course the analytic-scientific method
is open to him if he choose ; he can, like Hugo
himself, tack on an occasional 0 mystere! Mal-
larme, who, since Hugo covered the whole poetic
field in a horizontal fashion, was compelled, like
Baudelaire, to drive his shafts deep, preferred to
make mystery an aura exhaled from the verse.
People are apt to confound this with obscurity.
But the word has nothing to do with Mallarme, a
spirit of exceptional lucidity, an artist heroically
bent on absolute unity between his poetic thought
and his poetic form.
The poetry of Mallarme, extremely quintes-
sentialized, always presents the sublime ex-
quisitely: it was possible for him to- write most
imaginative verse on a fan.* In this respect, as
in many others, he was the man of his poetry.
Those who knew'him bear witness to the complex
impression he produced, that at once of the “ com-
plete man, ” the intelligence vaster than his work,
and the friend of subtle, more than feminine,
charm. Yet, for all his potent attraction, he
seemed always “to remain with himself alone for
witness ”: his conversation, from which so many
learned so much, seemed to be but the continua-
tion of a monologue which he never ceased to
carry on with himself. Physically even, he im-
pressed one as being beyond his really small
stature. His eyes were strange, filled with golden
points of light, so that more than one personality
seemed to gaze at you. And he bore a strange re-
semblance to his own faun: just as he was,
above all, a sylvan poet, “like Pan listening to
the murmurs of the forest.” Year after year his
one delight was to escape to his beloved Valvins,
where the Seine spreads broad as a lake in the
forest-amphitheatre of Fontainebleau ; and, now
that he is gone, one is glad to think that he lies
there, like Merlin,
Wrapped round with deep soft spells in dim Brace-
liande.
C.B.
FUNERAL TOAST
from the Gautier Memorial Volume
1873.
O emblem of our hap, whom fate design'd /
Think not that towards some magic hope divined
In the dim corridor distraught I hold
Wreath’d with a monster’s suffering in gold
My empty cup, libation of white fear l
Nor deem me answer’d if thy shade appear.
Whom in a place of porphyry I hears’d
And crush’d the torch-flame piously revers’d
Where the dense gates with iron clangour daunt.
We know, whom this our festival, to chaunt
Simply the poet’s absence, chose, not fond,
That all of thee lies there pavilion’d:
Save that the dome of art, thy tended trust
Unto the base and common hour of dust,
Now thro ’ this pane the west is proud to imblaze
Is mingled with the passing godhead’s rays.
Magnificent, in lone totality,
Exhaled as thine, their pride were plain a lie,
Yon haggard crowd’s ! by its own din betray’d
Dim wretchedness that death will thin to shade.
But, the toalls’ blankness thro’ their draping clear,
I scorn’d the lucid horror of a tear
Did one of these that pass unvocal, proud
Of blind immurement in a wandering shroud,
Unanguish’d by my sacred verse, assume
The hero-maid that fronts his after doom.
Hark, in the gathering mirk vindictive, pour'd
On eager ivind of words that he ignored
The void to him that teas a man of old
Boars like some fear o’ the dark ; “ Tell, if thou
hold :
Remembrance of horizons, what is Earth ? ”
Then rings from hall to hall of space its mirth
Echoing the dwindling cry : “I cannot tell 1”
But he, our Master, with clear gaze could quell
Along his path the troubled Eden-lures
Whose swooning thrill in his sole voice conjures,
Mysterious named, the Lily and the Rose.
Shall nought of him be left that haunts our shows ?
All ye, forget that rude and dark belief.
No ghost of genius lingers, begging grief.
I, heedful of your pious care, would see
Surviving him, that y ester a tranquilly
Evanish'd in that sacerdotal tire
The gardens of our tarrying-place inspire,
A sacramental stir upon the air
Of words, rant parole and great chalice bare
Which the pellucid gaze dispers’d in shower
Of diamond yet on each unfading flower
High-isolates amid the dying ray !
For there, in sooth, all our true boskets stay
And each true-poet, humble, with wide-hung arm
Forbids them when the rash dream threatens harm:
That on the morning of his great repose
When ancient death bids him like Gautier close
Eternally the sacred eyes nor sing
Some vista's term may tributary bring
The sepulchre seal’d fast on mortal blight,
Tenacious silence built, and massive night.
STEPHANE MALLARME.
Trad. de C.B.

SPECIAL NOTICE
THE AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL PROVIDENT SOCI ETY is prepared to LEND in any amounts,
at lowest current rates, upon FREEHOLD and LEASEHOLD CITY and SUBURBAN PROPERTIES ; on
FREEHOLD and CONDITIONALLY-PURCHASED PASTORAL and FARMING LANDS.
LOANS can be arranged either for a fixed term, or repayable by instalments of principal and interest
extending over long periods.
Arrangements have been made, whereby valuations for country properties can be made for a very mode-
rate fee, to be paid as evidence of lona fides on the part of the applicant.
The legal expenses in connection with mortgages are also on a specially low scale.
The Society is also prepared to entertain proposals for purchasing Reversionary Interests in Estates, whether
in this colony or elsewhere, or to lend upon the security of the same on liberal terms.
Forms of proposal may be obtained on application at the Head Office, or any District Office of the Society.
Head Office, 87 Pitt-st., Sydney.
RICHARD TEECE, General Manager.
ROBERT B. CAMERON, Secretary.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to tneir advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman ; is also a
buyer of all other Australian seems.*
Moule, M.L.A., objects to Woman Suffrage
for Vic. on the ground that it will expose his
‘ wife, sister and daughter ” to the danger of being
canvassed by male politicians. Moule lodged his
protest quite solemnly. When the House guf-
fawed, the humorless legal gentleman said, “ It is
very easy to laugh, but it will be a very serious
thing to have male canvassers asking women for
their votes, and specifying certain classes of legis-
lation for which a particular candidate would
vote.” What, in the name of Social Purity, is the
alarmed Moule thinking about ?
Francesco.
A low sweet light of sunset on his face,
Francesco lay upon his dying bed,
Beauty in dust, last of his ancient race,
And, as he neared the dead,
Before his eyes,
So faded now, he saw the past arise.
O fleeting past! Within his antique cell
And fragrant cloister, full of sacred thought,
Dream gave to dream from dawn till evening bell,
By tranquil beauty wrought
And old repose
Made fine by scent of violet and rose.
No Gothic blossoming on beam or door,
But all is simple, white, save for a stain
From saintly robe of long-forgotten lore,
Painted with loving pain
On sweet old glass
With graver hues of wind-swept sky and grass.
And, as he gazed, faint undertones were near
Of yellow afternoon, when time delays
How tenderly ! and solitude is dear,
And on the old dream-ways
In subtle fairy dew
The heart fares forth to find Romance come true.
He wandered through the corridors again,
And, sighing, passed from sunlight into gloom
Of old monastic church, where, pale with pain,
Retiring to the tomb,
The brethren stood
Beneath their canopies of rich dim wood.
Ah, what a pang of sadness took the heart
As colour died to penitential grey
On foliaged roof and choir, save where, apart,
The tapers’ golden day
Made misty light
On bronze and marble tombs of priest and knight.
And when with delicate preluding strain
That shivered in the soul, the soul of youth,
The violins began their yearning pain
And sang of ancient ruth ;
How sad, how deep,
The Latin words that seemed to sob and weep 1
But, oh ! the music of that heavenly dawn,
Blue as the Virgin’s robe, when straying far
Beyond his cell, he saw on fair green lawn
A girl—and found his star !
Star sweet and white,
And his true self with love leapt into sight.
They drew a-near with all the sense of tears
Unshed that goes with first wild love, and cries
Out on the fate that gives a few dark years,
Short joys, and many sighs,
To flesh—alas !
Infinity allied with fading grass !
His day of choice came round ; he chose the world
And left the cloister ; in the lofty hall
His household gathered with his flag unfurled,
In armour, stately, tall;
But, sad and grey,
The time-worn monks went on their ancient way.
Rich clad in white and gold, with sword at belt,
He went a-roaming through the climbing town,
With all the joy of life first seen, first felt,
No longer pensive, brown,
The lights were gold,
His blood was singing, ardent, gay, and bold.
The Springtime odors streaming down the street
Grew keener in the sunset’s dusky fire—
He heard a cry and rush of hurrying feet,
And with his trusty squire
Dashed in the fray,
And saved his love, but lost his life in May.
Ah, to be dying with so vast a love
CJntold, unspent—the key of life just found—
« Ah, Death,” he cried, “ give me but time above
The green and sunlit ground
To tell her part
Of all the love that swells within my heart ! ”
■So young, so pure, so beautiful were they,
The watchers wept as on his lips she prest
Her kiss, the first and last, and knelt to pray
For his eternal rest
With anguished breath
To Him behind the shadowy veil of death.
Tas.
JAMES HEBBLETHWAITE.

Von Kotze ” : Read “ Scotty’s ” opinion
on modern Army training, and did not find it
easy to fish the true inwardness out of the
characteristically-weird rigmarole. If I under-
stand him aright, he discourses in a very mediaeval
strain indeed. Apart from the little guerilla
border wars in which England habitually indulges
and where “ Scotty ” apparently got his “ colonial
experience,” the very life and breath and spine of
the modern army is this same uniformity which
gentleman from the backblocks derides.
Modern armaments have annihilated the indi-
viduality of the soldier. The best proof of the
.French army’s efficiency is the fact that they can
shift Private X from the 112th infantry garrisoned
m Algiers into the 13th regt. somewhere in Nor-
mandy with less trouble and fuss than it takes to
start the English Derby. War has become a
mechanical science, and the only independent
genius who can affect the issue is the chief of the
commissariat. All of which recalls the yarn about
a Russian officer, who in his exam, had to write a
paper on how to exchange two regiments stationed
in Moscow and Samarkand respectively. “ Cut
off the numbered epaulets and swap ’em by parcel-
post,” the youthful genius answered. And he
wasn’t far wrong, either.

Some weeks ago the Office Crank spread
himself on the subject of “Liquid Air,” and
another exceedingly cock-sure crank replies, and
the Office Crank replies again. “ D.H.S.” howls
first:—
Bulletin, 22/7/’99, has a par. re “Liquid Air,” and . . .
has repeated Mr. O. E. Tripler’s statements, which are
both misleading and incorrect. Misleading because,
while capable of doing many of the deeds claimed for it,
liquid air cannot do away with the steam-engine and
boilers, or even lessen them, as they would have to be
used to liquefy it; and incorrect because it would be
cheaper to use the engine direct than the liquefied air.
Mr. T. has also publicly stated that he can make three
volumes of liquid air produce 10 vols. of ditto, and we
will presently hear that he can raise himself off the
ground by tightening his braces. Let it alone, “ B." ;
the subject, as treated by the non-technical press, is full
of difficulties, and only whets the appetite of ignorant
novelty-mongers, who like to see the sparks and smell
the smells of science, and air their dulnesS before the
initiated. Take a day off and consult the engineering
Prof, at the University, who has been making L.A. for
the last month, and you may, but probably will not, give
us a decent par. on the subject.
The Office Crank howls second :
The fact that steam-driven machinery has to be used in
its production does not in any way destroy the value of
liquid air as a power-generator. In Sydney N.S.W. Govt,
is erecting enormous boilers and a great mass of
machinery to produce electricity with which to drive the
city tram. Isn’t it beastly absurd-considering that,
according to “D.H.S.’ “it would be cheaper to use the
engine direct.” Also, there are hundreds of people in
Sydney using gas-engines to drive their machinery. Coal
and fire, and water, and a lot of machinery are used to
make the gas, and according to the argument of the
“initiated” “D.H.S.” all these people are drivelling
idiots because they do not use steam direct instead of
going to the increased cost of buying gas from people
who have had to use steam machinery to produce it.
Tripler’s statement that with 3gals. liquid air he can pro-
duce 10 gals, more is not on a par. with the affirmation
of the engineer who said he could lift himself by tighten-
ing his braces. Tripler says that with 3 gals, liquid air
he can keep his machinery going till it has condensed 10
gals, more from the atmosphere; which is no more
absurd than if “ D.H.S.” declared that with 3d. worth of
coal he could keep a machine going till it earned enough
to buy lod. worth of coal. He doesn’t get his lOd. from
his 3d. The rest about “Let it alone, 8.,” and “the
ignorant novelty-mongers ” is merely frill.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor ivill carefully read and. acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, .Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 9, 1899.
The 41 Municipalities of Sydney.
Ttte scheme for uniting the 41 toy munici-
palities in and around Sydney into one large,
solvent, respectable city has led to a good
deal of tired conversation within the last
few weeks, but nothing has been really done
up to date. The proposal that a conference
should meet to deal with the subject forced
even the tiredest and most local of aldermen
to be more or less aware that the subject
was in existence, but he wasn’t aware in any
very large or violent sense. A few of him
definitely approved, and the rest were
divided into four classes as follows : (1) The
class which required further time to con-
sider the matter—length of time not indi-
cated ; (2) The one which thought that the
scheme was premature and the “time was
not yet ripe ” —when the time would be ripe
not indicated either ; (3) The one which
regarded the idea as a base and horrible con-
spiracy on the part of the big municipality
of Sydney to extinguish the independence of
the small suburban boroughs, and seize their
assets, and make them pay its debts, and a
scheme to bury their bright and brilliant
young butcher-aldermen in obscurity ; aud
(4) the class which started to express its
views on the subject, and got far enough to
state that it had views of some kind, and
then fell asleep in the middle of them, so
that they remain unexpressed to this day.
• • * *
The matter has already progressed far
enough to show that there is little hope and
less enthusiasm and no velocity at all in
conferences of aldermen. The alderman is
troubled, in the first place, about the “in-
dependence of our parish ” which shouldn’t
enter into the question at all. It affects
nobody except the Mayor and the aider-
men, the town-clerk, and a few other muni-
cipal functionaries whose billets may be lost
if Sydney gets a better and cheaper form of
government. As regards the people, if they
can get advantages worth even Jd. each per
annum by sacrificing the “independence”
of their parish they are quite willing that it
should go. There is neither joy nor money
in the knowledge that at a certain corner the
home-going citizen passes from the effete,
corrupt, and morally fetid political atmos-
phere of Sydney into the free and bounding
air of Paddington, and that if the tyrannous
ash-cart of the city oppressor comes one
step beyond that boundary it will be re-
pudiated with scorn as the symbol of an
alien power. The alderman who brings the
“independence of our municipality” into
the discussion is wholly irrelevant and should
be suppressed as a man who is only consider-
ing his own billet.
* * * *
The rest of the reasons advanced by in-
furiated suburban aldermen are, in the main,
even worse. It is claimed, in the first place,
that the idea of making the 41 ragged
municipalities into a Greater Sydney is a
deep conspiracy on the part of the city
to annex the suburbs for some ne-
farious purpose. But the -city of .Syd-
ney has only 97,000 population, while
the suburbs have about 320,000, and the
suburbs would have such a vast prepond-
erance of voting power in Greater Sydney
that it is really a case of them annexing the
city, instead of being annexed to it. On an
equal rating system the city would supply
one-half of the rates, and have perhaps one-
third of the collective voice in the disposal
of the money, and if the suburban alderman
isn’t satisfied with that arrangement it would
be hard to devise any arrangement that
would satisfy him. As regards the plea that
Sydney has managed its own affairs too badly
to be trusted with those of others, it can be
safely alleged that there is very little to
choose among the 41 boroughs. Besides, it
isn’t so much a question of extending the
awful municipal management of Sydney to
the suburbs as of conferring the blessing
and illumination of the suburban alderman
on the city. As for the theory that unifica-
tion would lead to oppressive taxation of the
allegedly solvent suburban districts for the
benefit of the tottering city government,
there is no visible ground for it either.
Among the 41 municipalities in the metro-
politan area one has a rate of 9d. in the £ ;
two are rated at Is. in the £ ; one at Is. Id. ;
10 at Is. 3d. in the £ ; two at Is. 3Jd. ; six-
teen, including Sydney, at Is. 4d. ; four at
Is. sd. ; four at Is. 6d. ; and one at
Is. 7d. The last nine would probably
get their rates reduced in the saving
of interest effected by a general con-
solidation and conversion of debts ; the
great bulk of the boroughs—the one-and-
threepenny and one-and-fourpenny crowd—
would probably remain just about where
they are ; and as for the four low-rated dis-
tricts, which would be the only losers, it is
possible that some special terms might be
devised for their benefit.
• • • *
On paper, the city of Sydney has £2,000,000
worth of assets and £1,400,000 of liabilities,
so, in case of unification, it would have
£600,000 worth of net assets to put in the
pool. The 40 suburban municipalities have,
also on paper, £419,000 worth of assets and
£768,000 of liabilities, so, taking them in a
lump, they have nothing to put in the pool
except a net debt of £349,000. According
to Coghlan’s latest figures in the Statistical
Register of N.S. W., there are only seven
out of the 40 suburban municipalities (Ash-
field, North Botany, Darlington, Hurstville,
Kogarah, Manly and Vaucluse) whose assets
are greater than their liabilities ; all the rest
are more or less insolvent. If all the 41
municipalities combined the saving in in-
terest on the various debts would be so great
under any decent system of finance that it is
probable they would all be gainers, but the
suburbs would be the greatest gainers. And
the embittered suburban alderman who
talks of unification as a scheme whereby the
insolvent city seeks to cast its burden on the
solvent suburban municipalities is mainly a
fool, and a gross misrepresenter of facts,
and an inflated butcher-man or ill-directed
greengrocer to boot.
* * * •
Outside of the low-rated municipalities of
Darlington, Concord, Marsfield and Lane
Cove, which might find their rates increased
a little by unification, the only sound objec-
tion which can be raised to the creation
of a Greater Sydney is that some aider-
men and a few municipal functionaries
will probably lose their billets. There-
fore it is a dead sure thing that unifi-
cation will never have a fair show while
it is left to aldermen. The public wants it
because the consolidation of debts would
make a very large saving in interest and
there would be another very large saving in
mayors, town clerks, and other officials.
Also, because the money thus saved might
serve as the beginning of a fund with which
to start reconstructing and beautifying the
most shapeless, ungainly and ill-planned city
that has grown up in this world within the
last 150 years, aud because unification would
create for the first time a central authority
that could undertake big works for the
good of the New South Wales metropolis
in general. Further, it wants unification in
the hope that it may kill off the whole race of
aldermen. In London, Glasgow, Birming-
ham and other model cities the alderman is
practically extinct and a perfectly new
creation, the municipal statesman, has
arisen in his stead. In Greater Syd-
ney there would also be room for munici-
pal statesmen, whereas the 41 separate
boroughs only offer enough space for
the small and unprofitable alder manic
Bumble. But any person who expects the
alderman to help on the period of his own
extinction, or the small mayor to hasten the
end of his little dignity and his allowance, or
the small council clerk or inspector of
nuisances to put his shoulder to the wheel
for the purpose of making himself super-
fluous in the public interest, is over-san-
guine. If the N.S.W. Government wants to
do some good to the muddled city that lies at
its doors, the shortest way is for it to set to
work and construct an ideal municipal con-
stitution for Greater Sydney, and then submit
it to the people of each municipality for
acceptance or rejection. Or if it feels the
responsibility too great it mightauthorise each
of the 41 munieip'alities to send two popu-
larly-elected delegates to a municipal con-
vention, and let it draw up the ideal consti-
tution. That would at least put some life
into the movement. So long as the breath
of life has to be breathed through the thick,
gross medium of the alderman the movement
is never likely to be more than half-alive at
the best.
Braddon’s Opportunity.
Tasmanian Parliament has just succeeded in
tying itself in a tangle that is most interesting
to look at. The present Electoral Act of the
island expires on Sept. 30, and owing to the
Leg. Council having fired out the Constitution
Amendment Bill —which contained certain pro-
visions without which the new Electoral Bill,
now before the Assembly, is mere waste paper
—there is actually no method by which the
Tas. people can legally elect anybody to either
House after that date. In the ordinary course
of things next February is the earliest possible
date by which the Royal assent can be received
for any Electoral Act that can now be passed
in the Land of Sleep, so at the best Tasmania
will have no electoral system for five months.
The House of Refuse is, of course, directly
to blame for this, but the Braddon Ministry
has also been guilty of flagrant contributory
negligence—or else has done one of the finest
strokes of policy on record. It has a big work-
ing majority, and is allegedly desirous of pass-
ing a decent electoral law, yet it took five solid
months to get the first half of the measure as
far as the Upper Chamber ! Land of Sleep-a-
Lot, indeed ! It could hardly go slower if it
was dead. The Constitution Bill, as thrown
downstairs by the Fossils, contained provisions
for manhood suffrage, women ditto, reduction
of property-vote for Leg. Council, reducing the
number of Ms.P. to meet Federal needs, disso-
lution of the Leg. Council, and the prolongation
of the life of present Parliament till Federation
gets started. It was certainly loaded with
reform-clauses which no self-respecting House
of Sloth and Fat could be expected to swallow
without a grimace, but that it would swallow
none of the pills any Premier should con-
sider an insult too terrible to be over-
looked. It is an open secret that many
Government supporters desire Braddon to
gird on his armour and smite the Leg.
Council hip and thigh, but at time of writing
that weary statesman doesn’t seem inclined to
smite. At the same time, The Bulletin can
conscientiously say that in Braddon’s place it
wouldn’t smite either—not yet that is, till the
Council was tangled up beyond repair.
» * m jp
The Assembly doesn’t legally die until
January 28, 1900, but unless a new Assembly
is elected before the 30th of this month it can’t
be elected under the old law, and no new law
can be passed unless the two Houses come to
some agreement. What will become of the
blessed Constitution under these circumstances
not even Adye Douglas knows, and he is old
enough to know pretty well everything. Of
course one alternative would be to re-enact the
present iniquitous and ridiculously inadequate
Electoral Act, but the necessity for obtaining
the Royal assent to even that abortion
would leave the province without electoral
machinery for some months at the least. The
Bulletin’s advice to Tasmania is “ settle it if
you can ; but fighting is the best,” and if this
paper doesn’t hear the noise of a battle between
Tas. Toryism and the Tas. people in a few
weeks it will either begin to make enquiry
into the cause of Premier Braddon’s inex-
plicable funk, or begin to admire Braddon as
the finest tactician in Australasian politics.
There is one Premier in Australia who
would be bold enough and prompt enough to
grasp the present opportunity by the hair,
and that is Kingston. It is really a
magnificent chance to secure sweeping reforms
—only it needs an iron-handed Democrat to
seize that chance. The way out is to draft
another Bill containing all the democratic ideas
that were in the old one and some new ones,
including a most sweeping reform of the Upper
House ; to pass it through the House of Assem-
bly by the force of the Braddon majority, and
then send it to the Council with a practical
statement of the case as follows : “ This is the
House of Assembly’s ultimatum. It will pass
no electoral law save this one. If you reject it
the present Parliament will end in January
leaving no electoral law behind it. The old
electoral law will have expired, and there will
be no Parliament to pass a new one. There
will, therefore, be no elections. There will be
no new Parliament. The government will go
to pieces. No more taxes will be collected.
The police will probably retire for want of their
wages, and everybody will defend himself and
his property as best he can with an axe. The
unpaid judges will probably cease to administer
the law. The interest onthe publicdebt will stop,
property values will go to chaos in the general
stoppage, and ruined Fat Men will fall like
the brown leaves of autumn. The whole
machine of Government will leave off, and re-
main that way indefinitely—or till the British
Government gives us a new Constitution and
starts things afresh. Everything in the mean-
time will dissolve into its original elements,
and nobody will be put in gaol for anything
through want of the necessary vote to feed him
while he is there. We will go back to the
primitive ways of our ancestors, and reside up
a tree pending the next development. And
the responsibility for all this will be at youi
door. If you want a responsibility of this size
we can’t help it.”
* * * *
By insisting on a most democratic new elec-
toral law or none at all, Braddon can put the
Fat Men of the Council into this stupendous
cul-de-sac, or something very like it—if he
will only do it. He can get any reforms he
likes if he will only stand like a rock.
It is a glorious chance—if only Scotch-
Portuguese fighting-man Kingston had it.
If Braddon will seize this occasion, as
Kingston would. almost certainly do, he
can pose as the strongest political! in
Australasia and the boss Democrat of the
Seven Provinces. Such an opportunity, used
for all it was worth by a strong man who knew
how to advertise, might raise the tired, pic-
turesque, crows-footed Premier of the smallest
of the Australias to be first boss of the Aus-
tralian Commonwealth. If Braddon lingered
so long over his electoral-reform scheme out of
mere Tasmanian weariness, then Braddon is
only a tired nothing "hr-particular. Hut if he
lingered because he saw ahead of. him his one
and only opportunity to get the awful Leg.
Council into a cleft stick—because he saw that
by waiting he could make that exasperating
body pass the biggest and most sudden Reform
Act in Australasia, then there is the brain of a
genius behind the old knight’s hollow eyes, and
he deserves all the honor that Australia can
pay him. If Kingston had loafed over his Re-
form Bill through these past five months, the
chances are ten to one that he saw his oppor-
tunity and was making the best of it. But
Braddon doesn’t look so hopeful. If only the
chance had come to a man who was 20 years
younger and 25 years less exhausted !
N.S.W.’s Alleged Third Party.
Dear Bulletin.— The decision of the N.S.W.
Labor-party to once more support Premier Reid
suggests that that party has outlived its energy,
and its courage, and its conscience, and every
other good quality it ever possessed. In theory
its mission in Parliament is to secure the pass-
ing of much-needed democratic measures, and
to respect no one who stands in the way. It is
in a position to secure many democratic
measures, and it has been there for a long time,
and it does nothing. It holds the balance of
power in N.S.W. Assembly, and no Ministry
can exist without its permission, therefore if it
cared to seriously demand its price it would
almost certainly get it, or a good portion of it,
from some one. It might not get it from Reid
nor from Lyne, though it is more than probable
it would get it from either of them if it asked
in real earnest; but so long as it holds the
balance of power it can go on unmaking
Ministries till it gets its price from someone.
But, as Premier Reid has long since abandoned
any serious pretence of democratic legislation,
and the Labor-party has abandoned any serious
pretence of urging him on in that direction, the
only possible inference is that it doesn’t care
much about the alleged programme nowadays.
It got one of its members into the Ministry,
and it has, at different times, had more than
its share of representation on the profitable
Public Works Committee; also, it has got
sundry life-nominations to the Upper House.
And it consistently supports the Premier who
gave it these good things. His predecessor,
Dibbs, who gave N.S. W. One-Man-One-Vote,
did at least as good service to the democratic
cause as George Reid has done, but he didn’t
offer the Labor-party any portfolio, nor did he
put its members on the Public Works Commit-
tee. And his career was comparatively brief.
* * * *
Almost every democratic measure which
Premier Reid promised away back in 1894 is
still being waited for, and the chance that it
will arrive appears just about as poor now as it
did in 1894. If the Labor-party is ever to do
any good, it must, some day or other, pass
sentence of political death on the fatted
deceiver who promises everything and gives
practically nothing. The plea has always been
that to put Reid out of office was to put the
equally hopeless Lyne into office, but, at the
worst, that could only mean that Lyne would
get his chance to do some good democratic
work, and if he failed he would have the same
sentence of death passed on him in his turn.
Unless the Labor-party either tries to secure
office itself, or else uses its power to dethrone,
as a warning to his successors, every Premier
who, after a fair trial, shows himself an utter
failure from a democratic point of view, then
nobody will fear it, and it will get nothing for
the people, however many billets it may get for
its own members. It is no good holding the axe
over the head of a Tory Government if there
isn’t the least dread of the axe falling. There
is nothing in the knowledge that the Labor-
party can eject Reid from office if he doesn’t
progress in the right direction, when Reid
doesn’t progress at all, and the Labor-party’s
power is onfy used to keep him in office longer
than any other Premier in N.S.W.’s history—
and to get billets for its own members. The
power of execution amounts to nothing when
it is clearly understood that it won’t be used
and that the possible victim will be allowed to
die of old age.
» * * *
George Reid, as an inveterate office-lover
and office-seeker, would almost certainly climb
out of his old Tory rut and set some at least of
his long-promised reforms in active motion if lie
was given distinctly to understand that his
position depended on his doing so. He fails to
do it because he isn’t given to understand any-
thing of the kind. The Labor-party lias
settled down in a rut —settled down to
enjoy whatever good things come in
its way, and to make the best of
them. It has settled down so comfortably
that it wouldn’t even ask a fair hearing for its
shamefully-treated fellow-worker, Suffused,
lest it should thereby make Reid feel unsettled
in his mind. Its apparent excuse is that if
Reid is bad Lyne would probably be worse,
which is no excuse at all. It should offer its
support to the best man—not the second worst
one. Its business, as a democratic third party
holding the balance of power, is to show that


neither Reid nor Lyne, nor the man who
comes after both of them, nor that? man’s suc-
cessor, nor the person who succeeds him, nor
the next man, nor the Premier who comes after
the next man, nor any other man, shall keep
office in peace if, after a fair trial, he proves a
failure from the democratic point of view. So
long as the balance of power remains in its
hands it should maintain that attitude. Its
first object should be to secure power itself
and carry out its own reforms, but while it is
too weak to do that its aim should be to make
the N’.S.W. Premiership an impossible billet
for every man who doesn’t keep moving
steadily in the right direction. While it fol-
lows the policy of supporting a hopelessly
bad man in office on the ground that
another man may possibly be as bad or worse
there is nothing to be expected from it. In
adopting that policy it deliberately shuts its
eyes to the fact that if the other man proved
as bad or worse it could shift him also, and
that its business, as an advocate of purity and
progress, is to give all the bad men notice to
quit office in succession. If there was any
reason to suppose that Lyne, once in office,
would be an irremoveable Tory and quite in-
dependent of the Labor-party, and in a position
to refuse all reform and yet defy any possible
combination of forces to shift him from his
billet, then there would be some excuse for
putting up with many things—even with
George Reid —to keep him out. But there is
no such reason. Lyne would come in with the
intimation that the old office-holder had been
deposed because he only talked reform, but
didn’t act it; that he was himself on trial and
was expected to take warning by the fate of his
predecessor, and that if he didn’t improve
greatly on his predecessor his fate would be
similar, only much more sudden.
• » • •
At present the chief use of the N.S.W.
Labor-party is to pass George Reid’s big Loan
Bills; to aid and countenance him in his policy
of faking the accounts, and charging ordinary
expenditure to loans ; to connivd at his shame-
less political dishonesty in a general sort of
way; and to say nothing about his failure to
carry out its alleged policy of reform. And
the sinister aspect of the case lies in the fact
that, out of three Governments, all more or
less bad from a Democratic standpoint, which
have held office in N.S.W. during the term of
the Labor-party’s existence, the only one which
it has supported through thick and thin is the
one which treated it handsomely in the way of
salaried billets. Perhaps, after all, it is a mis-
take to say that the Labor-party could get its
price if it liked to insist on it. Perhaps it has
already got its price.—T.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Queensland Victory.
Last Saturday Queensland gave a majority of
several thousands for Federation. The cave-
dwellers of Brisbane voted for Parochialism by
9000 against 5000, but South Queensland out-
side of Brisbane was practically a dead heat;
Central Queensland gave a four to three
majority for Federation ; and the north—
all good things come from the north—went
for the good cause by about five to one. So
the result is that outside Brisbane two-thirds
of Queensland is strongly Federal and the
other third is equally divided. The Brisbane
parochial push is already calling its weighty
defeat “a great moral victory,” and is rising to
allege that the “rights of the minority must
be respected ” —in the bright lexicon of anti-
Bill the majority never has any rights—
and that “ South Queensland will never
consent to be dragged into Federation by
the North,” and similar maunderings. But
South Queensland, outside Brisbane, isn’t
dragging perceptibly either way—in fact, it is
neutral ; and the great North and Centre are
not at all likely to allow themselves to be
dragged out of Federation by Brisbane alone.
The Brisbane parochialists try to discount their
great defeat by pointing out that Federation
was “ only carried by the Northern vote,” just
as the Sydney cave-dwellers claimed that in
their province the Federal majority should be
disregarded because Federation was “ only
carried by the southern and western vote.”
But a Northern vote is as good for all practical
purposes as a Southern one, and a Northern
majority counts for quite as much as a Southern
one. When the North and Centre asked for
separation Brisbane refused it, and declared
that these districts must remain part of
solid Queensland, and vote as such—and
having thus declared the Northerners and the
people of the Centre to be good and sufficient
Queenslanders Brisbane can’t go back on its
word now. However much it may try to find
consolation in the fact that Federation was
only carried by the vote of some places which
overruled the vote of some other places, and
that if three or four places were left out the
result would be very different, these three facts
positively refuse to shift: Queensland in
general has given a strong Federal majority.
The Government is responsible to Queensland
in general; and it is only authorised to take
cognisance of the voice of Queensland in
general. And, however awful it may be that
(say) 27,000 voters are dragged into Federation
against their will, the Dickson Government has
to keep its eye fixed on the fact that it would be
much more awful if 32,000 voters were dragged
out of Federation against their will. The
“rights of the minority” must be respected
without doubt, but though the minority has a
great many rights, that of coercing the
majority isn’t one of them. Moreover, it is
the majority that keeps the Dickson Ministry
in office. No Ministry could afford to alienate
the whole North and Centre and a full half of
the South outside of Brisbane for the sake of
gratifying Brisbane alone. If it did, it would
be in the glad situation of the gentleman who
lost sixpence and found two coppers.

The Bootmaker’s Lamentation.
The Westralian Federal committee is wearily
examining local jam-makers, bootmakers, fur-
niture men and the like, who say monotonously
that “Federation and iuterprovincial Freetrade
will destroy our industries.” Of course, these
scared bootmakers and sofa-builders don’t
really know that Federation and interprovincial
freetrade will damage their industries. They
never tried it. It is the same position as that
of a bygone English Parliamentary committee
which sat to consider the idea of building rail-
ways, and heard expert evidence that the loco-
motive was contrary to the will of the
Almighty, and would drive cows mad. Every-
thing depends on how a completely new set of
political conditions works out, and the most
experienced man who ever put a brown-paper
sole on a boot doesn’t necessarily know any-
thing about the way new political conditions
will work out. But the attitude of the small
bootmaker everywhere is one of abject scare,
and everywhere that strange superstition
about what the wonderful and almost omni-
potent people in Melbourne will do if they
get a show seems to be in the tradesman’s
bones. Westralia pays no higher wages than
some other provinces ; it can get as good
machinery as any of them ; its manufacturers
are not supposed to be all incompetent - and
idiotic ; and Melbourne goods will always be
burdened in the W.A. markets by freight on a
2000 miles voyage, which will give W. A. a
very good measure of Protection even when
interprovincial Freetrade is the order of the
day. But its small traders are eaten up with
the pitiable cowardice of men who have
mouldered in their own parish all their lives
and believe that the next parish is a strange
land full of devils. One amazing individual
even turned up to allege that interprovincial
Freetrade would ruin the W.A. coal industry—
which is under Freetrade now, and always has
been, though apparently this strange coal
crank isn’t aware of the fact. And while the
committee is hunting up small bootmakers to
swear that Federation and interprovincial
Freetrade will ruin their industries, Big John
Forrest is explaining that he rather likes the
idea of Federation and interprovincial Free-
trade, provided a transcontinental railway to
bring in the competing boots of the other pro-
vinces with all possible velocity is guaranteed.

Some Figures that Smell High.
When Premier John Forrest resolved to turn
his massive tail-coat inside-out, arid go back on
all his Federal pledges, and trail his word of
honor in the mud, and run Westralia in the
anti-Federal interest, the local State actuary
was turned on—like Coghlan last year in
N.S.W.—to show in figures the “disastrous
effects of the Bill.” And that functionary
prostituted about two bushels of numerals in
the bad cause, and produced a document so
tangled that it was practically impossible for
any ordinary individual to follow its meander-
ings and deviations. But it ended with the
moral, supposed to be deduced from the
figures, that Federation would leave Wes-
tralia with a great deal less revenue
than it has now. Then the Forrest
Government appointed a commission to
find reasons why the Federation Bill shouldn’t
be sent to the people, and to save appearances
it put one or two Federalists—notably Mathe-
SON, M.L.C.— on it, with sufficient Disunionists
to vote them down twice over. And things
would have been all right if these people
hadn’t started asking the Government Actuary
awkward questions about his figures. It was
discovered, for one thing, that when he
reckoned up the loss Westralia would suffer
through the abolition of duty on goods im-
ported from the other provinces, he reckoned on
goods imported from Maoriland coming in free
—which they won’t do, seeing that Maoriland
isn’t in the Commonwealth movement, and
thereby he made the loss look larger than it
really is. Then it was extracted from this
public official that he had made another weird
hash of his figures to the extent of £21,000
per annum. The Federal Government, it is
assumed, will have to pay the W.A. Govern-
ment £688,000 for buildings, dfcc., taken over
(it will, presumably, hand the State Govern-
ment bonds to that amount), and the interest
on this (£21,000 a year) will have to be paid by
the people to the Federal Government. Then
the Federal Government will have to pay it to
the Westralian State Government—so things
will be as broad as they are long. Matheson,
M.L.C., dragged it out of the public function-
ary in the witness-box that he had made no
mention of the fact that the money would come
back to the Westralian Treasury, and by this
dodge he had made, Westralia’s share of the
cost of Federation £33,500 instead of £12,500.
It is further alleged by wire that the actuary
admits having included one item of £233,000
federal expenditure five times over, and thereby
vastly increased the apparent cost of the
Federal Government and also the apparent cost
of Federation to Westralia. Big John Forrest
grew angry in the course of the proceedings,
and declared the cross examination which
brought out these dreadful mistakes to be mere
waste of time, and departed with offended
dignity. Considering that Forrest is against
union, and that the actuary’s figures were pre-
pared by his instructions, and that all the
actuary’s blunders tend to create an utterly
false impression about the excessive cost of
Federation to Westralia, there is a strong odor
of decayed emu-egg about the whole business.
No smaller egg could smell so strongly.
♦
Two Bridges.
Apropos of the demand that has been raging
any time the last 25 years for a high - level
bridge between Sydney and North Sydney, The
Bulletin sometimes wonders why the two
municipalities don’t simply get an Act of Parlia-
ment authorising them to build the bridge
themselves, and build it, and thereby end the
tiredest political subject in N.S.W. Then they
could lay a tram-line across, and get the
Government to run its electric-tram right
through on the shares system. In all proba-
bility the bridge would cost nearly a million,
and that would represent an interest bill of
£30,000 a year—£ls,ooo a year for each muni-
cipality if the account was equally divided.
A penny toll on foot passengers and the
profits of the tramway business would almost
certainly yield £12,000 a year to begin with—the
ferry business produces much more than that—
which would leave each municipality with
£9OOO a year to provide. In course of time
the receipts would probably increase sufficiently
to cover this and a great deal more. In the
meantime the estimated value of property at
North Sydney is £3,736,000, and if the bridge
raised it to £4,000,000, which it would probably
do without turning a hair, the extra rentals
would pay the additional rate levied to
cover interest, and still leave some £4200
a year profit for the bloated landlord.
Probably it would start such a boom that in a
year or two the extra rentals would pay interest
on the cost of the bridge, and leave an- extra
£30,000 a year for the bloated landlord. It is
a practical certainty that, between the direct
receipts from the bridge, and the indirect
ones through increase of property values
created by it, the municipalities would
get their bridge for nothing from the
very beginning, and have a big profit
as well. And as population increased—which
it would do rapidly when you could get into a
tram-car at Redfern and make a straight run to
St. Leonards—the profit would get bigger and
bigger every year. A bridge from
valory Hill to Balmain would probably be an
equally good spec, for the local landlord ; when
the electric-tram took a bee-line into that
suburb instead of the inhabitant having to pick
his way on foot down a dirty back-street to a
slow and dilapidated green boat, property in
that hitherto cheap suburb would go up 50 per
cent, in one act. If the local Fat Person would
only realise how' many hundred thousand
pounds of good rent he had lost in the years
that the locust has eaten—the years in which
he has been vainly moaning to the Government
to give him his bridge instead of getting it him-
self, and becoming vastly rich right off—he
would go and hang himself in disgust on the
rail of his own office desk.

Neild’s Bill.
Considering the size of the recent trouble in
N.S. W. Assembly and the smallness and mean-
ness of the small, mean job which brought it all
.about, Premier Reid’s strategy appears to be
getting very loose. Some time ago burlesque-
Major Neild— he of the bear’s-greased mou-
stache—was understood to be going to Europe,
and he offered, either with or without re-
muneration, bo collect information about Euro-
pean charities, and other more or less useless
knowledge. He brought back a mass of
written erudition, and in the concluding por-
tion of it he set forth that he had done all this
work without hope of reward and for love of
his bleeding country—so either the Premier’s
statement that the bear’s-greased one intended
doing the work absolutely gratis was quite
true, or else it was considered advisable to
pretend that he intended it to be gratis,
and that the payment was only a sudden
afterthought. Anyhow, Neild was paid £350
without the authority of Parliament, for the
work which Parliament had been assured was
being done gratis, and, as Majah Neild had, to
some extent, appointed himself to the position of
charitable inquirer in Europe, this sudden and
violent presentation of his bill made the House
angry. Why Premier Reid paid that money
will probably never be explained. The single
vote of the man with the amazing moustache
could be hardly worth buying at such risk.
Parliament winks at a lot of unauthorised ex-
penditure, but when Reid gave it his solemn
oath that Neild wouldn’t be paid, and then
paid Neild, even a tame Parliament
that would feed out of the hand was
bound to grow angry. Unless Neild
hung on to Reid’s door-bell in the middle of the
night and wouldn’t go away without the
money, and Reid came down attired in his
night-shirt and carrying a candle, and paid him
before he was sufficiently awake to know what
he was doing, and Neild had got round the
corner with the cash before Reid was fully
aware that he had wrecked the sacred Consti-
toosh’n, there is no publishable theory that ap-
pears to fit the case. The most charitable
theory is that Reid had to pay the money else
Neild would have wakened the baby.

The Australian Illegitimate.
“ Hermes,” to The Bulletin
The recent remarks of a certain medico, to the
effect that foundlings should be allowed to perish on
the casual doorstep, because foundlings do generally
perish, in the long run, of indigestion, born of artificial
food, make one regret that some statesman did not insert
a clause in the Federal Constitution to the effect that all
foundlings should be adopted and declared the legal
children of the Nation, and that the Nation would be
responsible for their upbringing. No medical savage has
got the effrontery to stand up and advocate that legiti-
mate children who are deprived of their natural food
should be sent swiftly and painlessly out of the world, but
this humorist advocates the practical murder of the poor
friendless illegitimate. Some medical authorities have
declared that love-children are more virile and intel-
lectual than those of respectability, yet, according to
statistics, they die off three times as fast. The offspring
of romance has a lot of difficulties to contend with,
chief of which are the careless preparation of arti-
ficial food (the unfortunate mite is generally fed
on artificial food), and the ghastly fact that it is to the
individual interest of everybody connected with him that
he should die. But it is to the interest of Australia that
he should live and grow’ up to be a good and well-
equipped citizen. Few’ people realise what an addition
to the population it w’ould be if the foundlings could be
reared. Between 1877 and 1896 there w’ere born in
N.S.W. alone 35,114 illegitimate children. A huge pro-
portion of them perished before reaching the age of 15,
but if the death-rate could have been reduced to that of
the ordinary infantile mortality they would have formed
a large addition to the population. Marriage is
on the decrease, and the legitimate birth-rate is also on
the decrease, as the following table from Coghlan’s
“Seven Colonies” for 1897-8 (page 118) will show :
1871—1685 illeg. births; 74,217 total births ; illeg. p.c., 3T9
1881—3524 ~ „ 98,721 „ „ ~ 4'24
1891—6001 „ „ 128,445 „ ~ „ 4‘74
1896—6597 ~ „ 118,746 „ „ „ 5.56
Which shows that in 1896 there were actually 10,295 less
legitimate children born than in 1891, but, in spite of the
decreasing birth-rate, 596 more illegitimates were brought
into the w’orld without benefit of clergy than in 1891.
Australia’s interest in the matter is just this : Some day
this continent will find that human beings are of more
value than all its alleged great resources put together.
At present, Australia would be a poor place indeed but
for the distant oversea market of Britain. In 1896 Aus-
tralia exported in all £40,141,435, of Which £29,172,801
went to the United Kingdom, and £2,065,103 to British
possessions, or a total of £31,237,904 to the British Um-
pire, w’hile all the rest of the world took only a miserable
£8,903,531. This ought to make it plain that without
Britain this continent would be in a very bad way com-
mercially. It is just possible that in 20 years’ time the
development of South Africa, Uganda, the grain-growing
highlands of Unyoro, Argentina, Canada, and the almost
certain “ civilising ” of Morocco, all of whioh are some
thousands of miles nearer Britain than Australia, will have
in or eased their output sufficiently to cope with the
demands of the British market, while their closer proxi-
mitv will enable them to undersell Australian products.
When that day conies population to absorb our own pro-
ductions will be the salvation of this continent. The
trouble won’t come suddenly, like the thief in the night,
but gradually, with many signs and warnings of decreas-
ing exports, and if the population grows as the exports
dwindle we shall escape the economic shock. For this
reason alone it is advisable that the medico referred to
be promptly executed and the nation take steps to care
for the foundling, and see that it gets a fair show to
grow up a useful man or woman. When the legitimate
child is fast becoming too tired to be born it is neces-
sary to make the best of any kind of child we can get. If
artificial feeding kills the foundling, let the State provide
a wet-nurse. The mothers, if relieved of the responsi-
bility of attempting to do what is mostly beyond their
power, would generally be available, and where it could
be done safely it might be possible to make an unmarried
mother an allowance for the maintenance of her child
past the suckling age. There are other reasons why the
poor, wronged love-child should be cared for and dealt
with tenderly, and to those who have the sap of human
affection within them one glance at the helpless
mites with their deep, wondering eyes, dimpled hands
and chubby feet, is more powerful than the arguments of
many sages. Bui I started these remarks from a piirely
economic standpoint, and humane or mere Christian
drivel is out of place.
A simple, true and melancholy letter from
Cootamundra, N.S.W.
The present friction between the heads of the R.C. and
Protestant Churches in N.S.W. has its lesson for laymen,
although perhaps not the lesson which the disputants
would in their calm moments read to their respective
flocks. Were no clerical pressure brought to bear, a
Protestant and a Roman Catholic could live most peace-
ably and agreeably side-by-side, with all the mutual
respect and good-fellowship of citizens. The folks next
door are Roman Catholics, let me say, and I a Protestant.
The exchange of neighborly- offices and obligations pro-
ceeds, and some degree of Christian Catholicity is present
with us. Nothing is further from the inclination of either
of us than to hurt the feelings or insult the belief of the
other. What is the motive, and whence, that has
induced the princes of our Churches to supplant this
goodwill with distrust and religious rancour ? These fol-
lowers of the Prophet of peace on earth and goodwill
towards men expound weekly the victim of long-suffering
charity and humility. We believe they could not possibly
preach a nobler religion. But, alas I what an example of
charity ! What an exhibition of brothedy love ! How
lovingly could Christ greet these ministers and ex-
pounders of His Gospel ! Daily', before their flocks, they
read and tear and malign each other in the press and in
the pulpit. The moral sense of the laity is shocked and
cries shame alike on the Cardinal and his opponents, yet
the un-Christian strife goes on kindling and nourishing
the spark of religious hatred and war. Already my
neighbor holds aloof from me ; I avoid his company—and
this is the work of these men of peace and followers of
Christ ! !
Dr. Maund’s expressed ideal when he in-
augurated the Melb. Women’s Hospital was
the founding of an institution where women in
distress might be succoured by women. The
subscribers to that Hospital have now a chance
for the first time in its history of giving effect
to the praiseworthy wish of its founder. Dr.
Helen Sexton is the first woman candidate
for the position of honorary out-door surgeon,
and at the coming annual election on the Btli
inst., subscribers can hardly go wrong if they
cast one of the twelve votes to which they are
entitled in favor of this distinguished and pro-
fessionally-experienced alumnus of Melbourne
University. With the present resident woman
doctor (Miss Plummer), and one honorary out-
door surgeon (Miss Sexton), the Women’s
Hospital would begin to throw off the reproach
which necessarily attaches to an institution
intended solely for women, being officered
entirely by men.

A letter from Adelaide ;*
Editor Bulletin. — Re the “Adelaide striking doctors.”
I appear for the other side. It is too late to go into the
genesis and history of the painful Adelaide Hospital, dis-
pute. Therefore Ido not wish to controvert anything in
particular that has appeared in The Bulletin. This is
merely- a mild and respectful appeal for Australian’ fair
play. “We” (the “strikers,” for I “struck" with the
North Terrace men, and so did 99 per cent, of the country
G.Ps, in S.A.) have had our real grievance : we have
fought our fight. We have backed up our leaders at
much loss of time and trouble and, in some cases,
money. We have had a whole Government and half a
cityful of people against us. We made a drawn battle of
it and each side retired (a little way) with the honors of
war. Here let me say that all non-paying patients whom
I have sent on to the ex-honoraries of North Terrace
have received as prompt and thorough attention as
when they had the same men’s services in the Hospital
aforetime. Also I (to whom this aspect of the ease “is
equal”) have good reason to know that, this gratuitous
work was not to “spite Napier” or “keep their hands in,”
or for any such low-down motive, but in response to a
direct appeal from the poor patients themselves. I wish
to be brief. With all due respect for your Adelaide cor-
respondent, (I am ignorant of his identity) it may he
noticed by any of your readers that his’ pars re the
whole “strike” episode from first to last have been
absolutely one-sided. This is so unlike the normal
Bulletin matter, that I have been sorely tempted to
furnish you with the whole history- of the “strike,”
whereby to judge. But it has become like the smellful
Dreyfus case, in so much that half the “ evidence ”
available at this date would be the “bloke” calling
the “cove” a blanky liar;, and that would be .about
all. Now for my points in summary
I. There are two sides to the question.
11. You have only heard (and therefore printed) one
side.
111. If this side is not unfair intrinsically, it certainly is
in the absence of the other version.
IV. Those who had any local axes to grind have no
doubt got them quite sharp enough by now.
V. Let up.
Because—(a.) It can’t be so interesting to the general
public.
(b.) It is a little hurtful to a class of men who are per-
haps more in sympathy with pain, with shame, with
poverty-, and with Australia than most classes.
And, (c.) Nuff sed.
Nil.
