Australian legislators persist in their slavish
habit of swallowing English legislation “ with
all faults.” Wrixon and Sargood are intro-
ducing Chamberlain’s Workmen’s Compensa-
tion Act into Vic. Parliament just as it passed
the Lords in ’97, despite the fact that every
law-report reaching Australia is full to reple-
tion of cases revealing flaws of draftsmanship,
cumbrous provisions, and general slip-sloppi-
ness in that Act. Apart from its limited
application to a few tradejs, an inherent
defect of the English Act is untouched. The
contracting-out provisions, which were only
left in the English Act on account of the fact
that there were already in existence large well-
subsidised benefit societies connected with
some of the railway co.’s, are also, and quite
unnecessarily, retained in the Vie. Bill. Even
as it stands, the Bill is well worth accepting,
but why disfigure the proffered orange with
quite-unnecessary bites?
The interest with which the medical
fraternity lately watched the case of a man who
lay fh Sydney Hospital totally unconscious for 40
days before dying moves this paper to marvel at
the spectacle of that influential Sealyland politic-
ian, Hugh Nelson, who has been unconscious of
politics for about 40 years and isn’t dead yet.

The “ Indian Mutiny,” with which Rig-
nold re-opened Sydney Criterion last Saturday,
doesn’t come up to the promise of its name. It
deals with a very small, outlying fringe of the
mutiny at the best, and, besides, there is hardly
any mutiny at all. The characters, too, are
mostly sketchy and unsatisfactory. Through five
long acts Glover, as the big brown man, irrigates
the play with a slow rain of conversation to the
effect that the time will shortly arrive when he
will give the signal on a big bell, and then the
terrific upheaval will start with a whoosh, and
the white man’s hat will only be seen for half a
second above the torrent as it sweeps him away.
And near the end of the fifth act the slow brown
man grasps the bell-rope at last, but a sword cuts
it short above his head, and there is no signal—
only a futile, brown Augustus talking himself to
death with a bit of rope in his hand and a silent bell
above his head. The audience doesn’t sympathise
with brown Augustus, for though he is supposed to
be the leader of the rebellion, and he talks more
than any other three, he always departs at a heavy,
camel-like run when a white man comes round the
corner, dropping his artillery out of his pockets as
he goes. Abul Khan, who is a strong, concise,
brown rebel and doesn’t run when a white man
comes round the corner (unless he runs furiously
after the white man), is killed in a most unsatis-
faetoryfashionhalf-waythrough the play. Diver, as
the white Major in command of about six Britons,
has hardly anything to do except form Private
Sweeney into a hollow square now and then. The
very light-brown girl who is supposed to be Abul
Khan’s daughter, proves to be really the daughter
of the Major, but the circumstance can hardly be
heard above the ceaseless talking of brown Augus-
tus and his incessant statement that he is going
to give the signal when he is fully rested, and
then all perdition will break loose, and that in the
meantime he sees a small white boy in the dis-
tance and proposes to go and conceal himself
under the bed.
* * * *
Miss Lilian Wheeler, as the Major’s lost
daughter, has the best part in the play. She
slews the big cannon round all by herself, and
fires it point blank at Augustus, who seems quite
well after it, only rather tired. She raises a
Brahmin from the dead, and she swings herself
across a bottomless abyss by the aid of the Union
Jack ; and it is she who cuts the bell-rope when
the brown conversationalist is almost going to do
something at last. The next best character is the
Major’s wife, by Miss Watts-Phillips, a crisp,
definite little woman who knows exactly whom
she is going to hit with her ’brella, and nits him
right there. Among the great haze of talking
niggers, she is a distinct relief. There is much
military evolution in the play, and it leads to very
little. The British force is generally advancing
in great haste to a place where nothing happens,
and marching in great, haste back again ; and
Johnson Weir either arrives covered with blood to
tell it to return at once, or else arrives covered
with dust to say that it is returning. Also two
very fine scenes by Vaughan manage to rise above
the wash of the brown man’s conversation, and the
cannon which is fired at Augustus is a very effec-
tive “prop.” indeed. In fact, it is the best stage
cannon this writer remembers.
** * *
The other Holloway Co. has come back
to Sydney Lyceum, and revived that vigorous
melodrama, “When London Sleeps.” Said melo-
drama, if it is taken in a large, general sort of
fashion—the way one takes a terrace that doesn’t
belong to him —is rather an interesting pageant,
but if it is dried and inspected through a micro-
scope the logic of it goes to pieces. The heroine
is a circus-girl—a pure and lovely saint who used
to bound in rationals on the slack wire-—and she
inherits a huge fortune and a wicked captain.
There is no legacy duty to pay on the captain.
He sets fire to the house in order to persuade the
girl to marry him, and she has to fly on the tele-
graph wires over the house roofs while London
sleeps. Also, he breaks into her bed-room
when she has her beautifully-frilled night-
dress and her bare feet on, and wants to
offer her his hand informally on the spot.
Also, he beguiles her into a house of ill-
fame where he proposes to win her affections
by introducing her to some of the worst Totties
and Flossies in London, and urges her to come to
the midnight banquet and join in the revel, and
the wine-cup, and the high kick. It is only in
dramas that a lady with enormous wealth can be
thus pawed and mauled by a poor relation of bad
character, or that a gentleman can call and set
fire to the house and go away again; also, it is
only in dramas that a huge policeman on night
duty warms his soul by talking about his beloved
Queen and about the greatness of the empire. The
only excuse is that the average drama is a trifle
off its head, and not fully responsible for its
actions. There are some few changes in the cast
as compared with that of last January. Laurence
Dunbar is the wicked “capting,” vice Charles
Holloway, and Dunbar sets fire to his best girl,
and tries to drop his daughter into the river,
with considerable success. On the other hand,
the substitution of Miss Ada Guildford for pretty
and clever Amy Gourlay is not an improvement.
Miss Gourlay’s part was that of a red-hatted siren
who called out to men at street corners late at
night, and her intentions were thirsty but not
honorable, yet she made this risky character the
brightest feature of the piece. And spidery
William Gourlay—the gentleman who seems to
have eight legs and to be fully accountable for
none of them—is also gone, therefore another man
disguises himself in a skirt and hat, and assures
the policeman that he was once a good girl and
went to Sunday-school regularly. But to a great
extent the cast is the same as before, and it isn’t a
very bad cast on the whole.
* * * *
The Brough season is near its end at Syd-
ney Royal, and ‘ ‘ Lord Quex ” will probably fill
in most of the remnant. The Bulletin has come
to the conclusion that it gets nearer to absolutely
disliking Mrs. Brough in the present production
than it ever did before. This painful fact is not
wholly her fault; the exceedingly tough nature
of her part is largely responsible. A tall, hot, flushed
lady who is locked up in a bed-room and weeping
in a yellow dressing-gown has certain disadvan-
tages to contend with. And when the gentleman
who has her locked up explains candidly that he
intends to call the household next morning, and
exhibit her in her dishabille as the person who has
been in his room all night, and when one reflects
that by then she will have wept in the dressing-
gown for four hours, and that her face will be
swollen to twice its natural size, and that she will
probably be a damp, crushed, disarranged heap on
the floor, the character has its drawbacks. Even
after only an hour of confinement the prisoner
goes badly at the knees, and it is quite obvious
that, if Lord Quex keeps his purpose, by the time
the exhu.ition comes off the lady will be a quadru-
ped feebly retiring (after her character has been
blighted) on all-fours. Or else she will be too
weak to retire at all, and the justly-outraged
housemaid will carry her away under her arm.
This horrible event doesn’t really happen, but it
looks for quite a while as if it was going to happen.
* * *
“Secret Service” pursues its deserving
career of bombardment at Her Majesty’s, but
Sydney takes less interest in the scrum between
the men in blue and the inen in grey than this
paper expected. Some day, when Sydney has
had a bombardment of its own, and the invader
has shot all the pugilists in the Golden Gate
Athletic Club, and sacked Kiama, and wantonly
dynamited the “ blow-hole, ” and insisted on his
horse winning the Melbourne Cup, and has made
his prisoners stand in a row and disparage “the
harbor, ” this country may take more kindly to
military drama than it does. But, at present, it
is a remote kind of thing. Yet there is a lot of
clever realism about “ Secret Service ” ; the far-off
pounding of the cannon at Petersburg is a good
effect, and so is the tramping of the grey-coated
regiments as they go past with their cannon in the
darkness. And the author knew his book when
he made his two girls so short of clothes—one has
two dresses left, and the other has none and wears
her grandmother’s frock. The South, not being a
manufacturing country, grew woefully short of
clothes in the latter days of the war ; the proudest
Southern girl was happy if she had one turned
skirt left, and if her best boy had time to lead her
to the altar she was glad, in the dark days of ’64,
if she had two stockings to go there in. Very
often she had to trot there with short steps, to dis-
guise the fact that she had no stockings at all.
* * * *
At Sydney Tivoli there is overwhelming
evidence of a widespread determination to work
to death James Mooney, the man who bounces
like an india-rubber ball, turns somersaults back-
wards and forwards off his head and his feet, and
uses his legs as a liver-pad while he walks around
on his hands. An inconsiderate audience simply
won’t lie down till he does it all over again three
times. Rayston and Rayford are in their last
week, and Rayston concludes the turn with a neat
speech thanking the audience for its toleration of
himself and wife—he mentions that the lady is his
wife in case some one should go away under the
impression that she is his mother—and he thanks
it for sitting there and applauding, because he
knows it wouldn’t have done it had it been sober—
just here his maligned little wife hauls him off into
outer oblivion by pre-arrangement. The void left by
Oinquevalli is partly allayed by twenty damsels in
brief brown dresses, brown gaiters, black shoes,
white collars, cuffs and belts; who march round
with fixed bayonets and give a whistling chorus.
The Farley Trio do some clever balancing, which
culminates in the man with the large pink nose
standing on his head on the thin end of a big viol
and playing a fiddle with his hands while in that
weird position. George Spry and Florrie Austin
give a more or less comic sketch. Paul Pelham’s
“Klondyke” is responsible for the only senti-
mental tears this writer ever saw shed in the
Tivoli. It shows the sturdy miner in a London
street listening to an invisible chorus singing,
“ On to Klondyke, Land of Gold ? ” The London
street vanishes aloft, and snow-clad mountains
heap their peaks against the Yukon sky, while in
the chilly moonlight the exhausted miner who
listened to that chorus raves of home and wife
and child. The mountain splits and shows a
vision of the far-off home, with the old familiar
picture on the wall, the lamp with the red shade
over it, the wife, and the little bright-haired girl
praying for the absent dada; and the Tivolians,
who mostly describe the absent wife as the “old
woman ” and the little bright-haired child as “ the
kid, ” break down. It is rather cheap and shoddy
pathos, but it goes every time.
* * * *
The Trebelli song-recitals at Melbourne
Town Hall seem like a protest against recent
worshipping of wooden idols. Mile. Antoinette
Trebelli exhibits all that culture can do for a born
vocalist and a gifted woman. She sings many
song's in divers tongues with unaffected gracious-
ness, devoutness, archness or merry humor, as
the case may require. Her platform manner is
more impressive than the theatrical style of
Albani, and her voice is at its prime. The “star ”
contributed a dozen numbers last Tuesday
night between 8 o’clock and 9.50 p.m. (play-
ing her own accompaniment to some of them)
without showing the least sign of fatigue. She
sang as though she shared in the pleasure she
was giving to her hearers. There was no
pretence of trying to escape the inevitable encore.
A Trebelli recital is an up-to-date entertainment
—all prizes and no blanks —calculated to make
everybody come again next time, and tell their
friends not to miss it. John Prouse, the resonant
baritone, from Maoriland, who took part in the
first Trebelli concert-tour, is the only co. engaged
this time. Prouse lifts up his voice what time the
lady is resting hers, save during a five minutes’
interval, when they are both off. Perhaps it is
more correct to say that the baritone throws his
voice, as a rule. He is a vigorous, spirited artist,
with a black beard, and no nonsense about him.
The first bar of his opening song —a savage Hun-
garian ditty, entitled “ Had a Horse ” —went bang
up against the far wall of the building, and re-
bounded back to the tuneful singer. Whereupon
th ' audiev.-e decided that Prouse was the right
man for the billet. The second recital was to
happen on Tuesday of this week, the night pre-
vious to Amy Castles’ farewell, which will be
followed on Thursday by some more starring of
damsels from Bendigo. Antoinette Trebelli, con-
sidered in contrast with Amy Castles (at higher
prices of admission) was the cruellest reflection
that will ever be cast on the little girl’s run of
luck.
* Hf # *
Among the audience who heard Trebelli
give the laughing song (Auber) at Melb. Town
Hall the other night there may have been a few
grey-headed applauders who had tried to encore
Madame Fanny Simonsen’s performance of the
same old song in the same building long years ago.
The occasion was a benefit to the late William
Saurin Lyster, and owing to the length of the pro-
gramme encores were prohibited. Fanny Simon-
sen’s laughing-song so “knocked” the audience
that it rose in insurrection against the rule, and
yelled for a repeat. Lyster’s wife, Georgia Hod-
son, was the next on the programme. She came
forward to sing, but the house wouldn’t listen to
her. Then Lyster took the platform, angrily re-
buked the noisy ones (his patrons of the night) and
told them in effect that he would see them hanged
before he would let Madame Simonsen give the
encore. Whereupon the bulk of the audience
walked out of the hall, leaving the concert to its
gloomy fate.
m# * *
“For England,” the huge, red-coated
melodrama which is filling up the final fortnight
of Bland Holt’s season at Melb. Royal, turns out
to be a more Boerlike revival than was stated in
The Bulletin last week. A weary scribe does
his best to forget the plots of these awful Sutton
Yane narratives as time rolls on, otherwise they
would settle on his mind and break it beyond
repair. The present scribe, on renewing his long-
lost acquaintance with the show aforesaid, found
that President Kruger and General Joubert of the
Transvaal were introduced into Act 3, and he got
a severe shook in his foreign intelligence when he
saw that Oom Paul stood only five feet high, and
wore a black bell-topper, likewise mild uneventful
trousers and plain, ordinary boots. Onelikestothink
of the real Kruger as a wickeder oppressor than
that, but no matter. “For England” is the
drama wherein Bland Holt lends all his money to
the British Army, and converts himself into a
Zulu on business principles. Roughly speaking,
“ For England ” is a dramatic outrage of the most
desperate character. It contains nothing worth
believing in except scenery, and horses, and the
indignation of an aged father whose penitent
daughter looks very nice when she comes home to
be forgiven. The horses distinguish themselves at
the outset by cantering down a forest glade in
pursuit of an alleged fox. Later on they carry
warriors to the rescue of Walter Baker, Bland
Holt, Arthur Styan, and the principal ladies of
the co. in the darkest hour of Albert Norman’s
treachery. The horses deserve all the corn they
will get during the run of this well-dressed spec-
tacle. Likewise, gallant Baker earns special
praise for sticking to the dear old Union Jack
with more emphasis than usual, and the incidental
gunpowder explosions shed a profound perfume
throughout the house. “For England ” has the
sort of influence which can be smelt by all classes.

“ Trip to Chinatown ” was staged again at
Melb. Princess’s last Saturday, out of respect for
popular opinion re “A Stranger in New York,”
which had been advertised to carry the ’Murkan
co. ’s season through to its bitter end. Said season
terminates to-night (Thursday), and on Friday or
Saturday the cheerful music of “The Geisha”
will be heard again.
** # *
At Melbourne Alexandra the struggle
with “ Faust ” is being prolonged over a second
week. Rebottaro, as Valentine (the one really
satisfactory feature of the show), likewise Mephi-
stopheles, and the agreeable young man in the
name-part, have improved with practice, but on
the other hand Marguerite seems a more hopeless
case than ever, and the alternate Siebel is a mere
apology for a she-male. Touching the weird
mezzo-falsetto of Martha, The Bulletin would
say much, if that susceptible widow were not a
pleasing amateur in her silent moments. Criticism
is withheld for the sake of the time when she
doesn’t sing.
Mr. alld Miss Valdares, on their joint and
several bikes, were added to the Bijou programme
last Saturday, and the acrobatic skill of those two
whirling wheelers vyill help to bloat the Rickards
exchequer for the next few weeks. Short of turn-
ing a double somersault on one spoke the Val-
dareses can do everything imaginable in trick
wheelwork, and with Cinquevalli out of the way
they would seem quite marvellous. Meanwhile
the sublime juggler is playing billiards in his
sweet old way, and catching plates as they never
were caught before, and Melbourne has very little
amazement left for any other artist.

At St. George’s Hall the last nights of
M‘Adoo’s minstrels are announced, and it is
stated, as a melancholy fact, that the cake-walkers
will walk elsewhere after the 11th inst.

Scherek’s 15th Orchestral Concert will
happen at the Town Hall next Saturday after-
noon. The two previous gatherings were at the
Bijou ; therefore this intimation is addressed to
all customers who don’t wish to look for their
Scherek in the wrong shop.
** * *
Last week’s promise to say something com-
plimentary about the German-Reed entertainment
at the Athenaeum hall, was rather a rash promise,
and it is hereby cancelled. The artistic success
of any soft and gentle show with recitations and
small salads in it is a matter depending almost
entirely upon the audience. If a large audience
comes along and laughs, the entertainment bucks
up and is cheerful. But when a very small audi-
ence trickles into the tomb-like building, and
gives itself up to despair, the people on the stage
lose all power to raise even a laugh among them-
selves. That’s what the matter at the Athenaeum
hall.
** * *
The policy of the Amy Castles manage-
ment in holding back her lucky last concert until
the very night before she was to start to be sea-
sick is discussed from two different points of view.
Most people assume that the “ future _ Melba ”
voice was so sore that Father Robinson didn’t like
to “ give it away ” earlier lest disappointed enthu-
siasts might make some more nasty remarks. We
must assume that A - selection of Sept. 6th as the
date of Amy’s farewell was a diplomatic stroke
intended to keep things as pleasant as pos-
sible for the departing warbler. The other theory
is altogether too uncharitable. It assumes that
because Mrs. Doubleday had booked Melb. Town
Hall for Sept. 7th for a Lili Sharp and Lfansen-
Knarhoi concert, the Castles party came along a
fortnight later and decided to “queer the pitch ”
by producing their Farewell Amy on the preceding
night. But, admitting that the assumption of
rivalry in the matter is probably foolish, it is
difficult to understand why Amy Castles, who
owes her “discovery” to Mrs. Doubleday, has
been allowed to appear ungrateful to that lady.
** * *
In a weak moment the Brasseys promised
to spend an evening with “Faust” at the Alex-
andra, on behalf of musical culchaw for the masses,
perhaps. The exalted pair, with a gilt-buttoned
martyr in attendance, tried hard to carry out their
contract. Silent and unmoved they sat staring
sadly at the stage or averting their gaze from the
awful Alexandra drop-curtain during the intervals,
until the end of the Garden Scene. Then they
gathered up their belongings and filed forth into
the outer world of woe. Their Nibbses’ patience
was exhausted. Lady 8., who took away
a souvenir of the melancholy occasion in the form
of a bouquet provided by the management, looked
ready to die as she made her escape along the back
of the d.c. Two hours earlier the weary woman
thought herself ready for the worst that could
happen, but the vice-regal imagination had never
pictured such a “Faust ” as this.
* * *
The poor little performance of “Faust”
at Melb. Alexandra revives memories of the last
time Gounod’s opera was properly treated in
Australia. The plump little Cutticas were Faust
and Marguerite, and when the Signor’s uncertain
tenor happened to be in good order they gave the
public a musical treat. Signora Cuttica, an ex-
cellent actress as operatic artists go, was always a
delightful singer.
»* * *
Dante must have done big business in
Westralia, for manager Louis Lohr still persists
in the opinion that the West is going to boom
again shortly. He declares, indeed, that the
second boom is already an accomplished fact,
although sundry other recent showmen have seen
no signs of it. Dante just now is flourishing in
S.A. and from there he will tour the Victorian
provinces.
Premier Dickson’s statement that, if the
Federal Constitution was accepted, he would
proceed to introduce a One-man-one-vote Bill for
Queensland State elections, but not otherwise,
raised a deafening curse in anti-Federal circles,
and was called bribery, corruption, coercion, and
pretty nearly everything that was bad—barring
elephantiasis and bigamy. Yet there was nothing
very remarkable in Oily James’s attitude. He
simply said in effect: “If you accept this Bill
establishing a One-man-one-vote system through-
out Australia I will understand that you want
One-man-one-vote badly enough to make it advis-
able that I should move. If you don’t, I will
gladly assume, as a restful, tired man who dis-
likes changes, that the present order of things will
hang out a while longer.”

MVphan Ferguson, engineer, boiler-maker, etc,,
of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, lias just secured a
contract for the manufacture and supply of all the bolts,
nuts and washers which may be required by the Victorian
railways during the next three years. He has also con-
tracted to supply all the carbonic acid gas necessary for
working the railway refrigerating and ice-making plant—
this is one of I. and E. Hall’s patent carbonic anhydride-
machines, the same system as has been adopted by the
Govts, of South Australia, Queensland, and Western Aus-
tralia. Mr. Ferguson has a large stock of these machines
(suitable for meat-preserving co.’s dairies, butchers, and
others) on hand at present, which he is selling at greatly-
reduced prices.*

The spectacle of New South Wales, as the only province
which isn’t keeping pace with the general improvement,
makes that old jibe of N.S.W. anti-Federal politicians
about the “necessitous States” look a very foolish jibe
indeed.
In proportion to population Westralia has the
biggest increase, with Queensland very close up. Assum-
ing that things go on as they are doing for the whole of
the financial year the results will be about this :
Increase per
inhabitant
per annum.
Westralia ..£l2O
Queensland ... .. 118
Tasmania .. .. .. .. 0 6 8
Victoria 0 5 0
N.S. Wales Decrease
Westralia’s revenue (partly through increased
taxation) is the largest month’s return in the last 1J
years. For July', the first month of the present financial
year, the expenditure was £382 less than the revenue,
and, for August, it is hoped that the expenditure will be
£40,000 less than the revenue, though the complete re-
turns are not yet available. At this rate, Premier Forrest
should soon make a large reduction in his accumulated
deficit.
N.S.W. spent <£180,324 of loan-money during
August. If this rate keeps up, 1899-1900 will show still
another increase in the ever-expanding loan-expenditure
of N.S.W.

¥ ¥ * *
It is alleged that, for the year ended 30th. June,
1899, the N.S.W. railways made a clear profit of £71,912.
But very similar statements have been made in previous
years, and every time the Auditor-General proved them to
be a fake and a sham, and showed that the deficit was
there just as usual. The N.S.W. railway department has
a curious way of burying its head in the sand, and refus-
ing to recognise things as they are, in order to produce
bogus surpluses that do no good to itself or anybody else.
Many of the old railway loans were floated at a discount.
When a £1,009,000 railway loan only realised £930,0u0
actual cash, the interest has still to be paid on the full
million ; but the railway department claims that it should
pay interest only on £930,000, and that the interest oti
the other £70,000 should be charged to the general
revenue. This device makes the total interest bill no
smaller, and all it does is to enable the depart-
ment to stick its breast-bone out further, and talk more
loudly about the results it has produced. Yet, if the
department were a private co., and it floated a one-million
construction loan for £930,000, it would have to debit
itself with the full million and with interest thereon ; and
seeing that it is supposed to be run on commercial prin-
ciples, the same rule applies.
* * ' * *
The profits of the Union Bank of Australia
again move down a little, the net earnings for the half-
year ended 28th February, 1899, being £42,071. Some
previous profits compare as follows :
August, 1593 ...£60,295 August, 1896 ...£58,837
Februarv, 1834 ... 44,132 February, 1897 ... 37,578
August, 1894 ... 44,676 August, 1897 ... 37,436
February, 1895 ... 37,172 February, 1898 ... 47,696
August, 1895 ... 36,063 August, 1898 ... 42,578
February, 1896 ... 37,996 February, 1899 ... 42,071
The earnings are. equal to £3 7s. 4d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds (capital £1,50(1,000,
reserves £750,000, contingency fund £250,000). The
dividend, which has been stationary at 5 per
cent. for four years, is now raised to 6
per cent., which absorbs £4s,ooo—nearly £3OOO more
than the earnings, thus reducing the old profit-balance
from £29,778 to £26,849. Considering that the Union
Bank has only twice in the last eleven half-years earned
6 per cent., and further considering that the profits have
again been tending downwards in the past tWo half-
years, it is difficult to imagine why the management
decided to make this remarkable splash, or what it saw,
or thought it saw, ahead to justify such a thing. There
is no change of any great importance in the busi-
ness, though the figures show a pretty general increase.
Some three years ago the Union Bank decided that there
were heavy losses, or possible losses, to be provided for,
and transferred £250,000 from reserve to contingent
account for that purpose. Since then, however, there
has been no attempt at a definite cleaning up ; the losses
alluded to haven't been written off, and the old values
still remain in the balance-sheet with the £250,000 as a
provision against them on the other side of the account.
This is only a minor point, however. The really interest-
ing feature in the present statement of accounts is the
action of the directors in increasing the dividend in the
face of a falling revenue.
• * * *
The United Australian Mutual Fire Insurance
Co. issues a balance-sheet for the 18 months ended 30th
June, 1899, and shows a loss of £997. Also, there are
serious doubts as to whether the loss isn’t really a good
deal larger. The doings of the United Australian Mutual
for some time past have been as follows :
Net profit. Dividends.
1891 .. £2873 6 per cent. = £lBB4
1892 .. 2275 „ „ = 1884
1893 .. 1922 „ „ = 1884
1894 .. 1349 „ „ = 1884
1895 .. 2191 „ „ = 1884
1896 .. 2064 „ „ = 1884
Loss.
1897 .. 1508 Nil.
1899 (1J years) 907 Nil.
Profit : £10,259 Dividends : £11,304
During these 8)4 years the United Australian Mutual has
written off £3185 to cover depreciation of securities. It
now gets itself out of a very tight place by
adding on £3672 as increased value of securities.
But for this item, which is put in as revenue,
the visible loss for the 18 months would have been £4579.
The co. never, in all the 20 years of its existence, made
any serious attempt to create a reserve-fund, or reinsur-
ance-fund, or contingency-fund, or to make any other
provision for the future. At the beginning of 1898 it had
a credi t balance of £9OB, and as its losses and expenses were
£3671 more than its revenue and its former credit balance
put together, the situation was awkward. And at this
crisis the property considerately came to the rescue by
increasing in value by £3672 —which just filled the gap
and left £1 to carry forward. The thoughtfulness shown by
the property in increasing by just the exact amount that
was wanted, and doing it just at the right time, can’t be
too much praised. Yet it would have been probably a great
deal better if the co. had simply closed the year with
a debit balance of £3671, and decided to practise strict
economy till it was wiped off, and had not resorted to the
doubtful practice of writing up the property, however
willing the property might be to come to the rescue by
increasing in value to the precise extent required just
when its assistance was so much wanted.
The gross premium income for 18 months was
£28,475 —about £19,000 a year—so the U.A.M. isn’t by
any means a large affair. It has a paid-up capital of
£39,966 ; and as its Sydney and Melbourne properties are
put down at £50,709 (there is a £22,000 mortgage on
them) its capital appears to be, to all intents and pur-
poses, wholly buried in office property. According to the
balance-sheet this asset cost £47,037, so it is put down
at a good deal more than cost—yet the Melbourne pro-
perty, at all events, was bought in the comparatively
glorious days of 1892, when the great slump had only
commenced, and it might almost be expected to be worth
less than its cost instead of more. The United Australian
Mutual is an expensively-managed office, and the policy
which it has pursued of paying away practically all its
profits in dividends is about as bad as could be imagined.
It is now starting afresh with a nominal credit balance of
£l3s. lid. to show as the result of 20 years’ work (though
there seems reason to suppose it possible that a cast-iron
overhaul would reveal instead a considerable deficit), and
with that nominal £1 3s. lid. only arrived at by writing
up property values in a very dubious fashion ; and with
practically all its paid-up capital invested in offices. As
it has £207,000 of callable capital, it is undoubtedly a
safer concern than many for insurers, but so far as share-
holders are concerned it appears to require a kind of
management very different from what it has had in the
past. ■

The National Bank (Melbourne) has repaid
another £410,000 of its 4J per cent, reconstructed de-
posits, leaving only another £410,000 outstanding. The
Bank of Victoria repays £525,000 of its old expensive de-
posits this month, leaving £525,000 still owing. The
Commercial Bank of Australia also repays £350,000 of its
reconstructed debts on 30th September.
* * * *
The Australian Star’s balance-sheet for the
year is a melancholy document, containing very little
detail ; perhaps on the principle that the less said about
the matter the better. The year’s profit is only £922,
and even the miserable little 4 per cent, dividend absorbs
£160(1, so the co. goes £678 to the bad, and its profit bal-
ance is reduced from £B2B to £l5O. The - actual earnings
were only about £2 6s. per cent per annum. And the
Star paid 6 per cent, in 1896, and 8 per cent, in 1897 and
1898. The co. has no reserve fund whatever, and even
then it balances its accounts by including “ goodwill ”
(amount not specified) among its assets. What is the
goodwill of a concern which earns £2 6s. per cent, on its
capital worth? The long-established daily newspaper
which, in these days of printing-paper at, apennv a lb., lino-
types and “ ring” cables, can’t pay a decent profit ought
to die—or, as an alternative, its conductors ought to give
place to a new set. The Star management can’t plead
that it has been spending its funds in the improvement
of the paper, for you never see any expensive special
matter in the Star—which, absurd to relate, is now
(despite that good old story about the man who condi-
tionally sold his soul to the devil) both managed and
edited by the same man. The curse of the Star is that
so many personal and personal-political axes have been
sharpened at its grindstone. It has never been abso-
lutely-bossed by newspaper-enthusiasts and experts, but
has, year after year, been under the heel of one dull,
prosy self-seeker or another. It has been brought into
contempt bv, for one thing, the disgracefully devious
course it took regarding Federation.
* * * *
The London syndicate now nibbling at Adelaide
tramways will probably have to fight the municipal bodies,
as well as the Govt, and Laborites. For some time it has
been proposed to organise a municipal trust to take over
the existing wretched system, an I Kingston favors Govt,
control as the only other alternative. In any case, the
London syndicators will —thank Heaven ! —find it very
hard to get their suggested monopoly through Parlia-
ment.
Earl Kilmorey is making another attempt to
get his Outer-harbor scheme through S.A. Parliament.
Stated by him that a powerful syndicate has been already
floated privately. S.A. Govt, rather frowns on lhe pro-
ject, as an Outer-harbor is part of its policy this session.

In Westralia the three-ball Israelite usually
charges 3d. in the £ per week interest —about 66 per
shent. per annum. A Bill was recently introduced to
make the interest Id. in the H per week, but member
after member rose up to point out that the local “spout”
was a most respectable philanthropist, and the measure
was thrown out with enthusiasm.
B. H. Junction North has gone still further up
Queer-street. Two years ago it issued a balance-sheet
showing that it had paid in the 12 months £25,090 odd in
dividends—the first it had distributed. It wound up
that year with a surplus of nearly £15,000. Then its
workings were upset by the Junction “creep,” it had to
provide new machinery', and it had to fill up its old stopes,
so it ended the next 12 months with no divs. paid and a
surplus reduced to just under £5OOO. No divs. again this
last 12 months : and the surplus is . now back to £2635.
The directors once more try to cheer up shareholders ;
they remark that really there would have been a
profit if it hadn’t cost so much to work the mine.
Soulless banks will not let shareholders draw
against that kind of dividend, though. Setting down the
rugged truth without frills, the mine is poorer on the
year’s work by 49,000 tons of ore and the shareholders
are poorer by £9OO of capital. Prospects are not brilliant,
either. Certainly development work ought to cost less
for a while ; the main shaft is about finished ; the filling-
in of worked-out ground (which ought to have been con-
tinuously attended to) has been completed ; the ore, the
manager says, is getting a little freer from the plant-
racking rhodonite and sandstone—stuff harder than ordi-
nary steel, some of it—and so a profit ought to be made
in the year just beg-un. But the stuff is all too poor to
allow more than a trifle. Moreover, the zinc contents are
comparatively small, so that the solving of the zinc-ex-
traction problem will not mean so much to it as it will to
most of them.
Rhodonite, which stands between Junction
North shareholders and divs., has, like a good many
other cruel things, more than moderate good looks. It
is a silicate of manganese, always ruddy, sometimes dark
rose-red ; the dump wherein it is piled after it is crushed
and washed out of the ore is like a huge pile of rouge— in
fact, it is impure rouge. As encountered underground it
is brutally hard and tough ; and after wearing out drills
and patience there it wears out the hardest steel rolls
when it gets to the mills. Block 10 is also a sufferer
from it.

Developments in the deepest ground of the
Central (Sulphide Corporation) have kept pace with excel-
lent work at the mill and the Cookie Creek works. The
co.’s profits are second only to the B.H. Prop.’s ; and the
first distribution announcement is nearly due. Ashcroft,
by the way, is quite adrift from the co. now, but has got
other backing and is still sulphide-experimenting in
London.
* * * *
“Constant”: (1) B.H. Prop, shares return
about 9 per cent. Until zinc can be profitably extracted,
the divs. are not likely to be larger. The chances are
that the extraction processes will be imperfect at first ; so
a large increase of profits is not to be reasonably looked
for even with zinc-operations in full blast. As to ore-bodies
there is no room for anxiety ; but the uncertainty of the
metal market makes even the B.H. Prop, something of a
gamble. Just as well to observe that divs. have been
kept up to present pitch only by an increase in price of
lead from £9 10s. a ton in ’94 to about £l4 10s. lately.
(2) There is so little likelihood of the B.H. Water Supply
being taken over by council or Govt, that it is not worth
talking about. The Govt, will take it over without any
payment at all in less than 20 years.

The biggest diamond yet found in N.S.W. was
unearthed the other day at the Star of the South mine,
Boggy Camp, Inverell. It is straw-colored, brilliant,
apparently flawless, and weighs between 6 and 7 carats.
* * * *
The Overflow Co. is doing the right thing by
issuing 20,000 preference shares to provide £5OOO, which
will be used solely in developing the property at the
lower levels. At present the Overflow people don't know
exactly what they have got—they are only sure that they
have most alluring prospects. The blast furnace to treat
available sulphides will, it is stated, soon be at work, and
the directors’report says that the profit from cyaniding
gossan, though insufficient to provide funds for develop-
ment, makes the mine self-supporting so far as it goes.

* * * *
The Bulletin office often has a lot of ore in
sight, and though the practice of sending it in has never
been encouraged, this paper receives too many requests
for gratuitous assays and opinions as to value and treat-
ment of samples submitted. Arrangements have now
been made for testing, by assay and otherwise, samples of
ore, provided the sum of £1 is sent with each to cover
cost. Assay's alone do not serve the miner’s purpose,
hence certificates supplied to Bulletin' correspondents
will not only give a statement of metallic contents, but
owners also will be advised by an experienced, practical
metallurgist as to the best methods of treating the ore in
bulk, whether by wet or dry crushing and amalgamation,
smelting, cyaniding, chlorinating, etc. Parcels should
not be less than lib. nor more than 101 b.
* * * *
Editor Bulletin.— ln this week’s issue it is
stated that carbolite is likely to supersede carbide of
calcium, and that it can be manufactured at one-tenth of
the cost, also that large works are being erected at Neu-
hausen. Evidently someone has been pulling your leg.
In the first place, carbolite cannot be made at anything
like the price that carbide of calcium can be turned out;
secondly, there are no works going up at Neuhausen on
Rhine—or anywhere else—to manufacture carbolite. Car-
bolite at present exists only on paper, and is never likely
to get any further. The carbolite wheeze has been going
the round of the colonial newspapers for the last two
years. Whenever it has been proposed to put up carbide-
works, the vested-interest men trot forth carbolite, simply
with a view of stopping the carbide-works being erected,
by frightening timid capitalists. If you live till the first
carbolite-works are in operation, you will be able to take
the belt from Methuselah. I enclose extract from a recent
lecture of Prof. Lewes’, and would like to point out at the
same time that, since carbolite was first boomed, 12
millions of money have been put into new carbide-works.
Yours truly, the Acetylene Gas Company of Australasia,
Ltd., W. Tyree, Managing Director. Sept. 3.
* * * *
Worturpa (S.A.) field threatens to survive the
shock caused by the announcement that its telluride is
telluride of nickel and not telluride of gold. The forma-
tion (writes a correspondent) is a good dioritic ore, but
there is not enough free gold to make a mine of it. As,
however, copper begins to make in the shaft as depth is
attained, and as the copper is rich in gold, it is not im-
probable that a gold-copper mine may eventuate. Inde-
pendently of this formation, there passes through, or
rather on the eastern side of the proclaimed goldfield, a
rich, ahnost-untested belt of copper country, in many
places auriferous. On it has lately been found an enor-
mous cupiferous ironstone body, which is nothing more
nor less than a quarry of copper-ore of high percentage.
A big gang of men is now being put on. Mr. Chaffey
Baker has ten or a dozen men on another new show
known as the Worturpa copper-mine.
¥ » ¥ *
Operations have re-commenced at the old
Kapunda copper-mine, which, it is hoped, will, at copper’s
present price, pay handsomely. Diamond-drilling opera-
tions still proceed at the old “ Burra,” but nothing sensa-
tional has yet occurred. Govt, continues diamond-dril-
ling at Ediacara old silver-lead mine. At least 30 mines
are being worked profitably by small parties in Leigh
Creek district. The Lorna Doone (late Mottram’s) has
been floated into a £50,000 co.—about ten times the v
capital the proposition warrants, when it is considered
that no lode occurs, and that it is practically only pick-
and-shovel work at which the two Mottram brothers
struggled hard and ineffectually for years.. The old
Reedy Creek (S.A.) mine, now renamed “ Kitticoola Gold-
Copper Mine,” is producing magnificent ore, averaging on
the whole only about 3 to 4 per cent, of copper, but con-
taining from 10 to 15dwt. gold. £BOOO has lately been
spent on this show, which is held by three private indi-
viduals. Smelting has been more or less in progress
during the past two months, but so far only some few tons
of high-percentage matte has been produced, as the selec-
tion of material for the furnace was most unhappy.
Judanamutana mine has changed hands and is to be
worked.
Sample of S.A. copper-boom statements : “The
ore body is 600 ft. long and 400 ft. wide The ore
here, which is in veins throughout the entire block, will
average from 8 to 10 per cent, copper.”
* * * *
Mr. David Marks has taken up some leases at)
Kiandra, and just made a contract with the Austral Otis
Engineering Co. Ltd., of South Melbourne, to supply and
build on the leases a complete Bucket Gold Dredge' with
a capacity of 120 tons per hour, and to dredge to a depth
of 30ft. This plant will be one of the most complete
dredges yet built, embodying all the latest improvements
and patents of Messrs. Cutten Bros., the well-known
dredge engineers of Dunedin and Sydney. The contract
requires the dredge to be in full work the first week in
January next. The time is very short, but no doubt the
Austral Otis Co. will be equal to the task.

Westralian Govt. Gazette the other day pub-
lished a list of leases to be cancelled on Sept. 1 unless
overdue rents and incurred fines are paid before that
date. There are no less than 198 Coolgardie leases in
the list, besides others.
Dr. Laver, cricketer Frank’s brother, couldn’t
wait to see the ’Stralians do much playing in England—
he is back again in the West looking after the Mt. Mar-
garet shows which he and English partners own. The
most remarkable thing about these shows so far is
that it is not proposed to hand them over to companies
to work. The infant township thereabout, by the way, is
Laverton.

Of Bendigo mines the Moon group is likely to
make the most noticeable headway for a while. The New
Moon is on good stone, and is div.-paying ; the South
N.M. is putting its profits into a new battery ; and the
Suffolk Tribute is getting into the good country.
* * * •
Sohlapp’s report on the Hampden mines Is hut
a repetition of previous experiences out Cloncurry way.
Enormously rich outcrops and a surface literally strewn
with (often cwt.) slugs of native copper—and nothing to
speak' of below. Apparently no true fissure lodes, as
former co.’s found to their sorrow and expense (beer is a
shilling a glass in Cloncurry) ; and Govt, should think
twice ere building a railway into that howling wilderness
of hairy men and artesian bores. Why not prove the
finds and then construct the line ? Writer knows an old
fossicker who washed a dwt. prospect out of a certain
creek, and in the gladness of his heart put in three
months building a solid humpy and fence, and laying out
a vegetable-garden, to last him to the end of his days.
But when he got to work, that prospect, alas, was about
the only gold to be found for many miles around.

In shipping circles, at least, the coming season is
not too bright. Catering for the anticipated light wool-
clip there are the new 12,000-ton White Star liners, new
Aberdeen liners, 10,000-ton Norddeutscher-Lloyds, besides
the P. and 0., Orient, and French boats, and the huge
fleet Of odds and ends that come out mostly in ballast,
grabbing cargo here with both hands. Soon procuring a
full hold will-be a worse job than writing new life-assur-
ance. The'large shipowners conferred recently as to
standard cargo rates, result as yet indefinite.
The proof of the existence of a Broken Hill
mines’ black-list, which has been a good deal talked about
in Melbourne lately, got into Trades Hall possession
through the carelessness of the manager to whom the
circular “ notice of dismissal ” was sent. Picked up in
the public street leading to this “ Captain’s ” mine, it was
promptly photographed—the local unions had a pre-
vious experience of having to give up a similar tell-tale
uC"ument before it had been even copied. The man
mentioned and minutely described in the paper has since
been provided by the Lahor party, at a moment when it
was dominant in the local Council, with a municipal
billet. But it had got a declaration from him before-hand
that he knew of no reason why he should be refused
employment on the mines.
Concerning!, the black-listing of broken Hill
miners, some of the co’s. directors disclaim responsibility.
If, they say, anything at all is done it is simply a friendly
arrangement among the managers. The system was,
however, carried out under a resolution of the Mine
Managers’ Association, though hardly any men were
“ listed” except from the B.H. Prop. mine. Thepre ent
General Manager of that mammoth concern has a way,
though, of disregarding the Association, and the man-
ager of the next-biggest concern also detests the practice,
and has even given work to good men who have been
repeatedly refused it in the past.

Mayor M‘Eacliarn, as chairman at a
Melb. Try Excelsior meeting, hearing of boys
going out “on their own ” at 13 and 14 and get-
ting a living: “If mere boys can do this, why
have I heard so much about the unemployed ?
Surely if boys can maintain themselves persons of
mature age can do the same.” This is a useful
utterance. It enables people to plumb the depths
of the M‘Eacharn intellect. M'Eacharn as a
supply-and-demand philosopher can not understand
what happens when the supply is of married men
and' the demand is for unmarried boys.
* » * *
The latest in the craze for sending musical
prodigies to Europe for education is the idea of a
Perth (W.A.)’man who is running a distribution
of his local property for the purpose of sending
his daughter, aged 13, “ who is a composer of
music, to Europe, being desirous of giving her a
thorough education in that particular profession,
and not having the means at his command.’’
Subscribers taking five tickets receive (additional
to chance of prize) a copy of the lady’s “Federal
Waltz ” ; subscribers for ten tickets a copy also of
her “ grand triumphal march in commemoration
of the glorious victory of America over Spain.”
* * » »
The Salvation Army at Perth (W.A.) lately
celebrated the opening of its new “citadel” with
the wedding of a male captain and a lady adjutant,
when the blood-and-fire warriors yelled and banged
the drum and shouted hallelujah, and laughed at
the officiating commandant’s jokes for two hours,
IT GENERALLY DOES, TOO.
SHE: “ How dare you, sir, kiss me without my
permission? ”
HE: “Well, I’ve known you ever since you were
ten, and I thought that — that —that ”
SHE: “ That familiarity breeds consent!”
before a crowded audience which paid 6d. a head
to see the show. The marriage ceremony lasted
two hours, and everyone voted it a splendid six-
penn”orth. But next day the morning papers
gravely announced that the real marriage had
taken place in accordance with Westralian law
before six the preceding evening. Consequently
the gorgeous ceremony was merely a coin-collect-
ing device. Now what organisation but the Army
would have thought of a dodge like that? ’

A Hobart gaol rule, that no prisoner shall
wear his own clothes, was responsible, the other
day, for a little awkwardness. A fresh lodger
(sent up for three months) rejoicing in the name
of Hastings Throckmorton Cholmondeley, or
something like that, and to whom all the warders
touched their caps in awe, thinking at first that lie
was a British Imperial Commissioner come to
inspect the gaol, was found to be of such huge
dimensions that he had to stop in bed whilst they
manufactured a rig-out for him.
* * * *
A gorgeous idea is suggested by N.S.W.
Judge G. B. Simpson’s recent punctuation of the
necessity for an official stenographic record of
divorce-evidence. Why should not Governments,
in providing this needful short hand assistance,
turn at the same time an honest, if malodorous,
penny by the publication of (say) the Matri-
monial News and Divorce Court Gazette?
Such a sheet need never be “ gravelled for lack of
matter,” since, under Federation, when there
would be no local “ rortiness ” to be served-np,
the divorce-proceedings of the other provinces
would come in handy. There would be no
material reason, for that matter, why the primest
matrimonial smellfulnesa of the world at large
should not be rehashed to contribute to the attrac-
tions of a sheet that would sell enormously amongst
the servant-girl class, and, by consequence,
amongst women in every position. And when,
to the scullery-wench, and the fat cook, and
the frosty maiden - aunt, and the matron
of terrific virtue, we add the white-
vested right-thinking person, and the monkey-
bearded Holy Joe, and the sainted young man
who teaches Sunday-school—and, in a word, every
soul who pretends to superior Respectability and
Piety—why, The Bulletin is almost sorry it
didn’t keep such magnificent possibilities a secret
of its own ingenuous bosom.

N.S. Wales Justice Department should
try to get a firm grip on the train of circumstances
that led to the return, marked “unserved,” of a
writ of attachment for costs issued against a
policeman doing daily duty in a Northern river
township.

* * * *
A late disappearance case in Maoriland (if
the disappearer hasn’t returned before this gets
into print) furnishes a curious detective problem.
He was an ordinary plain ratepayer, reason-
ably prosperous, and just as unromantic and
stodgy as any other ratepayer. Nobody
suspected him of having a Past—nor much
of a future; he was just a dull, flat citizen
leading a brownish-grey existence in which
he got up, and washed, and went to bed
again. But one day he had been reading
the Auckland daily paper, and he rose up
suddenly, dropped the journal, put a
few clothes in a travelling-bag, and
rushed out of the door. It has been
assumed that he saw, looking at him out
of the paper, the ghost of a horrid Past,
and in the absence of any other clue the
paper has been overhauled in search of
the news which gave him such sudden
notice to quit, but no item in it seems to
fit. And the local ’tec., whose idea in
such cases is to tramp heavily down to
the wharf and ask if an excited-looking
person with a brown bag was seen getting
on board the boat for Hokitika or Hoki-
anga, or Hokanui, can’t make much of
such delicate investigations.
* * *
.Re the unsafe-boiler question. Is any
provision made for the inspection of the
boilers on the numerous steam-lighters
which knock about Sydney wharves? Pre-
sent writer is assured by an employe on one
of them that the condemned boiler of a river-
steamer was over a year ago placed on a
lighter. Of course, the men daren’t speak
for fear of the sack.

OVERHEARD AT A RACE MEETING.
GOOD OLD SORT (to his friend): “Come and
have a taste, Bill. Eve got a stitch in my
side. ”
“All right, old man; but you’d be better off if you
had the stitch in your mouth !”
How absurd are some of the Transvaal
cablegrams! “The Bulawayo forces, under Baden-
Powell, are drilling daily.” At the present junc-
ture, if the local regulars were not drilling daily,
the cableman might step in and tell the. world,
otherwise it is all palpable padding. But hold !
Probably it is a puff for Baden - Powell. The
Baden-Powell gang are big self-advertisers, and
are great in London “colonial ” circles.
* * * *
Gov. Brassey's intention as to his return
to England has been changed, and he now says he
is consulting the Colonial Office as to whether he
should wait till the arrival of the Governor-
General, or depart ere that personage comes. The
answer is almost certain to be that he should wait;
but he will be out of Victoria within the twelve
months.

Joe Wollinski’s “ Interior ” at N.S. W. Art
Society’s Show proves that High Art and sign-
painting are not incompatible. Strange also that
this work (the only one purchased in that quarter
by th(p National Gallery) should bear the same
catalogue-number (67) as Lambert’s “Across the
Black Soil Plains,” certainly the pick of the other
lot’s show.
* * * *
At last annual meeting of East Melbourne
Hebrew Congregation it was stated that of recent
years 25 per cent, of Jewish marriages have been
mixed. Apparently the Gentiles are absorbing
the Jews as the Saxons in England absorbed the
Normans.
* * * *
One development of the mixed marriage.
They point to a couple in Sydney who have fre j
quent differences of opinion, leading often to
physical violence. In extremes the Protestant
husband fetches the R.C. priest to talk the R.O.
wife to reason, and as often the wife fetches the
parson to reduce the husband in like manner.
* * * *
Down South a distinguished military offi-
cer of long service was transferred to a responsible
civil post, on the understanding that his army and
his civilian service should be reckoned on the same
scale in fixing his pension. This arrangement
does not seem to have been properly registered,
for on reaching the end of his service the official
finds himself shunted both by the army and civil
departments. Which difficulty is almost as bad as
that of the lady who got married in two churches,
and doesn’t know which marriage to get a divorce
from.

For ways that are dark and tricks that are
vain the garden Chinee is not in it by the side of
the woman who has graduated in matrimony. A
lady has turned up, in a Vic. maintenance case,
whose husband guarded his pockets so sedulously
that, to get at them, she was constrained to knock
him up in the small hours of morning, with
her elbow in his ribs, declaring, in a hoarse
and horrified whisper, that there was a
burglar downstairs. When hubbv
arose and stole below to pro-
tect his hearth and home,
his wife went hastily through
his pockets, and cleaned him
out so thoroughly that he
nearly died of loneliness next
day. The husband deserted her,
but the law tells him to return
or else stump up.
• * «
The demise of Lady Denison revives two
or three Gov. Denison references. First, Fort
Denison, in Sydney, was built under his super-
vision and after plans approved by him. It was
not through a Russian or Chinese scare, as is
commonly believed, that the structure was put up,
but owing to a strongly-entertained opinion of
the Governor’s that the Americans meditated an
attack. Second, Gov. Denison was an engineer
by profession, and one of his hobbies in that line
was to construct railway lines with hard Austra-
lian timber. Third, trawling, of which so much
has been lately heard in consequence of Frank
Farnell’s sea picnics, was a big subject with
Denison in 1859. He, Daniel Cooper, and a few
others hired a schooner and trawled Botany Bay.
Denison promised treats of fish to a host of people,
but all the day’s trawling produced was 1 john
dory, 2 flounders, and 1 sole—whereat Sydney
laughed.
* * * *
The secretary of the committee of the
North Shore (Sydney) friendly societies’ demon-
stration has resigned rather than ask the Governor
for a subscription to the fete. He explains that he
objects to asking money from one who might not
care to refuse, and who will be merely consenting
to be “ bled.” If His Excellency gave anything,
argues this downright official, it would not be
because he had any particular interest in North
Sydney or its friendly societies, but just because
he happens to be Governor. Good.
* * * *
“ Gavendo tutus": Madden, C.J., talks too
much, and glows more garrulous as he ages. The
suppression of reports of the J. B. Gregory case
by the Courts and the Press is national dishonor
and a treason to the public. Gregory has argued
for his liberty with sanity and sagacity, and
whether he is inad or not, it should be impossible
in Victoria to compile a secret dossier and practi-
cally judge the man in camera. Gregory has
three brothers-in-law, one an Ahgus sub editor,
another permanent head of the department con-
trolling lunatic asylums, and the third bar-leader.
* * * *
What music-teaching has come to in
Sydney. Clip from the local D.T.
It is somewhat startling to find you can receive a
pianoforte lesson for sixpence. The writer read a notice
to this effect lately, and made an enquiry,
“ Have you many pupils?”
“No, sir; I’ve only got threepupils so far ; but I should
like to have ten, because I wane to earn enough money to
get a bathing suit. Of course, I can’t teach grown-up
people ; I don’t know enough.”
* * * *
Australian-educated C.E. parsons who go
to England are not accepted by Bishops there
unless they undergo an examination. A recipro-
cation of this indignity would be about the best
weapon Queensland could employ against the par-
son-importing tactics of Bishop Webber.

Booth’s latest : “ I regard the people o f
Australia as a thoroughly religious people—a
people with whom religion is a great force ! ” This
criticism has at least the flavour of novelty. Pre-

* * * *
The Salvation Army boasts much of its
undenominational philanthropy ; but when it has
■wages to scatter it is a bit more exclusive. At
the opening of the “ Citadel ” in Perth, the other
day—whereat, by the way, Bigjohn, under the
guidance of the holy Throssel, offered up a little
prayer—the Commandant crowed that every bit
of the job, from the designing to the hod-carrying,
had been done by Army men.
• * * *
The Queensland Police Commission now
sitting in Brisbane is finding out a few curious
facts. Re the famous Gatton tragedy it turns out
that a telegram was received in the office of a
prominent police official on Dec. 27 at 11.16 a.m.,
announcing the triple murder. This same tele-
gram was never opened till the morning of Dec.
28, by which time the murderer or murderers had
gained an invaluable 24 hours’ start. The head of
the force—it is alleged—is mainly occupied with
office work ; consequently inspection and visitation
of outside stations are largely neglected. Dis-
content and inefficiency appear to be rampant.
At present Queensland, judging by its own
statistics, appears to be about the safest place to
commit murder in south of the line. It is also
about the unsafest of all places to insult the police
in.
* * * *
The other day, at Wilcannia, Judge Gib-
son sitting, an undefended whaler was sentenced
to a long term for breaking into a bush hut and
idiotically humping off most of the contents.
The gaol doctor gave evidence that the man was
pretty sane—“might be wrong on some points,”
but was right on others. In spite of this remark-
able evidence no other inquiry was made, and the
man was found guilty. The Judge said that he
passed the long sentence in the interests of
society; but if the prisoner behaved himself he
would probably get out in 12 months, if he was
properly sane. Tbe farce (or tragedy) of trying
and sentencing a man like that!

“ Doko ” : Heard this in an Australian
capital. “ Hullo—lookin’ pretty glum ! What’s
wrong? ” “ Oh, badly ‘ had ’ over that last mob
of cattle. Not a sound beast in the lot. A cow I
killed this morning was nearly eaten away with
cancer. Had to feed her to the pigs ! ” Apropos ,
it is a fact that cancerous and pleuritic cattle are
bought up wholesale by a ham-and-bacon curing
firm in the vicinity of said capital.
* » • *
A Melb. young man, aged 21, swallowed a
dose of arsenic the other day, all along of a 17-year-
old damsel who had won his heart at churcb choir
practice. He didn’t die, and when he was tried
for attempted suicide the damsel gave evidence
calculated to make her adorer feel very sorry for
the pains he had suffered in his innards. “ She
believed that defendant had tried to take his life
because she had refused to marry him,” said the
heroine of the romance. Apparently she was al-
ways solid on the point that she didn’t want to
marry him, anyhow. “On 23rd inst., accused
came to wish her good-bye, and said he had taken
poison. He had a bottle in his hand, but witness
did not really know if he had taken poison or not.”
This picture of the arsenieised swain hurling bis
sentimental adieux at an idol who didn’t worry
herself as to whether he would soon be dead or
otherwise is rich in serio-comic effect. It is a pic-
ture to study in the light of the young lady’s fur-
ther evidence : “About three months ago, accused
told her that if she gave him up he would first
shoot her and them himself. This frightened her
for a- time, but afterwards she dismissed the mat-
ter from her mind.”

* • * *
Queensland is the happy hunting-ground
of the adulterator. Publicans were fined at Croy-
don recently for selling “ whisky ” that proved
to be mostly compounded of an aniline pre-
paration and sulphuric acid. Brisbane grocers
are,being daily fined for selling vinegar manufac-
tured of more or less deleterious materials “on
the premises” or raspberry jam, compounded of
pumpkin, chemical flavorings, and colored with
aniline. As to milk and bread sophistication,
it is too common a matter to excite notice.
However, that’s nothing. When a province has
adulterated its population with the scum and
scourings of Asia, it doesn’t matter much if the
tucker is also a little doctored.
» * * •
The fire-alarms attached to the water-
sprinklers of Melb. warehouses have an unac-
countable way of starting off at every change of
pressure, and in one big hotel situated next door
to a softgoods firm, the boarders are continually
being scared by the booming of the alarm-bell in
the small hours of the a.m. Persons of assorted
sexes flock down the corridors in extreme disha-
bille, and are with great difficulty restrained from
bolting down street; while on one occasion things
were complicated by a wild, shrieking figure in
nothing but a singlet bounding into the midst of the
assembled guests. This proved to be a parson,
who had been suddenly aroused, fleeing for his life.
* * * *
The wise criminal shuns a Court as the
Devil shuns holy water ; but wise criminals are
few, and the other day a southern couple fell in
badly. A mate with whom they had been con-
victed in Sydney five years back was in the dock,
and, as it was known he intended denying the
Sydney conviction if found guilty, the Crown was
in a quandary. It wasn’t worth adjourning pend-
ing the arrival of a Sydney policeman, and it had
just been decided to let the prisoner go as a first
offender when a d. spotted the two mates. In
five minutes a couple of subpoenas were served on
them and they stood in the humiliating position of
being about to be called to testify to the convic-
tion of their pal. Happily for their peace of mind
the prisoner was “ turned up.”

One rule of a recently-formed Westralian
Wharf-Laborers’Union provides that “immoral
conduct” shall disqualify. Funny to think of
•“ immorality ”as a reason why a man shouldn’t
truck the sacred wool-bale or hump the holy spud.
* * * *
Noteworthy that none of the
coffins being turned up at the western end of the
Yic. markets, Melbourne, have name-plates on
them. The Evening Herald supposes they were
“ planted ”30 years ago, but 30 years ago none
but corpses possessed of freehold rights and
vaults were buried in the old cemetery. The
section in which the nameless coffins have been
found was chiefly patronised in the 40’s and 50’s
by people who didn’t care to pay more than they
oould help for their last beds. It is likely that
the coffins and bones recently disinterred were the
property of people who “ went out ” long before
the fuss and fury of the gold discoveries, and
when Melbourne was as yet in the egg.

* * * *
Joseph of Melbourne, is a bur-
glar of parts. In gaol he was “ put on to a good
crib to crack ”by a fellow-sufferer. .The “crib”
was in a country town, and Mr. Kitchen when
liberated was a penniless but impenitent thief.
How to get to Kerang on nothing was the problem
that beset him, and he solved it by interviewing
the Charity Organisation Society with a woeful
story of sorrow, suffering and want nobly borne,
of hunger, and worry, and Christian fortitude.
The C.O.S. provided Joseph with a free pass to
Kerang, “ where a job was waiting him,” and in
less than 24 hours Joseph had cracked his crib,
and was backin Melb. again with the spoil. Joe
undertook to return the railway-fare out of his
first earnings, but here his sense of humor failed
him, and the C.O.S. was spared the risk of pro-
secution as a receiver of stolen goods.

There was a queer scene on a
Sydney wharf t’other day. A couple of
burly fellows were waiting on a steamer
evidently for someone to come aboard,
but up to casting-off time the wished-
for person did not appear. As soon,
however, as the watchers were
ashore, and the boat well clear of
the 'wharf, the wanted one appeared
from below, and began playing on
a cornet “ You’ll remember me,” ac-
companied by a volley of language
from the disappointed ones.
* * *
The most lurid portion of
Thursday Island is called Yoko-
hama. In Yokohama are 77
“women” of many races, and
with not a moral to divide among
them, and when the pearling-sea-
son is over, and 1200 pearling-boats
have congregated at the little isle,
Yokohama becomes too dreadful for
language. The great anxiety of the
pearlers, divers and crews seems to
be to spend all their cash and have
an incandescent time before work
starts again. One out-of-the-
way member of the painfully
cosmopolitan population of
Thursday Island is an Esqui-
maux, who became a Christian
at Uppernavik, in Greenland.
Why anyone should be so
careful |to report that any-
body in the red - painted,
sun-blasted island is a Christian
passes understanding, but some
papers are so conscientious that,
whenever a man cuts his mother’s
head off, they will move heaven
and earth to find out what church
he habitually attended.
A mature and straight-laced maiden, who
owned and manag> d a farm in the days of old
Van Diemen, was in the habit of overseeing her
employes from the house-top by means of a tele-
scope. This unpleasant behavior was at length
resented, and a plot arranged. morning the
slavey heard awful screams from the roof, and,
ascending, found her mistress performing a rat-
tatoo on the shingles with her heels. The tech-
nique of the plot was never discovered (disclosure
spelt “cat” in those days), but it worked. One
had merely to mention “telescope,” and the
ancient virgin would close up like the article
itself, and no robin’s corset blushed redder than
she.
* * * *
*How does what Dickens wrote about the
oid Bow-street runners hit off the Australian
detective of to-day?—
They never lost a public occasion of jobbing and trading
in mystery, and making the most of themselves. Con-
tinually puffed, besides, by incompetent magistrates,
anxious to conceal their own deficiencies, and hand-in-
glove with the penny-a-liner of that time, they became a
sort of superstition.
Straight on the mark !
* • * *
By order of Gov. Hunter, in 1806, free
people found guilty of illicit distillation were
expelled from N.S. Wales. Despite this drastic
ordinance illicit distillation con-
tinued to be as vigorously pursued
as among the free people of
the present day. The illicit
distillers were them-
selves a fairly sober
lot, but they sold
the stuff at high
prices to the con-
victs and grew
wealthy by de-
basing them,
for many of the
convicts, men
and women,
were born drunk-
ards and would
commit any
crime to obtain
liquor. Hunter
drank and was
tolerant of drink,
and his ukase
against the rum and
whisky makers of his
day was in the inte-
rests of the morally
helpless class whom
they fleeced of money
and labor, and tempted,
if they did not coerce,
to crime. These illicit
distillers, as many of them
were not found out, and
those who aided and abetted
them in getting their products into
consumption, became either in their
own persons or in their immediate
descendants the first aristocracy of
Botany Bay.
a. man and his wife were on their veran-
dah in S. Melb. one recent evening when a man
arrived at their front gate breathless and excited :
“ Your little boy,” he gasped, “run over—round
corner!” The husband did not wait for more,
but dashed up the street, and the wife merely
paused to utter a cry of agony before making off
in the same direction. They spent ten minutes
hunting for an accident and inquiring in vain for
the mangled remains, and then returned home,
convinced that a mistake had been made. Mean-
while their informant had been through the house
and gleaned about £4O worth of portable valu-
ables. The police are “making inquiries.”

“ Puyakka ” returns to the charge :
But a few truisms, and the Hairy Man arises in his
might to devastate the intruder upon his tree-top. How-
ever, his cocoa-nuts have missed their mark. To
“Speculum” (5/8 ’99) I beg to point out that it is not
the average man or woman who is under consideration,
but the hairy man and the hairy-loving female, a pair
who diverge widely from the norm. The average healthy
woman possesses a rooted dislike to the sight of abnorm-
ally hairy limbs or chest, a fact well-known to “ beauty
men,” who regularly shave these parts if cursed
with a redundancy of fell. With regard to strength,
I take it that the potential of healthy develop-
ment gives the indication to a man’s physical capa-
city— i.e., development unaccompanied by an over-
taxed heart or other evidence of excessive strain.
The so-called “natural athlete” usually pays for his
feats with an unsound part, but the properly-built-up
physique enables the machine to be driven to its highest
speed without injury, owing to perfection of balancing of
parts. And this I take it is the end to be attained in
growing a race of perfect physique. “Speculum” may
take a “physiological interest in athletics,” but the
writer goes further—he makes part of his living from this
very source. The hirsute norm, so ingeniously manufac-
tured by “Speculum,” is logical! v denied by his creator
in the next breath by the admission of my former argu-
ment —that he is capable of less development in strength
and endurance than his smoother brother. In other
words, the hirsute pupil is at the end of his tether when
the smooth man is just commencing. Regarding the
progeny of “manufactured athletes” (a side issue, but
worth considering) “Speculum” forgets that the trans-
mission of marked acquired physical characteristics is not
a matter of one generation, but of many. And, anyhow,
the assertion that such children are “probably ... in-
significant both physically as well as intellectually ” is
useless as an argument, practical experience and observa
tion proving the direct opposite. To “Ben Sun”
(5/8 ’99) I beg to point out that there was no attempt to
prove that the smoother skin denoted the stronger con-
stitution, but merely the fact that hair—as hair —is no in-
dication of physical strength, despite a widely-spread
popular idea to the contrary upon which the worship of
the Hair-Loving Female is based. Nor, on the other
hand, is a smooth skin “ remarkable as a concomitant of
physical weakness.” The writer closely examines several
hundreds of adul s per annum, and his experience is dia-
metrically opposite. “Ben Sun’s’ racial theory, if carried to
its end, lands one in strange places, wherein the Hairy Aino
is classed above the Jap, and the African Pigmy stands
out as superior to the Yankee. As for the Montenegrin,
anybody who has knocked about in the Levant will not
hesitate to place him, despite his moustache, far below
the Northern Chinaman, both in intellect and physique.
Finally, for the question of the relative frequency of the
hirsute homo, let me refer “Ben Sun” to Darwin’s
Descent of Man, and similar evolutionary text-books.
“The politician looks in a kaleidoscope,
the statesman in a telescope.”

UP THE HARBOUR?
Sail Saucy Verse from over sea,
The editorial Heads between,
Nor lie despairing in the lee
Of editorial Quarantine.
The Sow and Pigs securely pass
Of editorial caprice,
Nor founder on the Bottle and Glass
Of editorial sprees.
The hungry Pinchgut shallows shun
Of editorial stint;
Up Darling Harbour quickly run
To anchorage in print.

the “ naicest ” social and artistic sets when she
gets to Europe, she will behold the same willowy
grace in the feminine form divine as sunny Aus-
tralia’s fair daughters are celebrated for. Wher-

P.D.s abouud. The P.D. is the world’s corset.*

was one of the principal books of its year, was an un-
doubted success, and without doubt so is the new light of
Australasia, the “ Albatross Lamp Wick.” “It requires
no trimming,” emits no odour, uses less oil, prevents
smoky chimneys, and is far safer than the ordinary wiok.*
