A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 5, 1809.
Mr Dear Myee,—
We have i-t on their own authority that all the
Nicest people in Sydney haven’t been invited to
the Govt.-House race-week revels. Also, on the
same unsupported authority, equally many will
be the guests who won’t know their way from the
door to the ball-room.
For the fatal cards are cut, with a vengeance.
That is to say, with a footnote that never fails to
hit Potts Point right in the centre of its oldest
wound, to the effect that “ You are requested to
show tickets at the gate.”
Another peculiarity of the new invitations is
that the Aide-de-camp invites, not the Governor.
This is something new to Sydney, which misses
the cordial brotherliness of “The Governor re-
quests the honor,” and can’t sun itself in a way to
make up for the sad bereavement in the Aide-de-
camp in waiting.
I hear that every man at the garden-party who
is not a parson will be an artist. Ever since he
came, Lord Beauchamp has been systematically
kind and friendly to our artists. He has had them
up alone to an evening-party, as you know, and
enjoyed them all to himself. Now he is going to
share them with Society. They are invited in a
body—two bodies, I should say—to the garden-
Kty on the 11th. At last there will be some-
y to admire the view in a whole-hearted
manner that has nothing tb do with strawberries
and cream. By the way, the Govt.-House people
themselves are never tired of rapturing over the
outlook.
Just at present I don’t know any clergymen, so
am unable to solve a delicate question that crops
up here—whether the cloth will have to show its
ticket at the gate along with the rest. A
Heavenly pew-opener might reasonably, from his
point of view, resent having to show tickets for
Govt. House. Which reminds me that a Wool-
lahra clergyman mixed himself up in a trouble-
some earthly problem the other Sunday—ran on
his prayers and prayed, “ God bless the Governor,
his wife and family,” before the agitated rustle of
the congregation brought him to his senses. I
haven’t heard if he has been asked to the straw-
berry struggle.
Apropos of artists just ncJW, the Art Society’s
conversazione went off smoothly on Thursday
night, speeded by some good music and singing,
and a supper that was taxed to the uttermost by
the large, hungry crush. Harry Leston recited ;
Henry Weir, Reg. Davis and Herr Staedtgen
warbled; a man made music out of jam-tins ; and
a sensible dog was satisfied to do his performance
in dead silence. Percy Spence’s three pictures
that have just been sent out were on view—horsey
subjects, all of them, and small. During the
evening Frank V .Sony’s top-hat was the subject
of an aniinateu argument. The new instructor,
who is very popular with the classes, can’t be
brought to confess to that chimney-pot despite
several single-visioned people’s belief that they
gazed hard on it in the nap.
The Society of Artists’ Ball went off pleasantly
at Need’s rooms last Thursday, worked up by Mr.
Goring Thomas and Mr. Leo Dobbin. The
frocking was neither fancy nor outre; in fact,
everyone looked quite normal. Colonel and Mrs.
Savage were among the guests. Mrs. Learoyd
wore the frock of the evening, a black net sparkling
in a dull, subtle way with steel paillettes. Miss
Thea Proctor struck one of her poster effects in a
red blouse and black skirt. Mrs. Julian Ashton
was in black, and Julian also—who, by the way,
was looking happier than usual because a certain
red paper had-invited him to take a bite out of the
critics who bite pictures. Did you hear the story
of the old lady who once entertained Julian Ash-
ton ? He tells it himself, delightfully. She gave
him a copy of the interview, and looked on proudly
while he read : “Mr. Ashton is a little man, with
red hair, long yellow teeth, and frayed cuffs ! ”
On Monday afternoon Mrs. Brough played
hostess at the Society of Artist -' rooms charm-
ingly, spontaneously. She looked wonderfully
fresh and unjaded after all her heavy work in
“Quex” —fresher, in fact, than she looks on the
stage. She stood at the doorway and received a
great crowd of guests with a white bouquet in one
hand, an elegant frock of black lace over white
satin, and a big black hat with black and white
feathers. Her “at-home” was a distinct success,
touched up as it was with the theatrical
element that always tells. Miss Temple was in a
simple black frock with a fit to it, a sailor
collar of tucked black silk, and a little mauve and
black toque. Miss Bessie Thompson (Mrs. Reg.
Dartrey) wore a grey boa over a grey and white
frock. Mr. Dartrey, whose old Governor in Quex
is such a telling bit of characterisation, from his
dyed butterfly moustache to his brown spats,
emerged from behind his elderly disguises as a
good-looking young man with an eye-glass. The
beautiful Joseph Carne fulfilled all expectations
by not being there. That sort of thing is part of
the fascination. Mr. Brough, Mr. Lovell, Mr.
Leslie Victor, commonly known as “Freddy,”
after his henpecked part in the “Liars,” Miss
Grace Noble in a dark blue and white foulard and
white wings in her little hat, were also in evidence.
One feature of the afternoon was the number of
madly-in-love little girls who hugged Mrs.
Brough’s arm and leaned rapturously against her
shoulder.
A second visit to “Lord Quex ” found crowds of
people pouring in, and everything going as usual,
with one exception. Mrs. Brough has given up
that much-discussed action of hers at the end of
the first act. She no longer publicly pulls up her
stockings. Everyone has an opinion about the
matter. Some people like it. Some people don’t.
All people say they don’t. For five nights Mrs.
Brough valiantly plunged at it, though you could
see through the glasses a forced smile, as though
she was not taking kindly to the deed. Then she
gave it up altogether. The house was keenly
disappointed—l wish I could write, “especially
those who had not seen it,” but that doesn’t seem'
to be the case, judging from an elderly gentleman
in tbe d.c. with another elderly gent. He was
heard whispering in a loud voice, “Now, she—
watch,” then “My heavens she doesn't 1 What’s
the meaning of that?” P.S.—Since I wrote the
above I learn that Mrs. Brough has resumed the
hose business. The act went as flat as a pancake
without it, and the disappointment of the house
couldn’t be misunderstood. The night she put it
in again, the play took on its sparkle.
The public are taking kindly to Quecks —that’s
the pronunciation. Surprise is the leading feature
of the play. The papers should be asked not to
tell the story, for all the excitement lies in not
knowing what is coming. As a matter of fact,
nothing happens. Everything goes flat or smooth.
But while the play is unfolding, the house is ex-
citedly using up all its imagination for a private
view of the finish.
Last week I had no time to allude to Miss
Bessie Thompson’s frocks—she sports a rose-pink
satin polonaise that would be better if it were not
so heavily up-braided in black, over some pink
chiffon flounces, for a dinner dress, and adds that
dashing air that characterises all her dressing.
Nor had I space to mention Mrs. D’Arcy
Read’s sweet old Lady Owbridge, the only
being in the play who gets into one’s
sympathies. Mrs. Brough plays the famous third
act in a night-dress, covered with an utterly
hideous red and gamboge Japanese wrapper that
would kill the complexion of a wax doll. But
Mrs. Brough doesn’t mind, and one cannot help
admiring her indifference to looks when she has
something big to do. She always cries real tears,
lets her cheeks go all over the place, and sports
redeyes with a truly heroic foititude that comes
as a great relief after the actresses who cry “dry,”
and carefully adjust their hair when their grief
has finished carrying them away.
In describing frocks, it is becoming more and
more difficult to give all materials their right
names. Particularly stage-frocks. Often a voile
can’t be distinguished from a cashmere, or a silk
from a muslin. Consequently, a Melbourne
paper gave Bessie Thompson a weird frock,
the other day—“ a muslin covered with
spangles.” Your feminine mind will know
that “net” was the thing. But in case an un-
enlightened man reads this, I must explain that a
muslin covered with spangles is about as melan-
choly, sartorially, as a dress-suit crewel-worked
with forget-me-nots.
A poor little man who used to know all about
embroideries and decorations died last week.
Everyone knew “ Freddy Wyatt.” A brain-
disease was the cause of his death. The first inti-
mation of it was in finding his room all draped
with ties —ties all over the floor and furniture ;
hundreds of ties about everywhere. His head had
been turned by. ties. Sydney people will very
much miss his able platform decorations.
Rignold’s newplay, “The Indian Mutiny,’’gives
the ladies of the cast no dressing to speak of.
Lilian Wheeler contrives to look fascinating in
Indian draperies, gracefully arranged, with a lot
of Oriental embroidery worth looking at. Two
striking English frocks are worn by Miss Roland
Watt-Phillips and Miss Roxy Barton. Miss
Phillips wears a ’57 costume of gas green, flounced
to the waist, and - topped with a poke bonnet.
Miss Barton’s flounces are pink, and her hat is a
little flat thing with strings and a wreath of rose-
buds.
Sydney Hoben’s concert is a fixture for Septem-
ber 26, at the Y.M.C.A. Hall, when the pro-
gramme will be all of Mr. Hoben’s composing.
The hunted flower-sellers are getting up a
petition to the Mayor and Aldermen, praying to
be allowed to continue their business as other
business men are allowed to continue. And why
not ? Everyone would miss the only picturesque
thing in our city streets, and women would deeply
regret their best chance of getting flowers for the
home in a cheap and convenient way. The flower-
sellers expect to get 20,000 signatures from the
general public. Let us hope they will. It looks
like one of those cases when it’s a good thing to be
able to write your name.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Melbourne people can scarcely believe it pos-
sible that Gov. Beauchamp’s women - folk are
going ’Ome without having once seen the Mel-
bourne Cup. In fact, the general discussion seems
to show that social Melbourne is : narked ’ about
it, especially as this year’s Cup is expected to be
the most brilliant since the Boom. Turner’s rosy
Budget is reflected in things fashionable. The
windows of the swagger milliners are gorgeous,
and a leading jeweller avers that old times have
come again.
“ Young Tom Brassey is expected in Melbourne
next month. He is just now in Capetown, no
doubt lending his counsel to Sir Alfred Milner.
His ponderous pa has a great opinion of young
Tom’s political abilities, though so far English
constituencies have not been induced to take the
same view. Tom will stay for some weeks at
Government House.
“The Findon Harriers Hunt Club meeting, an
annual dream of fair women, gee-gee, and after-
noon tea, takes place at Moonee Valley on 23rd,
and the Messrs. Miller have begun to boom the
toney function with businesslike ardor. All the
'Naicest will have nominations for the Lady’s
Bracelet, not to mention smaller races which are
just as convenient for any prudent pun tress who
only wants ‘a dozen of gloves to nothing.’ The
attendance of Gov. Brassey and spouse is included
in the Findon Bill, it goes without saying.
Seemingly the club has ‘supporters’ as well as
members. The latter are three-guinea personages
entitled to put two ladies through the gates, but a
supporter is a mere guinea item, who gets a double
ticket the same as though he were a charity ball
subscriber.
“Still that Hunt Ball fizz sizzles. For the
youths whose legs rebelled against the surfeit of
sweets there has been a kind of court-martial en-
quiry. Two of the iniquitous imbibers faced the
music and defied the Censor to prove that they had
swallowed too much. ‘I was disgraced,’ said
one brow-beaten Nimrod, ‘by having my name on
the committee, when such things occurred.’ But
his less severe colleagues sympathised and saw the
youth out of the inquisitorial chamber in an
au-revoir- chappie style. So there is an end to the
matter.
“ Extract from a recent number of Woman at
Home re Lady Hopetoun:—
“ ‘ Lady Hopetoun is so full of fun,’ was heard on every
side in Melbourne. Ever eager for youthful enjoyment,
Lord and Lady Hopetoun gathered round them a jocund
throng, and their ‘surprise’ entertainments are now
talked of by the Melbourneites—those delightful dinners,
when servants in native costume waited on their guests,
and the table decorations were a dream of beauty. Then
came the after-dinner visits ‘ to see the animals,’ for Lord
Hopetoun kept a menagerie at Government House, in
which his wife took an almost greater interest than he.
“ The intense jollity of Lord Hopetoun’s round-
eyed colleen is historical, of course. Two distin-
guished Melbourne potentates will never forget
her genial ‘Take away that plate ! ’ —tbe plate with
the ‘filipina’ nut on it, and her merry way of
drawing the line of familiarity at ‘ Lord Hope, if
you please.’ But the suggestion that she kept a
staff of black servants in beads and feathers, and
used to flutter around after dinner feeding her pet
lions and kangaroos with lumps of sugar, is quite
new to Melbourne. Fuller particulars are in-
vited from any member of the jocund throng who
took part in those wild doings.
“Widow Clarke is having a giddy time in Eng-
land, and makes no sign ot returning to Mel-
bourne. All her doings are faithfully para-
graphed by the penwoman who erstwhile wrote a
novel ci-uelly gibbeting good-natured old Swil-
liam’s wife, and then obtained the aid of the un-
willing victim for the publication of the screed.
The Argus, after every mail-day, duly chronicles
where Janet, Lady Clarke was, according to the
most recent information, where she was going
next, and how she was enjoying her well-adver-
tised self. She is now in Scotland.
“At a Hawthorn woman’s franchise meeting,
the other night. Major Reay, the chairman, was
going to ‘ put the motion ’ when an objector arose
and said he had travelled all the way from Ascot
Vale to ‘enter his protest against this foolish and
delusive movement.’ But, as the protestor was
only about 16 years of age, the women present —
all old enough to be his mother—refused to
take him seriously, and he was fain to depart,
plaintively murmuring that ‘if they got the fran-
chise Society would be ruined ! ’
“At the cosy Bijou, last Saturday afternoon,
Benno Scherek repeated his quaint ‘Talk about
the Orchestra,’ for the benefit of those gothic
‘outsiders’ who want to get a notion of how a
large, all-round musical effect is created. Herr
Bcnno’s lecturette is a neat way of spreading the
gospel among the heathen, and it makes the
cynical wisdom of a Marshall-Hall look very sel-
fish. Poet Hall doesn’t try to be in sympathy
with the poor, ignorant world that has never
studied the construction of harmonies, but the
broader-minded expert cries, ‘ Come unto me to get
the hang of the proceedings.’ He takes the
orchestra to pieces, desires each piece to stand up
in turn and give an illustration of its own parti-
cular sound ; then he refixes the complicated
machine a bit at a time until it is in full melody
again. The lecturer’s one fault is a good one. He
errs on the side of simplicity, not even forgetting
to mention that the big drum parchment can be
tightened to sharpen its tone.
“ After the conductor had talked for about 20
minutes, Elsie Hall and the orchestra gave a
beautiful performance of the languorous Chopin
concerto in E Minor. Nobody who heard fragile,
spectacled, spinster Elsie in this work will believe
that the pianoforte part was ever more tenderly
treated, or more artistically supported by an
orchestra. A German or French audience would
have stood upon its boots to applaud when
Scherek was shaking hands with the meek spirit
at the conclusion of her task. The Australians
merely sat where they were and took a lot
of credit to themselves for giving sister Elsie a
double re-call. Later on she soloed in Liszt’s
arrangement of the Mendelssohnian Wedding
March, and put a jewelled coronet on her previous
success. If Miss Hall were a Potteresque siren
with a come-kiss-me cast of countenance what a
rapidly-triuinphant pianist she would be ! Her
academic saintliness has a charm, it is true, and a
charm that grows on the thoughtful public. But,
alas ! the average public doesn’t think.
“ Marshall-Hall’s tribute to the memory of bis
late secretary, Ernest Moffitt, is a splendid ex-
ample of artistic reproduction, and contains some
convincing specimens of Moffitt’s cleverness and
versatility in black-and-white. There are some
striking woodcuts on the cover and title-page, by
Lionel and Norman Lindsay, and the printing is
perfect. Unfortunately, Marshall-Hall has ren-
dered the thing rather ridiculous by some charac-
teristic blither over the drawings. Anyone would
think Hall was criticising Michael Angelo, C. D.
Gibson, and Phil May all rolled into one. Mofiitt
could draw, but the Prof.’s soulful gush over the
‘graceful arabesques’ of leafy foliage and ‘the
silver iridescence ’ of a very commonplace sketch
tempts critics to go to the other extreme. By the
way, Moffitt’s really best work has no place in the
book. He was one of those artists who shine
most when they take the least care. Sitting in a
cafe with an ink-bottle within reach Moffitt would
throw (iff caricatures of all within eyeshot—first-
class, biting portraits, exaggerated scarcely at all,
but hitting off all the character of the original in
a few dashing strokes. In those sketches—mostly
on the margins of newspapers or backs of bills-of-
fare—Moffitt showed marvellous originality.
“ All the world over, it seems, identical manner-
isms draw attention to the hayseed in the curls of
country cousins. An illustration is the way in
which Harry Conor’s ‘Stranger in New York’
nudged the society belle to whom he had just been
introduced. Not a century ago, scfme N.S.W.
landed heiresses were sometimes invaded in their
way-back parental home by visiting Sydney beaux,
with an eye to profitable matrimonial invest-
ment. But the boldest wooer quailed when he
found that not one of the tall, taciturn sisters was
ever accessible ‘on her own.’ They always sat
in a row of four and when the would-be suitor
ventured a remark about the weather, his hearers
en masse turned aside their heads, tittered self-
consciously and elbowed one another in throes of
bitter-sweet bashfulness.
“ Local prodigies are, apparently, not especially
called upon to rise up and call their parents
blessed. One fond father impetuously threw up
his billet in his joy at the revelation of his
chee-ild’s gift. Another budding genius is closely
connected with a title—made in Germany. Natur-
ally, an aristocrat, foreign or otherwise, is high-
handed in her ways. And when such a one comes
in contact with the prodigy’s committee (mostly
petticoated)—Heaven help the prodigy !
“Of old, ‘Dick’ was thought an ill-omened
name, while ‘William’ always turned out well.
Nowadays, the virtue seems to have gone out of
the surname, ‘ Williams.’ Lately, within a few
days of one another, a youth of that clan suicided
(Melb.), and a namesake was shot dead in Sydney.
Not long ago, the death of a well-known Dr.
Williams (Melb.) from excessive injections in-
volved a chemist in a charge of manslaughter.
Also, ‘ Williams ’ proved an unlucky alias for
Deeming, who as ‘ Baron Williams ’ committed
the crime which led to his detection.
“ Lady Brassey, it is interesting to know, is a
stickler for ‘ form ’in the ball-room. ‘ She favors
the low Queen Anne curtsey, and a correct version
of the Quadrille. ’ They fell into the ways of
error since Earl Hopetoun’s kitchen style, and
now sassiety mesdames are trying to make up for
lost time, by engaging a fashionable trainer to put
them through their paces. Private classes are in
vogue at all the ‘ best ’ houses. At the recent
Hunt ball the gorgeously-attired teacher stood
mid-way between two of the first sets, and gave
sotto-voce directions at critical points.
“ A dare-devil sport, who has been more court-
plastered, splinted, and re-ribbed than any wretch
ever taken into our special Sawbones’ establish-
ment for repair, is wooing the first huntress of
Melbourne, the niece of a bygone Diana. Every-
one anxiously awaits the crisis. ‘He has nine
lives, like a cat,’ said the eminent surgeon Fitz-
gerald. He has had a love for each, from all
accounts, but the present one is understood to be
the only serious engagement he has ever tackled
with anything like apprehension.
“ The late Mrs. Fitz Gibbon, whose sad death
(the result of a burning accident) called forth much
Melb. sympathy for poor old Fitz.in his bereave-
ment, was reputed to be a wealthy woman. Long
before the White Knight had divided his name
into two vvords, he was known as the husband of
Mickey Dawson’s daughter. Like Henry VVrixon,
who married into the Money Miller family, Mr.
Fitzgibbon had studied for the Bar—but, in the
words of Major Pendennis, “ a wife with a good
income is a doosid sight easier profession than the
Law.”

(N.S.W.) has had but four incumbents. Marsden

Lady Forrest’s book on Westralian Abori-

Gov. Lamington squeezes in among the
possibles as Gov.-General of the Australian Com-
monwealth. The lever in Lamington’s case is
that lie was five years private secretary to
Prime Minister Salisbury.
Graham Berry entered his 78th year, the
other day. It is now 39 years since he entered
Vic. Parliament, and 29 since he became Treasurer
in Macpherson’s 6-months Ministry. He was 30
years of age when he arrived in Australia.
Norton’s pamphleteer, Gilbert Probyn

Probyn, V.C., Comptroller to the coming K.’s
household. He it was who raised the famous
*’ Probyn’s Horse ” company, to suppress the
Sepoys.

port, Works Minister in the first S.A. Ministry
under responsible Govt., formed by Finniss, in
1856. In those days, Davenport, now a successful
vigneron, used to ride 25 mile 3 to-and-fro every
day to attend the meetiugs of Parliament.

In a recent celebrated N.S.W. prosecution
the accused obviously had not drawn upon his
laundry-bag. “What! Still wearing the same
shirt i asked an unceremonious friend. “Well,
you see,” explained the accused, “I am so busy
washing the other fellow’s dirty linen that I’ve no
time to attend to my own.”
Frank Ward, the S.A. colt-breaker who
had a habit of falling off things on to his head,
and who, a few months ago, was only saved from
hanging for murder by the persistence of his
counsel, has been already passed on from gaol to a
lunatic asylum. And yet the chief Govt, medical
expert was certain the man wasn’t mad !

of the P.C. judicial committee, is not alone paid
at a lower rate than most other Australian Chief
Justices, but is paid less than most of Aus-
tralia’s puisne Judges. He receives ,£2000;
C.J s in Q., N.S.W., and Vic., .£>3500; puisne
Judges in N.S.W., £2,600 ; in Vic., £3OOO.
Arthur L. Windsor, who, to use his own
words at the recent opening of the Age office, is
“as unknown to the general public as the Llama
of Thibet is to his subjects,” has sat in the
editorial chair of that wily newspaper for close on
30 years. Recognising his long and valuable ser-
vices David Syme has increased the endowment of
the said chair.
Chief Justice Way and other leading local
greasers—that is the only word—made a tre-
mendous fuss the other day over Gov. Tennyson’s
“munificent” gift of original MSS. of the late
laureate to Adelaide Public Library, but these
turn out to be only a portion of a mediocre set of
verses in adulation of Queen Victoria—Tennyson’s
first effort as Poet Laureate.
They have just renovated the monument
erected in ’7B, at Mansfield, Vic., to the three
police—Sergeant Kennedy, and oonstables Scan-
lan and Lonegan—killed there by the Kelly gang.
MTntyre, a fourth policeman, whom the gang
held as a prisoner and who was unarmed, jumped
on Kennedy’s horse when the owner fell off, and
escaped. He was so much scared, however, that
he did little work afterwards.
Many Southern acquaintances of his firmly
believe that Harry Ricketson, now under arrest in
Queensland for shooting a kanaka, would never have
used a pistol had he not been overwhelmingly
beset. He ever showed himself a man of iron
nerve and strong as a horse ; also he was one of
the very foremost amateur boxers of Australia, a
pupil of black Mollineaux in Melbourne, and,
writer fancies, also of Larry Foley.
Robert Walsh, Q.C., Crown Prosecutor at
Melbourne, who died the other day, was son of a
Dublin button-and-trimmings merchant, and in
the early fifties was co-partner with the late J. H.
(Judge) Dunne in Ballarat Times. Walsh and
Dunne were also partners as lawyers and made
loads of money in the goldfields, and lost it all.
Walsh first went to Melbourne to become Attor-
ney-General in C. G. Duffy’s Ministry, 1871.
Ex-Judge Windeyer is not alive to soothe
his soul over the present tribulations of Premier
Reid. G.H.R., when struggling with the Abigail
case before Windeyer J., received a gratuitously
gross reminder from the bench. “I am not likely
to forget your Honor’s remarks. I repeat, lam
not likely to forget them.” Then in his topmost
key : “ I am not likely to forget the quarter from
which they come ! ” It was an Australian Star
reporter, and no other scribe, who recorded the
incident. Not long afterwards Reid (no doubt to
the Judge’s great astonishment) became political
king of N.S.W., and Judge Windeyer was
deluged with Dean. The Judge, who urgently
wanted a long holiday, only got six months leave
of absence, and even that only on the understand-
ing that his resignation would date from the end
of it. When the term was up the resignation was
duly cabled to avoid a test vote in Parliament.
Anthony Bennett Robinson, the Melb.
Age commercial editor, who is going to see Lon-
don after 40 years’absence, began newspaper work
on the Times. In ’7O, when John Curtain, F. C.
(now Speaker) Mason, Carlton Brewery Milne,
Brewer P. J. Martin and Michael O’Grady
bought Melbourne Herald from poor old stone-
mason Win. Forsyth, Robinson was brought in as
general manager. Queerly enough he succeeded
another Robinson, an old Radical politician, but
no relative. A. B. Robinson (who had been elec-
tion-agent for John Curtain at Carlton election)
made sweeping alterations in the Herald office.
The stonemason had nearly pulled the paper to
the ground ; Robinson built it up. His first act
was to raise the screws of all the literary staff, and
before a year every man who was worth it had
got two or three “ rises ” without asking for them.
Robinson was a newspaper office Czar. How
David Syme and the Age will manage to rub
along without him can only be surmised.
Archibald Michie, Q.C., who died the
other day, used to tell this story about Melbourne
Herald, when it was a morning paper and he was
proprietor, in the early fifties. The ponderous
Dr. Evans was editor, but Michie himself used to
write the Monday’s leader. He lived out at
St. Kilda, and a messenger was sent for it
every Sunday night. The messenger was a
well-known character, and very fond of beer.
He was allowed half a-crown ’bus-fare, but it
was suspected that he walked one or both
ways, and “melted” the silver in wayside
pubs. Well, one Sunday night, it was get-
ting late, and the leader hadn’t arrived, and, •
to make things worse, the editor had gone
home, relying on Michie’s stuff turning up as
usual. The overseer was in a succession of fits
when, at midnight, a policeman in uniform
walked in, and said the editor was locked up in
Swanston-st. watch-house, for drunkenness.
But the overseer didn’t worry over that —it was
theleading article he was troubled about. “ Ob, as
far as that goes,” sez Constable Fogarty, “ Oi’ve
got that right enough—Oi found it on him whin Oi
sarched him. Here’s the layder,” sez he, “ an’ it’s a
pretty hot one, too, aboutKerrand the corporation.”
The overseer snatched the parcel from the trap,
ran his eye down the first few slips, cut it up,
bawled out “ Copy on the file ! ” and the paper
“ went down ”in time, with the leader in it. The
messenger had got drunk on his ’bus-money after
leaving Michie’s, and was “run in.”
He had brains enough left to try to bluff
the police by telling them he was editor
of the paper, and that he was going to
the office with his leader, but the thing
didn’t quite work out.
John Bellew Graves (brother of
Graves, M.L.A., Vic.) killed lately cycling
in England at the age of 77, arrived in Sydney
in 1836, and was one of the little band that opened
up Port Phillip in 1839.
The Vic. public libraries (“mechanics’
institutes ”) have just received their periodic in-
stalment of Gov. Brassey’s contributions. It con-
sists of a handsomely-printed vol. of the Governor’s
speeches and a reprint of H.E.’s articles on naval
matters. A quaint feature of the first-named is
the vigorous sub-editing to which it has been sub-
jected after printing. More than half the pages
—sometimes as many as half-a-dozen in one place—
have been cut out, while there is hardly a page
without a thick ink-splash through some redun-
dant remark. Most of the excised speeches were
those made at —er country shows, and the
probability is that the book was primarily intended
for private circulation. The precious parcel was,
it may be added, franked through the post.
The illustrious name of an official who left
his employers to lament his loss, still adorns an
East Melb. public building. In fact, Melbourne
fairly bristles with foundation-stones recording the
names of various ephemeral notorieties who
flourished in the boom-times. Amongst them
appear the names of a lot of ex-millionaires, busted
land boomers, cronk secretaries, men who are still
wanted, and some who have got off, with others
who have done their little bit of time. It would
be good if a stonemason would go round with a
chisel and erase unpleasant records that merely
provoke nasty remarks. Almost the only name
that recalls any pleasant memories is that of
Justice Redmond Barry, at Vic. Public Library.
Vic. Clerk of Leg. Assembly, W. V. Robin-
son, lecturing at Hawthorn (Melb.), recently,
pithily sketched some of the departed politicians.
Of Higinbotham, he “doubted whether his com-
peer was to be found even in the Imperial Parlia-
ment,” He usually began in a low tone of voice,
but, as he warmed with his subject, his voice
became like the magic that “surged the blood of
him who heard,” and “ stirred hearers to valorous
action and mighty deed.” Of Graham Berry, the
lecturer said, “If Berry was on the scaffold, with
the vvhite cap on his head, he could persuade a
mass meeting that the executioner was the
criminal.” The late J. G. Francis once remarked,
turning over the pages of a former Hansard,
“ that he could not speak like that now.” “ But,”
caustically observed Clerk Robinson, “he never
had, for the reporter’s duty was to put the
speaker’s remarks into consecutive English ; in
short, to preserve the idiom but destroy the
idiot ”
At David Syme’s jamboree over the open-
ing of the new Age office, Editor Windsor came
out of his shell to announce his hatred of notoriety
—not assumed in his case, for few people who fre-
quent the office have ever seen the brilliant jour-
nalist, and nine out of ten of those usually take
him for the janitor. A few years ago, a new
reporter finished three months in the office with-
out coming into contact with the editor. One
night he was marked for a Trades Hall meeting,
and returning about ten was writing his stuff up
when a quiet-looking shabby old man sauntered in
and observed, “I want you to report that state-
ment about the railway workshops in full. Kindly
give the speeches of each of the delegates in the
first person, and make the boy-labor question
prominent.” The scribe looked up, tried hard to
remember the speaker at the meeting he had just
left, and finally concluding he was some over-
looked notoriety-hunter with an axe to grind, re-
torted, “ Thanks, very much. When I want
your advice, I’ll ask for it.” Then the old man
chuckled and left the room, while the reporter
went on, determined to leave out the boy-labor
aspect altogether. Then the sub-editor grinned
through the little communicating window and ex-
plained matters. Windsor enjoyed the joke
largely, reassured the now apologising reporter,
and returned to his room prouder than ever of
his obscurity.

George Lansell, the Bendigonian deep-
sinker, had his 76th birthday the other day. The
old warrior still takes the most active part in
managing his mines, and bustles about the Stock
Exchange. What he has done for the district is
naturally admired, but his personal popularity has
not bounded up. He married in his old age, and
has now six young children. On his 76th birth-
day he bought for each of them a life governorship
of the Bendigo Benevolent Asylum.

Sydney, who still has

WIDOW CASEY : “ Father, poor man... is

Re the par. in a recent Bulletin (on the
authority of J. W. Mackail) that Tennyson once
contemplated emigration to Australia, it is in-
teresting to recall the fact that the Pre-Raphaelite
painter, Holman Hunt, at one time had similar in-
tentions. Here the authority is undoubted.
Ruskin says (“Modern Painters,” Vol. V., p.
380): “ For sheer want of bread we brought the
question to the balance of a hair whether the
most earnest of our young painters should give
up his art and go to Australia, or fight his way
through neglect and obloquy to the painting of
the Christ in the Temple.” Some industrious
person might compile a list of the eminent men
who nearly emigrated to Australia. Note, in this
case, Ruskin’s taking for granted that Holman
Hunt’s emigration necessarily involved giving
up his art altogether.
Lady Denison, whose death in England
was reported a few days ago, was present at the
opening of the first N.S.W. Parliament under
Responsible Government. She was a bright, chatty
woman at that date, given to mild satire on
colonial sooiety, but tactful to a degree. Her
husband and her young family held most of her
thoughts, and she often privately complained of
the romping of visitors to Government House as
a disturbing intrusion. Denison himself was a
heavy-witted man, with much of Brassey’s por-
tentous gravity, who loved his wife and family
better than all Australia, and whose chief recrea-
tion was writing yard-long despatches to one or
another member of the British Cabinet. No
occupants of Govt. House led a more domestic
and happy family life than did Denison and his
wife, and when Denison got into trouble with
Ministers—as he frequently did —Lady Denison
and he put their heads together and planned a
way out.

General Remarks.—ln accordance with that law by
which nature is against exclusiveness, it being a plan of
the Eternal that everywhere shall there be mutual giving
and taking, so do we to-day especially find this exempib
fied in the isobaric lines. For yonder over south-western
Australia a new anticyclonic or high pressure system
from the Indian Ocean is “feeding” the south-western
side of the depression “Vinitius," the south-eastern
edge of which in its turn is connected with the south-
western quadrant of the anticyclone at present existing
over the Tasman Sea. The centre of the depression is
about 1,100 miles south from Streaky Bay, and there the
leaden billows will be rolling, ships labouring and alba-
tross and whale-birds all in their glory. The winds along
our own coast will still be fresh from south-east, but will
moderate ere long, and a spell of that soft genial
weather thereafter obtain which is so character-
istic of the Queensland coast during the winter and
spring months. Even at this season some disrepute
appears to attach to our balmy climate in the southern
colonies among those who are ignorant of climatological
facts. The heat is supposed to be enervating, and the
climate depressing. Just as famous politicians and
obscure individuals acquire a shaded reputation which
year in and year out clings Io them in the minds of the
shallow without the slightest right or justification, so is
our climate belittled by people who do not know. We
are glad to inform the Australasian public that climato-
logical synopses of this northern colony, thoroughly
typical of all districts, and embracing the last 12 years,
are in course of preparation for publication, supplement-
ing the monthly statistics already issued, and containing
full information as to temperature, humidity, and other
elements. Then should unjust stigmas be removed, and
the truth be better known, than which there is no higher
religion.

Aboriginalities.
A cocky little N.S.W. country professional
man, whose boast it has ever been that he’d like
to see the joker who could take him down, was
recently taken out in a buggy by a designing
“ friend,” who managed to get him “glorious” at a
wayside pub. As soon as the beery one was dead to
the world his friend called in the doctor, who band-
aged-up Buggins’ head, and put a leg and an arm
in splints. When B. awoke from his drunken
sleep the friend who owned the buggy was ready
with “Don’t speak, old man; the doctor says
you’re to keep perfectly quiet; you’ve smashed up
the horse and buggy, and yourself into the bar-
gain. Not a word, now, while I send for the
doctor.” B. begged hard for a drink, but was
sternly refused on the ground of danger of inflam-
mation, and had to lie and suffer while half the
town was brought to laugh in its sleeve at him.
He can wear a smaller hat, now !
• * * *
Complaints had reached Sydney of the
booziness of the man in charge of a small Northern
P. and T. office, and an inspector, new to the
locality, arrived at the place on a Saturday after-
noon, found no one in the office, and asked a man
who came staggering out of the adjacent pub.
where he could find the Postmaster. The beery
one replied that he was that official, and incon-
tinently challenged the stranger to mortal combat;
whereupon the scared inspector mounted his bike
and fled with the p.m. in hot pursuit, flourishing
a knife and yelling that he. wanted to cut the
fugitive’s throttle ! The inspector, however,
soon pedalled himself out of sight, and now
there’s an ex-p.m. the more.
* * * •
Not so long ago the shearer used to turn
up at his shed with a couple of horses—his hack
and his “pack.” Now it is only a rare one, in
N.S.W. anyhow, who has a horse at all. Of 18 at
a western shed recently, three “footed ” it, eight
came by coach, and the rest had bikes. One S.A.
denomination which sends four “missionaries ” to
the shearing-sheds and stations in the north of
that province and in the West Darling has given
up horseflesh altogether ; henceforward the Word
is to travel per wheel. Also, most A.W.A.
organisers are to travel that way, too—they found
last season that the experiment answered splen-
didly. Poynton, the shearers’ man, who landed
an easy first in the fight for the Parliamentary
seat for the huge Flinders district, looked up the
scattered voters on the whirling wheel—and has
given the bike-agent a testimonial accordingly.

Dear Bulletin. —“ Nell Cook’s” state-
ment about emus starving through not being able
to get through rabbit-proof fence doesn’t square
with fact that the Cobar district squatters have

* * * *
“ Shaker ” : Anybody know anything
about the “standards” (for want of a better
name) writer, when a lad, used to see carried
by the Maranoa (Q.) blacks in travelling to and
from their great periodical gatherings ? Said
standards were two in number, one being exactly
like a barber’s pole without the stripe, the other
like a 501 b. bag of flour, painted dull yellow and
transfixed lengthwise by a spear. When the
blacks were in temporary camp these insignia
were always stuck upright in the ground just out-
side camp, and about 30ft. apart. Have
never seen anything similar in other parts of Aus-
tralia.
* * * •
Dear Bulletin, — As to the dimensions of
carpet snakes (8., 22/5/’99). In ’B5 I bought in
Cooktown the skin of a carpet (or diamond) snake
which measured (dried) 23ft. Bin. The vandal
who shot it (at “ the Normanby,” second section
Maytown railway) had cut the head off. In reply
to enquiries from me, Prof. M‘Coy, of Melb.,
said: “We have no record of so large a snake
having been killed in Australia before. As a
specimen it would probably be worth 10s. a foot
if complete, but without the head it is scientifi-
cally valueless.” I was foolishly persuaded to
lend the skin for exhibition at the Indo-Colonial
Exhibition, and upon applying for its return was
curtly informed that “the skin had been de-
stroyed by ants.” Now, as I had cured it
thoroughly with arsenic and kerosene, I knew this
was untrue, but what I feel most sore about is
that if I am ever rash enough to mention my big
snake, people look at each other and grin.— Geo.
H. Brewster, White Cliffs.

The N.S.W. supply of ironbark timber, it
is stated, is running-out so rapidly that unless
export thereof is prohibited there will in a few
years be none left. The railway department has
already been forced to use other and inferior
woods for sleepers.
“Kirriwina”: The best fishing-
grounds in Papua are in the Trobriand
Islands, 120 miles or so north of Samarai,
B N.G. (Note. —B.N.G. usually denotes
to the layman British New Guinea ; but
its correct translation is : Blooming Nude
Gins and Blessed Nice Girls). The Tro-
briands are termed by Samaraites as the
New Japan or Jack Want group. Why
the latter appellation I don’t know.
Mam bare River, 26th June, ’99.

manager who had just left a far N.W.
township, and when everybody had

the gins speak verra highly o’ him, at
onyrate.”

Dear Bulletin. The following
may throw some light on the vexed
butcher-bird question : “Of the 1 butcher-
bird ’ there are 11 varieties: Magpie,
black magpie, hill crow shrike, grey mag-
pie, blaok butcher-bird, brown do.,
butcher-bird proper, black-chinned do.,
collared do., gray do., white-winged do.”
This list, it will be observed, includes
the so-called “ magpies,” which are
ornithologically “ shrikes.” —W.H.E.
• * * *
“ G. 8.” : Recently passing a selec-
tor’s place near Jundah, I was told by one
of the daughters that the old man and ma
were away on a three months’ spell. The
two girls (15 and 17) ride the fences, bring
the sheep into water, and see travelling
stock through the run. I gave them a
hand to pull about a dozen bogged sheep
out of a square well (they called it a tank).
Two of these were badly cancered, so the
elder girl shore and then killed them.
“ Billyram ” : Re Women Workers: As
there was plenty of timber on the N.S.W. selection
fib for “short-length sawn stuff,” the old man got
an ancient portable engine, and started a saw-mill.
“ Dad” handled the sawn-stuff as it came from the
mill and carted it to the railway; “ Billy ” felled
and carted the logs to the mill, and the three
girls did the rest. They were up at daylight to
get breakfast, and to fire and water the boiler. One
drove the engine and stoked, and the other two
handled the logs on the saw-bench, and between
times wheeled away the saw-dust.
* * * *
“ Alia ” : Re ’roo-fights. Years ago, in
New England, I once saw a pitched battle between
two 6ft. “old men.” They were surrounded by
about 50 other ’roos, apparently not much inte-
rested in the proceedings, and these made off on
my approach. The combatants, however, allowed
me to get within 20 paces of them, and, for 20
minutes or so, I watched a better “ scrap ’’ than
many a man has paid a guinea to see. They
dodged and ducked, struck flat-handed blows you
could hear rods away, and developed great agility
in keeping clear of the dangerous hind-foot. Not
till they had to pull up for wind did they become
aware of my presence and make off.

“ Bendee ” : “ G.iVL” (8., 29/7/’99) doubts
whether the native bear ever drinks. Saw one at
a water-hole once, but he didn’t seein to be used
to the business—drank like a fowl, throwing the
head up to swallow each mouthful, which he
appeared to chew vigorously. Looked very funny.
Puff your cheeks well out, and try to smile and
chew at the same time, and you’ll get a fair idea
of the koala’s expression during the swallowing
process. “G.M.” pronounces it “k0.01a,” but
“ koola ” in a great many N.S.W. and Q. tribes
means “anger” or “angry.”
* * * »
Up North, the other day, when the re-
mains of a three-years-defunet Chow were ex-
humed for the usual transmission to China it was
found that the new clothes and boots with which
the corpse had been rigged-out were in wonderful
preservation, but the bottle of best brandy which
had been placed in the coffin had vanished. A
local undertaker has since been very eloquent
about the destructive effects of noxious gases on
glass.
* * • *
At Black Hill, Ballarat, the other day,
while a number of workmen were taking down
the poppet-legs of the Old North
Queen, one of the huge pulley-
wheels started down the declivity
“ on its own,” and continued its
wild career until it struck a house,
into which it cut with the facility
of a buzz-saw ripping into a
cheese. The wheel went slap
through an out-house and two
rooms, and fetched up in the
drawing-room, where it was found
reclining against the ruins of the
piano. Several people were in the
house, and there were interesting
escapes of the narrowest kind.
Which recalls to writer a sight seen
in his youth at Mt. Cole (Vic.)
when a jinker-wheel chased an old
man down a track, and when within
a few feet of him, and apparently
about to split him like an apple,
jumped him as' clean as a dispensa-
tion of Providence, and went
scooting merrily down the mount.

melal-rules
For to punctuate the language of McNulty 1
When McNulty caine up country he went out to Seven-
wire
Where he got a job at hauling logs from hungry Mclntyre
(Him as wouldn’t work on Sunday if his homestead was
a-fire!).
He could drive a team of bullocks, could McNulty.
But Mclntyre’s old bullocks they were hungry as was he ;
Like them lean and lanky cattle in the Bible yarn, may
be.
Anyway, they couldn’t pull a log and ’twas a sight to see
How they shivered at the language of McNulty?
“ Why the (blank dashed parenthesis) and (starred ellip-
sis) hell
Don’t you pull ? You sons of (asterisks) 1 ” And here his
accents fell.

You’re a mob of (blanky) oows! ” exclaimed M'Nulty.
At this gross and brutal insult every bullock gave a heave,
And they hauled that blessed log out just as Mao. had
turned to leave.
“ I’m a champion ox-persuader, with some notions up my
sleeve,
And I knows the power o’ language 1 ” said M'Nulty.
But M'Nulty went to Sydney, where he drove a parcels
van,
And the suburbs got to know him just like Coonabara-
bran.
Took a load of apples over to a North Shore grocer man :
’Twas the only time that language failed M'Nulty 1
You must know “ the Shore,” how steep it is; you’ve
climbed the hills, no doubt ?
Well, M'Nulty led his horse up ; there were plenty folks
about;
And the blessed tail-board came unfixed, and let them
apples out—
A catastrophe unnoticed by M'Nulty 1
But a crowd was there a-waiting on the summit of the
hill „ , ,
In the hope of hearing language that would make em
fairly thrill.
When McNulty found what happened for a second he
stood still: .

And l am a champion swearer —but I candidly confess
That I can find NO language as would properly express
What my feelin’s is this minute 1 ” said McNulty.

Dear Bulletin. —Re foundering of Union
Co.’s s.s. Ohau (B. 19/8/’99). In a lumber-loaded
ship once, from Vancouver to Callao, we had deck-
load on main-deck and for’ard half of poop, with
passage left in midships of latter to get to saloon
and cabins. Got a snorting hurricane near the
Line, and, in heaving-to, shipped a terrific sea
that filled her fore and aft, lifted the main-deck
load, washed the chocks from under, blocked the
scuppers, ripped off the cleats and uprights in
passage of poop-loading, so that the whole caboodle
went over to starboard with a heavy lurch. Made
sure it was a case of “ turning turtle,” but, with
luck and hard graft, we pulled through, and at
the subsequent official inquiry, unanimously
blamed the deck-load.—C. Potteb.
One of the most wonderful inventions of
the age is Willson’s discovery of crystalline carbide
of calcium. There is little doubt that acetylene
gas is about to revolutionise artificial lighting.
As with all successful patents, a host of pirates
have started up to try and rob Willson of his
rights. However, the recent decision of the High
Courts at Leipsic, Germany, will somewhat damp
the ardour of Willson’s opponents. It has been
decided that Willson is undoubtedly the discoverer
of crystalline carbide of calcium. Following up
(his success, the English Acetylene Company have
issued a batch of writs against infringers, and the
Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd., of 36 Pitt-
street, Sydney —who own the Willson patents for
.Australasia —are getting ready to give the Aus-
: ralian pirates a surprise. Thisco. have now fitted
up some hundreds of installations around Sydney,
and are prepared to give estimates for any kind of
acetylene installations. Write direct to 36 Pitt-
street, and beware of infringers.*

* * • •
The city bushman swears by saltbush, of
course; a good many of him seems to think there
isn’t much other worth considering in the vast
way-back. But the way-baok man is not so cock-
sure about it. Some of it is good, he admits ;
but in other big districts -in nearly all the Dar-
ling country, for instance—it is going, and he is
not half so cut-up about it as the city/bushman
would think he ought to be. The fact is that the
saltbush of the far-inlands will keep sheep alive
and little more; the old hand who has studied
the business scorns the idea that it is either fat-
tening or good for fleece-making.

“Old Soldier ” on the missionary busi-
ness :
Re the Moran missionary-shindy. The secular person
who, caring nothing for dogma, has had opportunity for
observing the results of mission-work, never t liink's much
of it. To begin with, a dead-set is made at. polygamy,
which, to the noble savage, makes all the difference be-
tween want and plenty. He hunts : ajid;fights ; his wives
cultivate and housekeep. And, the more wives there are,
the lighter is the work for each. The missionaries want
the man to do the hoeing, but he dare not, even if he:
would. The women would drive hitn out of the village.
Then, when the young man begins to know his Bible, he
mostly wants to know why the patriarchs could have
such a lot of wives while he is only to have one, arid I
never heard of a missionary who could do more than
shuffle with that question. The children at mission-
schools learn a few useful, and a great many usele'ssif
things, and, when they leave school, the sharp* pupils get
billets of some sort, and the dull become practically
savages again—ptas a greater knavery and unreHableness.
And, all the time, Islam, working without organised
agencies, is quietly making 1-00 converts to Christianity’s
one ; the Mahometan proselytes, moreover, giving evi-
dence in their lives that their conversion is more than
nominal. The bulk of the missionaries are doubtless sin-
cere and earnest enough, but their whole system is
rotten.

“ Basalt ” : Be quality of bore-water
(“6x8,” B. 22/7/’99). The Richmond (M.L.) Govt,
bore gives splendid water ; while in some other
places the water is hardly drinkable. In summer
at early morning you can see a full stream of water
in the drain, but at evening you will see the stream
an inch lower with a white deposit on the margin
from which the water has fallen.

Dear Bulletin : Re leaky boats. The
owner of a ferry-boat up my way (Queensland)
finding that his boiler was beginning to leak, at-
tempted to repair it with a piece of wood. Some-
time later, a certificated mariner awoke in the
hospital and enquired who hit him. And that
boat in its time had carried thousands of passen-
gers.—Aramac.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

The advantages of employing the cheap
and nasty alien were strikingly illustrated recently
at Bundaberg. A cane-planter of local invention
was tested the other day and pitted to work
against 16 “boys.” The heathen were beaten
easily. Machine (cost <£2o) requires a man to
drive and a boy to stack rattoons. Strange to say,
the contrivance was the invention of the white
man, not of a Malayta cannibal.
Both Argus and Age managed to mis-
quote the passage from Longfellow’s “ The Build-
ing of the Ship ” with which Deakin perorated in
his speech at the Eederation banquet given in his
honor. Argus made the poet bid the ship itself
to “sail on to union strong and great ! ” whereas
the poet wrote, “Sail on U union, strong and
great! ” Also that paper made the first line,
“mighty ship of State,” whereas there is no
adjective in Longfellow. The Age turned the
line, “ Sail on nor fear to breast the sea,” into a
repetition of “ Sail on, O ship of State.” Curiously
enough, neither paper detected the quotation in
Gov. Brassey’s speech at all. The old man,
speaking of Deakin’s eloquence, exclaimed :
Bright is the ring of words
When the right man rings them.
The Argus printed “Right is the ring of words,”
but neither paper put in quotation-marks. The
lines are the beginning of R. L. Stevenson's 14th
poem in the volume of “ Songs of Travel.”

Sydney spieler just “going up ” for a stretch and
wanting tobacco worked-in to him :
Booby-hatch ; one moon. Send snout to Rattler.
An interesting and sweetly-printed little
document sent along by Regina Nagel to a Mel-
bourne friend is a programme of the last concert
at Marchesi’s school, punctuated with the sender’s
candid criticisms. Regina’s notes of admiration
and otherwise are about equally distributed
between the first and second year’s pupils, for
among the novices Mile. Elizabeth Parkinson
(from Kansas city) is summed up as “glorious, a
secotid Melba,” and a German soprano gets credit
for “grand dramatic voice.” Three of the more
matured Marehesians are either “ beautiful voice ”
or “ lovely artist,” but another appears to be
“ absolutely cruel.” As for the Nagel’s opinion of
her own pair of contributions it is “ better left
unsaid.” According to Paris press criticisms on
Marchesi’s concert, the rich contralto that used
sometimes to let itself go in “ Ben Bolt ” will stir
tiie feelings of the musical world presently^

Insurances remain !

MELBOURNIANA.


THE LATEST ADDITION TO THE FAMILY.


A leading shipping-authority spoke thus to
a Bulletin man : —Messrs. Ismay, Imrie & Co.,
the shipping firm so long famous for its splendid
transatlantic steam service, will shortly establish
direct steamship communication betvven Australia
and Liverpool, with their White Star fleet of mag-
nificent new steamers. The Medic, Runic, Afric,
Persic, and Suevic will each be of 12,000 tons, and
have accommodation for 80,000 carcases of mutton,
24,000 bales of wool, and 270 passengers. Punctu-
ality will be the constant rule ; and the trip from
Albany to Liverpool, calling at Capetown, a con-
tinental port, and London, will take 38 days.
This service will be invaluable to Australia, both
for produce and passenger trade. For, to the
Australian exporter it means that beef, mutton,
hares, rabbits, poultry, fruit, butter, &c., will be
carried promptly, at low cost, to the favorable
market proffered by the teeming populations of
the English Western Counties; whilst the perfec-
tion of the refrigerating chambers and cargo
accommodation generally, will ensure the landing
of the produce in proper condition, thereby
enabling the Australian producer to suc-
cessfully compete with the producers of
Europe and America. Great benefit, too, comes
to the wool trade of Australia by this innovation,
as the White Star transatlantic service can receive
the wool at Liverpool from the Australian service,
and ship it direct across the Atlantic for New
York, Boston, and Philadelphia. But it is in the
passenger trade that the White Star fleet inaugu-
rates the newest order. The business manage-
ment holds that the great mass of the Australian
people should be catered for; and therefore all the
passengers will be of one class, and the charge of
this Anthony Hordern of the sea will be according
to berths from £26 10s. to £34 return from
Sydney to London. The accommodation will be
on the middle deck, with large, airy berths, up-to-
date baths, smoking-room, reading-room, and
piano; and the long experience of the White Star
line in the Atlantic trade will be employed to
make the voyage as enjoyable as possible. The
bill of fare will be liberal and diversified, and
certain to make the democrat with a sea-appetite
happy inside during meals, and amiable during
intermissions,*

The Read Offices of the Company at the corner of Castjcreagh and Moore-streets, Sydney .
New Head Offices.
“ One of the finest buildings in. the city of
Sydney.” This undoubtedly- IST..a just de-
scription of the new Head Offices of the
Citizens’ Life, recently erected at the corner
of Castlereagh and Moore streets, and now
occupied by the Company. Of the purest
style of French Renaissance (so conspicuous
a feature of modern Paris) it is worthy of
the architect—Mr. Warded—to whose credit
stand two Australian Cathedrals. Erected
with a view to utility and convenience rather
than show, no stucco work or meretricious
effect offends the eye. Six stories high (ex-
clusive of basement and mezzanine floor) the
total floor space measures about 34,000 square
feet. Fifteen strong rooms are distributed
over the building. Within a stone’s throw of
the General Post Office, the position is a splen-
did one, and available space has let readily.
The Company requires the ground floor as a
public office, the major part of the firstfloor for
Board Room and general management offices,
also the fourth’knd fifth floors; the Head Office
clerical staff numbering upwards of sixty.
The Home of a Big Business.
Commencing business on the first day of
January, 1887, the Citizens’ claims in the
short space of 12 years to have accomplished
results without a parallel in the history of
Life Assurance the wide world over. Its
Policy-holders to-day exceed in number
200,000, and at the present time not far
short of two thousand policies are issued
weekly. Its invested funds, totalling almost
half-a-million, are being added to at the rate
of £lOO,OOO annually. Progress so wonder-
ful means that before very many years the
whole of the present buildings will be re-
quired by the Company for its own use.
“Look on this Picture and on
that,”
To exemplify the giant strides made, we
publish the salient figures of the Company’s
annual statements for the years 1887 and
1898 :
New Assurances
Premiums
Interests and Rents...
Claims (Death and
Endowments ma-
tured)
Funds
No. of Policies in force
Here surely we have an exhibit of wonderful
vitality and progress. The great Prudential
of London, the largest Life Office in the
British Empire, took 15 years to build up a
premium income of £lOO,OOO as compared
with the £236,000 of the Citizens’ (working
in a sparsely-populated territory) in twelve
years.
The Mission of the “ Citizens’.”
Until the advent of the “ Citizens’,” Life
Assurance was beyond the reach of all but
the wealthier sections of the Australian
Commonwealth.
To establish a society with a charter wide
enough to admit alike the humblest wage-
earner and the merchant prince was the life
work of the Founder of the Company—thelate
James P. Garvan—and in the “ Citizens’ ”
he has left an enduring monument to his
titanic labors. Over 10 years ago, in his
Chairman’s address, the Founder stated :
“ The good effects of Life Assurance on the
community at large consists more in the fact
of taking out a life policy than in the amount
of that policy.”
The “Citizens’” Lst year transacted a
larger purely Ordinary Business than any
Life Office in Australasia with but one ex-
ception, but the propagation of the prin-
ciples of Industrial Assurance has ever been,
and will continue to be, its chief care.
The Founder of the Company.
The Founder of the Company, as already
stated, was the late James P. Garvan, at one
time Colonial Treasurer, and at another
Minister for Justice for New South Wales.
For ten years (until his death in 1896) he
managed its affairs with consummate ability
and magnificent success, and it is pleasing to
note that the older officers of the Company
have decided to perpetuate his memory by
the erection of costly stained-glass windows
over the main entrance to the stately new
Head Offices. Mr. John J. Garvan has suc-
ceeded his father as Managing Director.
Prompt Payment Always.
Over 30,000 Claims Paid.
Over 30,000 claims by death of Assured or
maturity of Policies liav'e been paid by the
Citizens’. To pay its claims promptly is the
special pride of the Company and in thou-
sands of cases the Policy-moneys have been
actually handed over to claimants the same
day that intimation of death was given. The
Policies are practically free from all restric-
tions. Suicide or mis-statement of age does
not vitiate them. Members may, after assur-
ing, reside in any part of the habitable globe
without extra premiums being imposed.
During the current year claims have been
paid under policies on the lives of members
whose deaths took place in India, America,
South Africa, Great Britain, and “at ser ''
respectively.
Undoubted Security.
In 1895 the actuarial valuation of the Ordi-
nary Branch was conducted on a 3| per cent,
net premium basis, and that high standard
has been preserved ever since. The Citi-
zens’ was the first Australian Life to adopt
so stringent a valuation. In the Industria
Branch, no British office transacting similai
business has essayed, at an equally early
period in its career, to make so stringent »
valuation as does the Citizens’.
Annual Bonuses.
Annual actuarial valuations are made and
annual bonuses allotted to Ordinary Branch
with profit-policies. For the year 1898, the
reversionary bonuses declared on whole of
of life-policies were :
Policies ten years and upwards in force,
£2 10s. per cent. ; policies of five years and
under ten years’ standing £2 per cent., and
policies under five years in force £llls. per
cent, on the sum assured.
Purely Australian.
The Company transacts no business
outside of Australia, Tasmania, and New
Zealand, but has branch offices in each
capital city, and superintendencies and agen-
cies in every town of importance within the
sphere of its influence. Some years ago it
was found on investigation that 87 per cent,
of the lives assured were natives of the
colonies, and the Company claimed to have
a larger number of native-born policy-holders
on its books than any other office. At 31st
December, 1898, the risks in force were
distributed as follows :

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Albert Trott’s 200 wickets and 1000 runs in a
Single season of first-class cricket, combined with his ac-
knowledged front-rank fielding, stamp the ex-Australian
as the finest all-round cricketer in England. Trundlers
have often taken 100 wickets for 1000 runs, but none good
enough to account for 200 wickets could tot up four
figures in runs. W.G. is credited with 100 wickets and
2000 runs.
* • * *
Cricket: Re “ A.W.” (19/8/’99) and three-day test
matches : “The English system serves to make the game
more lively than we get it in Australia.” This is ques-
tionable so far as test matches are concerned. In the
three-day matches, if one side makes a big score, the
other only tries to keep its wickets up and make a draw ;
with an additional day’s play that move would be useless.
Barring wet weather, or an unusual collapse, no test-
match can be finished in three days.

* * •
At a N.S.W. country race-meeting a certain
jockey was suspected of intending to prevent the favorite
from winning, and one of the stewards was accordingly
deputed to post himself at the most likely spot to make
observations ; but by the time the race came off said
steward hil got slightly fuddled. The expected inter-
ference with the favorite took place, a protest was lodged,
and, when the steward was examined as to how far the
offending jockey was ahead when he crossed the favorite,
he gravely answered, “ Twenty minutes 1 ” —and stuck to
it, too 1
» • ♦ •
Poor old Newman, once first favorite for Melb.
Cup, is now battling for £5 prizes at unregistered subur-
ban meetings near Brisbane.
• • * •
Died lately at Auckland (M.L.), James
Kean, jockey, trainer, and horse-owner. Kean rode for
“ Honest John ” Tait and Edward Lee on Sydney side 30
years ago, and in ’7B went to M.L., where he was for a
time trainer to Governor Hercules Robinson. Amongst
many famous horses that have been through bis hands
may be mentioned Lady Emma, Pearl-Shell, Cuirassier,
Lottie, and that steeplechasing wonder, Silvio.
• • • •
The latest riding crank at ’Ome is the South
’Murkan jock, Malerba, who disdains saddles, and abso-
lutely scoffs at stirrups. The autocratic Jockey Club
lately ordered him to not only use a saddle but have
stirrup-leathers and irons attached thereto. Malerba,
without putting his feet in the irons, landed a 100 to 8
chance at Newmarket, and the natural consequence is
that “ fashionables ” at ’Ome will follow the cowboy till
they are broke.
* * ♦ •
The punters who follow Tod Sloan seem to be
getting hard knocks lately. Awhile back an American
E lunger made a start by winning £14,000 on one race,
ut “did it in,” and ended up the day £24,000 to the
bad.
* * * *
Australians in England unanimously agree that
in sport the old country is a hundred years behind the
times. Accommodation at most English cricket grounds
is not only far below that of Sydney and Melbourne, but
prices are three times as high. The same applies to
racing clubs. The starting-machine is still frowned at,
and on every course are to be seen delays at the starting
post of probably half-an-hour.
Memo, for the N.S.W. Treasurer. Victoria
thusly rakes in revenue and supplies a public want. The
result of every important racing, cycling, footballing,
and cricketing event is immediately sent to all telegraph
stations, and, by previously leaving his address and six-
pence, anyone can have the news delivered promptly at
his door within a mile of any telegraph office. The
revenue profits £7OO per annum by this device.
• * * *
At a fashionable Perth caravanserai it has been
the custom to fine anyone using slang at table a penny
for each word so used. The fund totalled 10s. at last, and
it was decided to invest it in a sweep on the G.N.
Hurdle. The winner was drawn and £45 divided among
the slangy.
* * • *
Re the Melbourne lady “ pony-gun.” When the
late Torn Challoner died in England his wife took charge
of his big training-stable, and trained horses with great
success for years. Writer knows of a lady member of a
Tas. racing family who habitually “ rides work,” and
who is always entrusted with the job of backing the
young ’uns ; clad in “ rationals,” right well she does it.
Bv the way, not a few Melb. racing ponies are owned by
“Flossies,” of course financed by “ mugs.”
* • * •
At A.J.C. annual meeting a motion to raise
minimum weight in flat racing to 7st.. was properly nega-
tived. To secure good jockeyship lads must serve a long
and rigid apprenticeship under thoroughly competent
trainers. Curious to note that the best of our jocks who
go ’Ome are failures, and never get good mounts,
whereas American cracks like Martin, Simms, and Tod
Sloan are “put up” by some of the cleverest cliques—a
pretty clear proof that Australian horsemanship at least
Is not reckoned much account in England.
* * * *
Writer always flattered himself that he could
pretty closely judge a man’s weight by his appearance,
especially when in the buff, but Sydney novice boxing
tourneys have flattened that notion out. One
with an 8.7 limit is a case in point. If at least a
dozen of the 27 entrants don’t tip the beam at over9st.
and some well up to 10st., then there’s nothing in height
and build as a medium for guessing weight. Promoters
don’t let such a trifling matter as half-a-stone or so stand
in the way of a big entry-list. An extra-clever little 'un,
giving away a lot of weight, often makes a fairly open
fight of it; this draws, and the draw’s the thing with
speculative boxing-promoters.
Middle-weight champion ’rastler of N.S.W..
Lorenz, now in Brisbane, talks of fighting' any light-weighs
in the flve provinces, if latter ean find a fair stake,
Lorenz may be a good fighter (and he’ll have to be to get
back any money he puts up), but, should it turn out 60,
it will be the first instance of a successful ’rastler suc-
ceeding under M.Q. boxing rules. Besides, Lorenz is a
German, and Germany has never been known to develop
a champion pug. No matter whom Lorenz fights, writer’*
money is on the boxer. Paste this tip in your hat.
-•. * • •
John L. Sullivan, Peter Jackson, Jim Corbett,
and Griffo are past examples of how brief the champion
pug’s “hour” is, and how soon “ the pace ” kills. The
whilom grizzly, oyclonio, man-eating Australian, Joe
Goddard, provides the latest instance. Going from bad
to worse for years, he capped everything in Chicago
recently by letting a comparatively unknown nigger
punch him into quitting under cloak of a palpably inten-
tional foul.

champion pug., received his son with open arms on liis
return to ’Frisco. “I do not uphold pugilism,” said
Parson Jeffries, “but I would rather see Jim the straight
hopetit fighter he is than a hypocritical preacher.” Tee-
totaller Jim, by the way, is announced to have lately
modified his principles as to wine-bibbing. “ I don’t
think,” he says, “ a man can fight without drinking in
moderation.” The dawn of a new ohampion 1

“ X ” : Know of two country football teams who
played a match over three times because either one side or
the other couldn’t fall in with the decisions of the referee.
Third.tinje they imported a referee, but as the game was
comparatively quiet- they didn’t scalp him when he
declared the result. But during play a giant forward
had careered with his head full-butt into the ref’s, bread-
basket. The latter landed 10ft. away, and during his
flight the whistle, still between, his teeth, was kept on a
continuous toot by the forced draught.

“Robbo”: What comes of having a parson at
the wheel ? A Sydney football referee was heard t'other
: day to caution a player for one small and solitary
“ Damn ! ” No wonder our champions are retiring !
* • • *
“Piripi” : These are the days of sport-loving
parsons. Rev. Mullineux is not 'unique. At, Auck-
land (M.L.) last month, Rev. Beatty, of the swagger St.
Mark’s (Anglican) church, won. the bowls championship
of Remuera Club, and Rev. Scott-West (Presbyterian)
secured Auckland Olub championship. A few years back
Methodist-parson Hodson was great in Rugby football
councils in Maoriland ; and Rev. James Hill, another
athletic parson, who died a few years back whilst
Bishop of Nigeria, West Afrioa, introduced Association
football to Auckland players. “ Not nearly so rough as
Rugby,” chirruped Rev. James in an uphill canvass among
players bound body and soul to Rugby “ footer.” At
last he got a couple of teams, and the inaugural match
took place in the presence of thousands of spectators,
the parson captaining one side. But, unfortunately for
the reputation of the Association game as a mild,
bruiseless sort of pastime, the rev. gentleman appeared
in his pulpit next morning with two undisguisabie black
eyes! In the roll of athletic soul-savers'in the island
province is Parson Calder, of Ponsonby, whose 10 or li
stone, of whipcord muscle used always to be trained to the
hour, and who was said to possess' the “ dirtiest left ”
known between Cape Maria Van Dieman, and Lake
Taupo. It was Parson Calder who obtained a glass of
whisky for Bishop Cowie in a ferociously-teetotal town-
ship one Sunday morning. “ How ever did you get it,
Mr. Calder?” queried the astonished though delighted
prelate. “ Told the publican that it was for you, me
lud ! ” replied the unabashed Calder.
• * * *
In an alleged copy of old-time Tasmanian
explorer Kelly’s log, printed in a Launceston
paper, it is stated that a single spring made by a
frightened blackfellow measured 33ft. Well, the judges
at the ancient Olympian Gaines passed a record of 55ft?—
but that was a classic lie.
* • * •
“Good Luck”: Re your illustration of Mr.
Arch. Forsyth’s pedomanieycle (or whatever he calls it)
I am pleased to see my prophecy so soon realised, and
hereby implore the patentee to stick to his parchment.
About five years ago I hawked the invention round
Sydney on the hunt for a financier—but only found scorn.
Several very learned cycle authorities said that the t heory
for increased speed was mechanically wrong, and used
much bad logic in consequence, whereupon I went back
and “putin” the sixty acres. Mr. Forsyth, or whoever
holds the patent, has a very big thing on; Lhe machine is
not only a long-felt want medically but also mechanically
for increased speed, and, incidentally, greater ease. The
rider will work it in a running attitude (left foot and
right, hand and vice versa), will only occasionally use the
saddle, and by utilising resistance (see sliding-seated
wager-boat) will break records and things like a flash
squatter in a tin pub. But the steering-bar is unneces-
sary ; the cranks will do for that and stop the familiar
wobble, too.

THE DARKIE IN THE TEAM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When all were men of British breed
All Hingland held their own :
In Cricket’s cause they did not need
To raise a foreign loan.
They ne’er complained of “ awful luck,”
Nor did their batsmen seem
To suffer from a want of pluck
Till the darkie joined the Team.
Year Ninety-six the precious “ Prinoe”
■ First paralysed their game ;
He starred, they lost —and, ever since,
They’ve mostly done the same.
For though he often makes a score
Whilst bright his eyeballs gleam,
They fail, as ne’er they failed before,
Now the darkie’s in the Team.
In Ninety-seven—Ninety-eight
We put them to the test:
They raised excuses for their fate,
And then they gave us best.
Four times we licked ’em out of five —
Yea, licked. All Hinudand’s “cream.”
For, how could they expect to thrive
. With a darkie in the Team 1
AH Hinglaud’s “ draws” of Ninety-nine
* Have merely gone to show
They should have drawn the oolour-line
. At Ranji., doneherknow.
Play well or ill, they cannot win,
They can’t keep up their steam :
The White Man’s Burden is the skin
Of the darkie in the Team.
Then, why not let “ his Highness” slide?
