Three Years a Dyspeptic.
A South Melbourne lady
tells a Reoobd reporter what
Bile Beans did for her.
Sufferers of this most dis-
agreeable and painful com-
plaint when asked the nature
of their sickness are fre-
quently heard to remark :
“Oh, it's only dyspepsia,”
as though this form of ill-
ness was only a very trivial
matter. Though it may be very common, this does not reduce its
seriousness. It may lead to most dangerous complications if
neglected, and the most serious diseases have their origin in it.
Few know better than Miss May Graham, 99 York-street West,
South Melbourne, of the awful suffering that accompanies this
terrible complaint, and the following is an account of her illness
given to a reporter of the .Record : “ For three years I suffered from
dyspepsia. I had frequent and fearful attacks of neuralgia in the
face, which caused agony for weeks at a time. All food disagreed
with me. I grew listless and melancholy. I spent six months in
South Gippsland for a change, but I got no benefit from it. I re-
turned to Melbourne, and became pale and sallow. I gave up hope
of recovery. One day my brother brought me a box of Bile Beans.
I commenced to take them, and in short time felt decidedly better.
Continuing with the Beans I was restored to perfect health. I can-
not too strongly express my gratitude for the good done me.”
FURTHER TESTIMONIALS.
Miss C. Mills, Charlton, N.S.W., states:—“My people have
derived great benefit from the use of Bile Beans ; in fact we would
not be without them here in the country.”
Mrs. Gannon, .Tindabyne, N.S.W., writes as follows :—“Please
find enclosed Is. 7d. in stamps, and forward me two more boxes of
your Bile Beans ; they are really good.”
The Approaching Hot Weather,
How to Keep Cool.
The summer season in Australia has started
rather early and vigorously this year, and the pre-
sent hot weather, coming suddenly as it does, has
an exceedingly enervating effect on the most of
us. It is as well to remember that at this season
of all others the human system needs to be toned
up and put in thorough condition to withstand
the trying severity of an Australian summer.
Bile Beans for Biliousness are the very best medi-
cine that can be taken for this purpose, as they
cleanse and regulate the liver, an organ to which
may be traced nearly all the minor ailments so
many people suffer from. If the liver is not in
thorough working order, the blood is impure, the
the circulation sluggish, the digestion impaired,
the sleep disturbed, and the complexion sallow.
The consequence is that there is a lack of energy,
and the sufferer becomes a victim of “ that tired
feeling.” To be out of sorts this hot weather is
most trying, as many people know. Be warned
then in time; take Bile Beans for Biliousness
NOW, and they will put you in a condition that
will withstand the most severe heat of the coming
season. Bile Beans are sold by chemists and
storekeepers generally, or sent direct from the
Australian Depot, 39 Pitt-street,. Sydney, post
paid, on receipt of the price in stamps or post
order, Is. l£d. per Large box.
A Valuable Discovery.
The nineteenth century, which is drawing to a close, has given
to the world a great number of marvellous things; but, perhaps,
the most valuable has been the discovery by an American medical
man of a certain natural vegetable substance that has the peculiar
properties of acting the same way in the process of digestion as
Nature’s own animal bile. The discoverer, realising the great value
of this vegetable substance, and the boon it would be to the millions
of digestion sufferers (who, according to the regular medical science
to date, were martyrs to pepsin, bismuth, starving, and purging),
decided to spare no expense to perfect this remedy, and make it a
cure for all digestive troubles and their attendant ailments. The
result of this experimenting was the addition of some eight other
ingredients, the same being titled l£ Bile Beans, a name given to
express exactly what the preparation was—“ a Bean for the Bile.”
The expense and care in perfecting and compressing this prepara-
tion to the size of a small bean has been very great, but the result is
a small oval bean that the smallest child can take with ease, and a
medicine that last year the consumption of which reached some
thirty millions of doses in Australia alone, the rich arid poor alike
being the friends of this marvellous specific. The consumption to
date has nearly baffled the best efforts of the proprietors, their
laboratories working night and day at times to meet the demand.
Bile Beans have been proved an undoubted cure for headache,
biliousness, costiveness, piles, liver trouble, bad breath, rheumatism,
influenza, indigestion, dizziness, buzzing in the head, fullness after
eating, lack of ambition, debility, female ailments, pimples, and a
host of other ailments that owe their origin to defective Bile flow,
assimilation, and digestion. They will also be of great service m
nervous disorders, loss of appetite, shortness of breath, blotches on
the skin, insomnia, and troubled sleep. They act quickly in re-
storing females to health, and for a general aperient and tonic
remedy they are almost unequalled. These Beans are placed on the
market in a form that anyone can take them without medical super-
vision ; and, as the price is so very low, there are few homes that
cannot afford to always have a box on the shelf for emergency.

Are Your Kidneys Weak?
HAVE YOU URIC ACID TROUBLE?
Have you ever considered what neglecting your
kidneys means ?
Have you ever realised the terrible risk you are
taking ?
Do you not pass on the streets, every day, men
and women whose countenances show that the
final stake is up and the die being cast?
How may you know them ?
By their pale, sallow, sunken, or bloated coun-
tenances ; dull, lack-lustre eyes; slow, languid
movements ; blotched, pimpled, unhealthy com-
plexions ; and general appearance of weakness and
debility.
In addition to their outward appearance, how
do they feel themselves ?
Weak, worn-out, tired, languid, restless,
nervous, irritable, worried over trifles. Feverish,
chilly, short of breath, dizzy, rheumatic, with
pain or dull ache in the back, neuralgic, sleepless,
hysterical, generally debilitated and used up.
They are neglecting their kidneys.
By carelessness in exposure to cold or heat, by
over-work, over-worry, by over-eating or over-
drinking, they have risked the substance of their
kidneys., Risked it and lost, for over-work wears-
out kidneys faster than nature can repair them.
Your kidneys are the most important organs of
your body.
You can repair, build up, cure them by using
Warner’s Safe Cure. It can be purchased of any
medicine vendor, and carries recommendations
from the leading physicians, chemists, and scien-
tific men from all parts of the globe.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Memo, for would-be-Premier Lyne :—A
party is known by the company it keeps.
The Constitution Act of the United States
was, at the start, accepted by three States only.
Australia improves on that.
Unless Democratic Australia “gets a hustle
on it ”it js almost certain that old, Tory, worn-
out Britain, will be. ahead of it in the Old Age
Pension business.
The gravamen of Premier Reid’s act in
granting money to Jawbone Neild, M.L. A., is not
the actual cash involved, but the pains taken to
conceal the payment from Parliament.
Premier Reid: “The situation really
comes down to this : that the present Opposition
thought it worth while to have a change of leader-
ship in order to gather in John Haynes.”
Sydney Telegraph continues to allude to
the “Secret Conference Bill.” When the Federa-
tion is in full blast, the D.T. will, no doubt, call
it the “ Secret Conference Commonwealth.”
The members of N.S.W. Labor-party,
fearful of a dissolution, always decide to support
the existing Govt, by a majority of one. Which
one ? Why, Number One. Our good .£3OO a year.
The other day, Arthur Griffith wrote (for
publication) to N.S.W. Premier-expectant Lyne :
“Dear Mr. Lyne.” Lyne prefaced his reply with
(for publication): “My dear Mr. Griffith.” That’s
right. Lyne’s got him.
Dear Bulletin.— Strange that nobody has
heretofore pointed out that the three-months’ resi-
dence-condition of N.S.W. Electoral Act practi-
cally disfranchises those thousands of miners and
navvies who are ever shifting from field to field
and camp to camp. Why should it be necessary
for a man who was born in the country and has
lived there, say, 30 years, to reside three months
in one place before being entitled to vote ?
Wouldn’t a week be sufficient ? Out of about 700
men employed on the big job I was working at
during last general election not more than 50
were entitled to vote. If we are to have “ One
Man One Vote”—let us have it !—C.K.
The S.A. Conservatives want the “Effec-
tive-Voting System,” but they object to the
arrangement of districts under it on a purely
population basis. In other words they want their
little provincial strongholds to be left "intact while
they split up parties in the big centres. Of 14
members returned to the Assembly by the Adelaide
constituencies 12 are Liberals and it is in regard to
them that the Tories want the “ Hare ” plan
employed as they stand to lose nothing.
About the finest specimen of political
inconsistency in Queensland is to be noted in
the discrepancy between the utterances of
Minister Chataway and those of his Mackay
organ, the Sugar Journal. Down south
Chataway supports Federation ; up in Mac-
kay his alleged organ barracks against it.
At the same time the kanaka journal above-
mentioned strikes a big hunk of truth now
and again. Just listen : “And though the
Federal Parliament has the power to move in
the matter of colored labor, it will undoubt-
edly be greatly influenced by the action of the
State Parliament. With a general feeling
that colored labor should be abolished, and
a State Parliament in Queensland asking
that action be taken in that direction by the
Federal Parliament, the labor for the Nor-
thern canefields is doomed.”
The Forrest Govt, is going it ! The
latest is the introduction of the S.A. home-
stead-blockers system, under which workmen
can get handy-sized allotments, big enough
to hold a garden and a cow, just outside the
larger towns. Perpetual leases were sug-
gested.; but Big John couldn’t stand that.
Still there is a fly even in this ointment; for
Adelaide Labor men say that blockers, get-
ting a trifle out of their gardens, are wil-
ling to cut-in for any jobs that are going for
less than regulation pay.
Maoriland Government submits a Bill,
fixing the minimum wage for beginners of both
sexes at 4s. a week. The nefarious system of
employing small girls' in millinery establishments
at nothing the first year, and the sack immediately
afterwards, has been growing of late years, and
Seddon’s honest democratic blucher has got on to
the swindle.
The Jawbone Neild job is utterly indefen-
sible, but it is curious to note that nearly every
N.StW. patriot who denounced it, anathematised
poor old “ Jim ” Toohey when that late lamented
brewer and guardian of the public purse girded at
G. Republican Dibbs, whose Govt, gilded.him, with
a thousand State sovereigns, on the occasion of
his unauthorised, and mostly pleasure, trip to Eng-
land in ’92.
When “ official ” Labor papers were first
barracked for in Australia the idea was to keep
them “ free from the enslavement of advertising
patronage.” Yet the S.A. Labor organ, which
has had a less troubled career than most of them,
received in its last half-year >£lB9 from the sale of
papers and <£s3s—just three times as much—from
advertisers.
The proposal to fit up a section of Mel-
bourne Exhibition Building for the first Federal
Parliament raises a question as to why the Federal
crowd couldn’t have the use of Parliament House,
leaving the local Legislature to shift into new
quarters. Melbourne’s finest building will look
much too expensive for its purpose when Federa-
tion comes along.
Gov. Brassey, speaking lately at Bendigo,
avowed himself a protectionist. He limits his
faith in the virtues of protection to the needs of
“growing countries,” meaning young countries,
thereby adopting the doctrine expounded by John
Stuart Mill, forty years ago—which exposition the
late Parkes alleged seduced him from freetradefor
a period of three years.
At latest advices the N.S. W. Government
is thinking of bringing the railway into Sydney—■
the new station to be built on a block bounded by
King, Elizabeth, Hunter and Phillip streets.
This presumably means the abandonment of any
railway connection with North Sydney, otherwise
the line would be on the west side of George-street
—somewhere about Wynyard-square.
N.S.W. Labor-party is of much greater
value to Premier Reid than the most subservient
of his direct followers. However abject ten
of his direct Freetrade followers may be they
are only ten votes ; but his ten followers in the
Labor caucus count 19 votes. There are 19 in the
Labor-party, and they always decide to support
Reid by a majority of one, the nine agreeing to
sink their conscience and go with the ten.
Bananaland has just been blessed with a
fresh Asiatio irruption. 72 Filipinos landed the
other day at Thursday Island, and 80 more are to
follow in a few days. Pearl-shelling is the little
game. Queensland Govt, is, as usual, “taking
steps ”; but the heathen have cast anchor, and
the weary Northern Democrat is mournfully ask-
ing %ohen United Australia is going to take in
hand the task of exclusion, in which Queensland
has so signally failed.
No sooner does a slight improvement show
itself in the public finances than the political
barnacles call out for more succour. Zeal, M.L.C.
president Yic. Leg. Council, has his screw put up
(as per Estimates) from .£750 to £lOOO, while
Mason, M.L.A., Speaker, gets £250 a year more
or £1250. Brown, M.L.C., Chairman of Com-
mittees, gets £5OO a year, while Beazley, Assem-
bly chairman, draws £740 ! ! And all this in sight
of Federation and an emasculated local Parlia-
ment!
AND HIS GOLDEN HAIR WAS HANGING
DOWN HIS BACK.
SHE: “No; my decision can never be altered! I

THE POET: " Ah! you say if.’ Then there is

S HE: “If you got your hair cut!”
No doubt of the earnestness of Federalists
on the Westralian gold-fields. At a recent
Federal league meeting in Perth it was announced
that the fields’ executive was willing to subscribe
to the funds for carrying on the campaign in Perth.
And to unite fields and coastal Federalists, and
have representation in Parliament, the fields
acquiesced in the appointment of George Leake,
Q.C., of the Six Families, and leader of the bogus
Westralian Opposition, as leader of the Federal
party in the province.
The N.S.W. Labor-party, to Premier
Reid: “ Look here, old man, we’re very good
friends, you know, and you gave one of us a port-
folio, and put some of us on the Public Works
Committee where the salary was very regular and
comfortable, and sent some more of us into the
Upper House ; but we promised our constituents
we’d get certain measures passed, and you always
put them on your programme but never pass
them. And if this sort of thing goes on for
another 25 years we’ll really have to look as if we
were angry with you.”
Westralia’s R.C. priests are, for the
greater part, vigorous Federal fighters. One of
them, Father O'Gorman, from the gold-fields, has
gone down to the city, and there uses his bit av a
shtick with tremendous energy.
SHORT.
THE PARSON : “So you are going to college with
your big brother, eh?”
THE BOY: “ Yes, sir”
THE PARSON : “ And do they call you ' Anstruther
Secundus? ’”
THE BOY : “ Oh, no. Just plain Lousy.”
A member of the present Yic. Government
estimates that his billet as a Minister is not worth
more than <£4oo a year to him. He receives
•£lOOO per annum, of which about £lOO is deducted
for Cabinet expenses, including whip’s salary,
social affairs, and so on. He gives away £2OO a
year more than he would do were he a private
member, thus leaving £7OO. Subtract from that
the £3OO. a year he would receive as a private
member (if he were not in office), and £4OO net
remains.”
One clause in Premier Turner’s Old
Age Pensions Bill provides that the recipient
of £lB a year must be of “ good moral charac-
ter ” who “ has for five years been leading a sober
and reputable life.” Seeing that the pensioner
must also be not less than 65 years of age, or, if
younger, permanently disabled from work, there
should not be much scope for interference by the
social purity brigade. Giddiness and chronic
unsobriety come very difficult to the stone-broke
citizen of 65. Drink is expensive and Totties are
costly receptacles for the same.
“ S. le W.” on the N.S.W. Parliamentary
letter-franking business :
In India they have stamps with “ Service ” printed on
them, and when an officer or official “indents” for as
many as he wants the foot-clause runs:—“l certify on
my honor that the above are required for Her Majesty’s
Service only, and will not be otherwise employed.”
People may jeer at honor, but in this case, as in every
other, the demand creates the supply. We ought to
have a similar system here, and some such declaratory
clause also should be signed by all judges, magistrates,
commissioners, experts, &c., when applying for travelling
exes. Let them pay (or owe) and send in a “ contingent
bill,” with vouchers attached. And then John Norton
could have a war-dance over the first caught “ working
the oracle.”
Deakin’s present seat in Parliament—
never too safe—is as good as lost since his Sab-
batarian somersault over the education question,
and to extend it for Federal requirements means
the inclusion of Footscray, North Melbourne or
Brunswick—all districts solemnly democratic and
dead against Deakin. Under the circumstances
Vic. Government is arranging a nice little lot in
pious Geelong, so cut that the ungodly will have
small chance of scoring. The versatile Alfred
will be thus provided with a brand-new Federal
constituency, while at the same time Higgins will
be debarred from a Federal seat by the opposition
of such an ardent Federalist as Deakin.
Sydney Daily Telegraph complains that
Premier Turner’s proposal to levy extra taxation
towards a Victorian Old-Age Pension Fund is “ a
direct attempt to tax the provident and thrifty
for the sustenance of those who have failed, per-
haps through their own unworthiness, in the
battle of life.” Well, imagine any community
taxing the improvident and unthrifty for the sus-
tenance of those who had been'magnificently suc-
cessful in life ! It is a sad thing, but owing to
the short-sightedness of the Deity, who was un-
fortunately deprived of the Telegraph’s valuable
advice when ordering the state of things, all taxa-
tion is a penalty on thrift. The benighted person
who has absolutely no thrift pays nothing because
he has nothing with which to pay anything.
Major-General French’s published appeal
to Queenslanders to vote for the Federal Bill
turned white a crowd of Constitutionalists in both
-N.o.W. and Q. Some of these talk of referring"
the indiscretion ”to British authorities.
“ Honest Tom” Playford, formerly King-
ston’s partner in passing many valuable re-
forms, and now fast becoming a hard-shell
Tory, is one of the strongest opponents of
Suffrage. The other day
“ Honest Tom ” was inveighing against
the habit Australian Govts, have of imi-
tating one another. “Look at Australian
railway management,” he said. “ When
Victoria and N.S.W. went for one Com-
missioner, S.A. immediately followed.
Then when Victoria had three we im-
mediately did the same, and lost thou
sands of pounds inconsequence.” “ Why,
it was your Govt, that appointed the
three,” interjected a member. And it
was so.
When the Truck Bill (ordering
that wages should be paid in money in-
, stead of the workman being forced to take
them out in goods at exorbitant prices)
was before the Westralian Assembly,
Mitchell, M.L.A,, had an inspiration.
He proposed that money should include
drafts or orders drawn on “any incor-
porated co., firm, or individual trading or
carrying on business in Westralia,”
whereby John Smith, of Albany, could
pay his men’s wages by giving them
written orders on John Jones, who keeps
a small shop at Roebourne, 2,000 miles
away. The amendment was lost more by
accident than anything else, but the House
regarded it quite seriously, and debated it
quite seriously, and nobody said any-
thing about the injustice of making a
man walk 2000 miles over heavy country
to get the 30s. justly due to him.
Westralian telegram of 31st August.
Kalgoorlib, Thursday.—To escape the heavy rates
which are to be imposed on ore shipped to the coast from
to-morrow, the Lake View Consols has sent away a large
quantity of stone. Some of the stone contained so much
gold that, had it not been sent away before the new tariff
came into force, it would, under the absurd doubling-up
system adopted, have cost £ll per ton for carriage to
Fremantle. The much-talked-about Transvaal dynamite
monopoly is not worth considering alongside this imposi-
tion of £ll per ton for the carriage of ore. Sir John
Forrest’s effort in imposing this tariff, and, at the same
time, a dividend tax of 5 per cent., it is said, out-
distances anything that Oom Paul ever attempted.
A pathetic complaint now circulating in W.A. is
that the Federal Constitution gives the Inter-
State Commission (to be appointed by the Federal
Parliament) too much power to interfere with,
railway management. It looks as if Westralia
would soon be only too glad to get any intelligent
outsider to interfere with its railway management.
One of the most amusing and amazing of
the cries raised by the anti-Federal party in
Queensland was: “Queensland will be a black-
labor State under the Union.” One would imagine,
to listen to this cry, that Bananaland was pure
white instead of being very piebald ; that it feared
contamination from its mongrel neighbors, and
that it had always done its best to preserve Aus-
tralia from alien contagion. The people of Queens-
land have been tricked by their own provincial
legislators time and again on the black-labor
question ; hence it was argued that the best thing
that province could do was to go on trusting its
own untrustworthy Parliament. In Queensland
the black-labor interests are powerful; in the
southern provinces they are not so ; hence the
anti-Federal lunatics inferred that a Parliament
in which southern legislators predominate will be
more favorable to the Polynesian traffic than a Q.
Parliament in which the planting interest is-
remarkably strong.

Dear Sir, —It is with great pleasure that I write you these few lines to tell you what your great remedy, “Yitadatio,” has done
for me. At the beginning of my illness I was living in America, and was attending a ball' to see the “ New Year in,” in 1897. On

feet deep). I was laid up for some considerable time, and treated by some of the most eminent physicians in America, but all to no
advantage. Hemorrhage of the lung set in, and my doctors' said that I had “ gone into Consumption,” and that there was no cure
for me, and advised me to return to my native land, Australia. So on the 4th June, 1897, I arrived back in Melbourne. I then
consulted many doctors, and tried all sorts of medicines, but got no relief, so naturally began to despair. My husband heard from
our friend, Mr. H. M‘Kenzie, sewer contractor, at South Yarra, how his son had been cured of Consumption bv the use of “ Vita-
datio.” Mr. M‘Kenzie urged my husband to give me a course of the remedy, which he did, and right glad lam that he did, as the
hemorrhages have ceased, and also the terrible racking cough that used to keep me awake all night. I can now attend to my duties,
and can truly say that “ Yitadatio” has cured me. Por the sake of other sufferers who may be in the same plight as I was, I <nve
you full permission to use this letter as you may think fit. We are well known in these colonies, as my husband was in the furrier
business for years. —I remain, yours truly, ' Mrs F. A. CLARK

The mining boom, which has recommenced since
the Boer scare comparatively died down, has distinctly
benefited the Sydney Sate Deposit. Brokers and buyers
alike see the folly of keeping valuable scrip either in office
safes or private residences, and there have been la me
numbers of visitors to view the massive strength of the
Deposit vaults, with a proportionate increase in the
number of clients. We are requested to state that the
management freely invite a visit. The Deposit is situated
in Ash-street (back of Paling and Co's), off George-street.*

A new Australian competition 25 miles road
record was established by W. Wade, of Melbourne, the
other Saturday, during the running of the Coburg road
race. Wade (on Dunlop tyres) succeeded in covering the
distance in Ihr. 3min. 44see., some 2min. 14sec. better
than the previous record, held by W. Hickox, of Melb.*
» * * *
W. Martin is evidently regaining his old form,
for at the Brisbane carnival, the other Saturday, he suc-
ceeding in winning (on Dunlop tyres) a mile event in the
fast time of 2min., and a 3-mile race in 7min. 31 2-ssec.,
which goes to prove that “ William ” will be interesting
company to the top-notchers this season.*

Raoing proper in ’Stralia begins to-day (Sat.) at
Randwick, where the A. J.C. opens the campaign that will
shortly be continued by V.A.T.C. at Caulfield, and V.K.C.
at Fleinington. Hurdles should be won by the Barber,
despite his recent bad track performance. Trial Stakes
looks a walk-over for Gauleon, whose only dangerous
opponent seems to be Gentle Hermit. The Derby may go
to Victoria by agency of nuggety Promontory (who is very
forward in condition), Cranberry and the better of Long’s
pair (Beaulieu and Parthian) perhaps hunting him home.
The Epsom seems very open, the favorites, Dandy,
Severity, and Superb, being marketable at 10 to 1. If
the fine weather lasts Maecenas should win, Continent
and Proudfoot also appearing to have good chances.
Given wet weather Chesterina might “ plough” through.
The Spring Stakes should be a match between Le Var and
Cocos, two horses that will anon prove themselves big
winners. The Metrop., run on Tuesday, might be raced
out by Cocos and Ace of Diamonds.
• • * •
Editor, BULLETIN : The writer of the para-
graph concerning my mare Cremona and a sweep on the
Spring Handicap, has either been misinformed or has
misconstrued his information. In justice to my reputa-
tion as a sportsman, I wish you to print the facts of the
case, which are as follow :—Cremona was engaged in the
Welter and Spring Handicaps at last Tattersall’s meeting.
Early in the day a well-known committee-man of Tatter-
sail's Club came to me and stated that the drawer of my
mare in the ordinary sweep wished to lay the owner a
share. Thinking the field would be large enough to give
a penalty for future events, I at once decided not to
accept any sweep money, and told the gentleman to in-
form the drawer accordingly'. At the last minute a num-
ber of horses were scratched, and the race did not give a
penalty. Now you can see how unfair your paragraph was,
as it is made to appear that I was waiting for sweep-
money, whereas 1 had been offered it and knew that the
result of the Special in which Cremona was drawn by
my partner would not be known until after the race. I
give you • for your own information the name of the
gentleman who offered me the sweep money. I may
mention that the first prize in said sweep was worth
4900.—Yours truly, J. M. McMastkr.
» • • •
Brighton Racing Club gave the Sydney trotting
men two trots last week. On Monday Prince Harold
won from 35sec. behind, the 2-miles being run in 5.45 J.
The second event was trotted on Saturday afternoon,
and was won easily by Vanera, a stallion by Era from
Miss Cleve. Vanera goes to the stud this season, and
should do well, as, in addition to showing pace, he com-
bines the blood of the two stallions which have done
most for trotting in Australia—Era being by Childe
Harold, and Miss Cleve by Van Cleve, the sire of Cham-
pion Fritz.
* • • *
Mr. “Johnny” Deeble’s new starting-barrier
was used in all the galloping races at Brighton (Sydney)
on Saturday afternoon, and acted throughout without a
hitch. The horses cannot break through. It is quite
impossible for it not to go off when the trigger is pulled ;
and, if time and experience prove that there is no danger
of the horses getting mixed up with the ropes ere it
reaches the ground, it stands a good show of being the
barrier of the future.
• • • •
Books report no dearth of betting and punters
at Hawkesbury (N.S.W.) races on Saturday. The noble
army of the guileless absolutely piled it on—this as a
result of besting Ikey at Canterbury on Tuesday week,
and at Rosehill the previous Saturday. For many a day
money hasn’t been sported, in or around Sydney, to such
an extent. Coal Queen, heav\ly backed by owner and
public, ran third to Whirlpool, but must not be dismissed
in consequence. Her chance in the Epsom looks rosy as
ever. She was interfered with three times, otherwise the
result would have been different.

Peace has been declared between the Victorian
Club committee and the insurgent forces under Alf.
Joseph.
» * •
Not generally known that Alf Joseph is the real
lord and master of the Shamrock Hotel, Bendigo, the
birthplace of Alfred’s greatness. All the poetic aspira-
tions of his soul are expressed in the furniture and fittings
of the famous “honeymoon apartments” at the Sham-
rock.
• * * *
Apparently nothing will knock it into the thick
skull of the average jockey that there are other ways of
winning a race than by “ a run on the rails.’’ No matter
what state the course is in, or how the race is run, there
is always the ruffianly rush at the turn for “ position,”
said position being in front and close enough to the rails
to stop anything from getting up on the inside. It only
occasionally strikes a boy that there is room enough fora
regiment to pass on the outside. That most Sydney
jockeys have only this one idea of riding a race was par-
ticularly noticeable at Tattersall’s Club recent spring
meeting, when, after the first couple of races, the course
next the rail was like the Blacksoil Plain, and out in the
centre was firm and good going, yet all hands hugged the
rails as usual! Cranberry, who ran second in Hampden
Stakes, was interfered with during the race, and when
young Fielder brought him in with a white mark right
down his side it was remembered that it was he who lost
Wait-a-bit last Melb. Cup through trying to squeeze him
up on the inside—so it is apparently an absolute disease
with some jocks. If an average horseman, with enough
horse sense to run out a little at the home turn and miss
the jostling for position, as Alec. Robertson used to do,
were to come along he would soon find his way to the top
of the tree. In a straight run it doesn’t matter if a nag
is on the inside or the outside rails, as long as he didn’t
lose ground in getting there.
• • • *
The conviction of Charles Westbrook, book-
broker, on a charge of using his office in Melb. Stock Ex-
change building as a common gaming-house, ought to be
followed by a wholesale arrest of jobbing stock-makers
for betting on the pavement outside the Exchange.
• * * *
Melb. suburban police have become suddenly
alive to the accursed betting nuisance, and here and
there traps have been laid for the brutes who live by the
temptation, speculation, and degradation of young jug-
ginses. Down at St. Hilda, where scores of well-dressed
youths seem to be punting on the kerbstone six days a
week, and spitting disconsolately into the gutter when
Sunday comes round, two local “ metallicians ” were
fined £2O last week. The bulk of the money taken by
these suburban pests comes from “ led-astray ” lads, and
in many cases it was raised at the pawh-shop on jewellery
pilfered from the punter’s parents.
* • • •
“ G.B. ” : Your recent picture of V.R.C. starter
George Watson recalls the day's when he used to carry all
before him at the Midland Steeplechase at Campbelltown,
Tas. For 6or 7 years he took ’em down regularly, mostly
on the magnificent jumper Ivanhoe, which used to “ lep ”
with the disconcering side-twist peculiar to nags accus-
tomed to the Irish stone-walls. But Watson having
served his apprenticeship on the ould sod, was equal to
all that. Can only remember old George being beaten
once in Tas.—by Tom Tabbart on VVill-if-I-can. George
was a revelation to locals as to the value of “ hands” in
cross-country work.
* * * *
The psalm-singers think of organising a rival
show to Melbourne Cup this year. The Methodists have
started the idea, and all denominations are to be invited.
The local Methodist organ puts the case thus, with no
sense of the ridiculousness of the argument: “Only
70,0(10 people attend the races, and about 430,000 people
in the vicinity of the course keep awa.v.” The conditions,
therefore, “are all in our favor.” Atheist Symes might
as well argue that as only a few thousands of the popu-
lation go to church on Sundays, the rest are all in his
favor. Anyhow, the united churches might safely be
challenged to work up a gathering of a fifth, part of
70,000. They couldn’t do it—no, not even by promising
to post the results ot the raoes between the singing of the
hymns.
• • • •
Two of the winners at Caulfield last Saturday
are expected to be dangerous in the Cups. Egypt, a
4-year-old son of Eiridspord and Hatason, in the Miller
stable, has been running away from fields of commoners
lately, and with only the minimum weight (plus a small
penalty) for Caulfield Cup, the gelding should have a
chance, if they can keep him bright for the next six
weeks. Egypt may be a good thing this season. He ran
several times as a 2-year-old without a win ; as a 3-year-
old he didn't show in public.
Disfigured won a race on Saturday, the first
since her return from India, where the mare could do
nothing against the climate, and was not raced at all.
In giving the scarred mare 8.5 and 8.2 for the two Cups
the handicappers were treating her as a back-number,
but now that she has shown a glimpse of her eld form,
she will be worth watching. Two years ago Disfigured
won the Melbourne Stakes, beating Hopscotch. Not a
great performance—the time was slow—but highly
respectable.

* ' * * *
Printed all over England that Jones stays to
play with Sussex and Howell remains to join Middlesex.
Both plaj'ers have flatly denied the rumors. English
cricketers, by the way, are crying out at this importation
of “ colonials ”by wealthy county executives. Middlesex,
which is playing Trott and Roche, is credited with being
the most unpopular county, solely through the success of
these two Australians. To meet the difficulty, some talk
of making the qualification for Australians five years’
residence.
• • * •
The Australians complain, that the biggest bores
they meet in England are Australian tourists, who con-
tinually turn up to talk about cricket and invite theril to
drink. After a month or two of cricket the mere idea of
the game palls after the day is over, but the persistent
bore insists upon talking about how Darling hit and
Howell howled. Noble* it is sajd, is the only Australian
who can effectually deal with these gentry, and his
method is a summary snub. But even Noble has his little
weakness. He is never tired of explaining how he makes
the ball curve in the air.
* * • •
The recent narrow escape from poisoning by
Australian cricketers in England roundaboutly recalls the
loss of nine out of an 1890 Hongkong Eleven by the foun-
dering near the Pescadores of the P. and O. s.s. Bokhara,
in which they were returning from England.
* • » __ *
There is plenty of indirect evidence that the
All-England batting in the two first test matches was
much too bad to be true, and -that the Australians did
nothing remarkable in making good scores against the
English bowling. Take the Gentlemen v. Players’ match
played early in July. The amateur bowlers were Towns-
end, W. G. Grace, Bosanquet, A. O. Jones, Jephson and
H. K. Foster. Against that attack the Players made 647
runs in their one innings—a bigger score than Darling’s
eleven would be likely to make. On the other hand, the
prof, bowlers were Young, Lockwood, Mead, Brockwell,
Hayward and Storer, and against them the Gentlemen
scored 611 runs in their two innings—which was quite up
to the Australians’ batting form. A lot has been written
about the alleged luck of Hayward in test-matches, but it
seems that his average up to July 9 was 54'17 per innings.
In this Gentlemen v. Players match Hayward scored 134
not out, the last wicket (Young) assisting him to bring
the total from 512 to 647 in SOmiti. 1258 were scored in
the three days, the game being finished an hour before
closing time on the third day.'

English pros, get plenty of encouragement to
do their best against the Australians. At the conclusion
of the Surrey match, young Hayes (who made 130 runs in
150 min. for the county) was presented with £6O, the
result of a collection taken all round the Oval.

Major Poore and Capt. Wynyard, batting for
Hampshire v. Somerset, scored at the rate of 100 runs an
hour for over four hours. The dashing Majah was doing
a good deal of this sort of thing in South Africa awhile
ago. He revels in hot weather, and would be just the
man to send with the next English team to Australia.

Vic. League football premiership seems practi-
cally a foregone conclusion for Fitzroy, but Footscray,
the Association crack club, i§ out with a challenge to
play off for the championship. No answer Iris yet been
received from the superiah_ League, which reckons its
component parts the cream’ of the game, and the possi-
bility is that the defi. will pass unheeded. Keen ob-
servers of the game consider League play a trifle too
good for the Association, though many would delight, to
see the former body discomfited for its unsportsmanlike
and mercenary action in seceding from the Association
three years ago.
* * *
As to the respective merits of the Rugby and
Australian games of football, Rugby would probably be
unknown on the mainland to-day but, for Omah, or
Gugga, or some of meteorologist YVragge’s friends which
came along to the assistance of dear old England’s game
and swamped Sydney Association Ground at the critical
moment. It was in ’BB, when Vic. football was in its
zenith and the two leading teams, S. Melb. and Carlton,
met in Sydney whilst returning.from trips to Queensland
and Tas. respectively. A match was hurriedly arranged,
the Association Ground was taken, and so excellently
was the show boomed that all Sydney was talking about
it. But six hours before ball-up the downpour began,
and so the match, which would have shown N.S.W. the
error of its football ways, was never played.
Speaking of the local game during his visit to
Melh., Rev. Mullineux admitted that it was more open
and breezy than Rugby, and the only fault he could pick
in it was that the place men stood a chance of catching
cold—a splendid recommendation from the Rugby Ranji
who finds fault with everything and everybody. YVhen de-
fending Rugby he was pointedly asked : “ if a number
of men who had never seen either played previously were
shown an Australian match and a Rugby match which
would they vote the better game?” His reverence hesi-
tated for a second, then smiled, and slung the towel
in favor of Australia.
* * * *
The finish for the Queen’s Prize at Bisley
(England) lately was >ne of the most exciting things ever
seen in rifle-shooting. YVhen Jones (YY’elsh), Anderson
(Scotch), and Priaulx (Guernseyman) had “tied” at 336
points, they were given three more shots each at 1000yds.,
when a second tie almost resulted between Guernsey and
Wales, but Jones's nervousness “let in” the Channel
Islander, who was as 000 l as a machine. The fact that
the winner uses neither alcohol nor tobacco will doubtless
be made the most of by the antibaccaboozealots.

* * * *
Worth mentioning, as evidence of the boom in
bowls, that two Sydney clubs (Waverley and Ashfield)
played a match last Saturday, wherein each side was re-
presented by 16 rinks, or 64 players, and of the 128 men
participating, the oldest did not exceed 60, while several
“boys” of 30 were engaged. As far as is known, the
match is a world’s record for numerical strength of
teams.
* * • •
Writing recently, Hunter River (N.S.W.)
sculler Geo. Towns, who is sculling champion of England,
and still “at’Ome,” said prospects were very bad as he
would be unable to row for months. On that account there
was little show’ of a match with his fellow-countryman,
Wray, and the probabilities of meeting world-champion
Gaudaur, even when Towns gets clear of the effects of
his accident, are most remote. The French-Canadian puts
so many obstacles in the w r ay that it is plain he has no
stomach for a further tussle. Towns’ communication also
conveys that professional sculling is as dead in America
as in Australia, and appears to be dying in England.
Dear Bulletin. —ln “Sporting Notions”
(29 7/’99) you say, “ Melb. University crew has on no
occasion been equal to the average maiden eight.” This
is going over the truth line. In '92 Melb. crew won in
Adelaide, and, all being maidens, some of them wished to
keep together and go for the maiden eight to prove the
truth or otherwise of just such a bald assertion as above.
However, their coac-h, Geo. Upward,-was not very sweet
on the pot-hunting proposal, and it fell through. Four
of the crew then went to Colac, and won both the
maiden and junior fours at local regatta, and performed
well in the Melb. (Upper Yarra) regatta. In ’92 and ’93*
Melb. crew was certainly well up to junior form, and I
don’t think those crews were above the average.—Pkrdu.

above-the-averagers) would probably have been able to
win maiden-eight prizes on their ’Varsity race “ form,” it
is equally probable that many ’Varsity crews would have
failed.—El). B.]
* * • *
Otto Cribb, the cyclonic bruiser from M.L., got
away with another fight at Golden Gate Club (Sydney) on
Saturday night—his fifth successive win. Joe Dunn,
brother to burly Charlie of the perennial smile, opposed
the man-eating Maorilander, but had no show. Dunn’s
great sprinting capabilities stood him in good stead for a
while, especially as he was in capital trim, but Cribb
barred the way two or three times and ladled out the
gruel so hot and thick that Dunn, completely broken up,
cried ago midway through the fourth round. Two more
challenges were thrown out to the winner afterwards.
He accepted both, and, bar accidents, will get away with
each. Cribb’s methods and manner of going through his
men—sort of mowing them down—recalled Joe Goddard
when he was Joe Goddard.

Editor Bulletin. —In a recent issue it is stated
that Tim Murphy defeated Albury Clifford in 3J rounds.
This statement is incorrect. Murphy and I never fought
in our lives, but simply boxed three exhibition rounds.—
Albury Clifford.

“ Old Timer ” : Another old ’un gone ! Tom
Sparkes, whose ring-name half-a-century back was “the
Sprig o’ Myrtle,” died at Canterbury (Sydney) Friday
last. He saw a good “ main ” fought on George’s River
three weeks ago, went home, turned in, and never got
up until three days before death, when he discarded
his night-gear and donned a clean white shirt in accord-
ance with the custom of the Sparkes family—every one
of whom, as far as can be traced, has acted similarly a
day or two before the end. Tom was in his 77th year,
and when writer saw him last (a month ago) looked like
seeing the century out. Heart-failure was the trouble.
Remember Tom at his top, in ’47, fighting middle and
heavy weights, though only a 10-stoner (light-weight)
himself. Two years later he got into double harness,
and at a crowded farewell ben., which was to have taken
place at the “ Fives Court,” Brown Bear Lane, Lower
George-street (Sydney), but actually came off at
“The Shipman’s” Hotel, George and Goulburn-
streets, issued a defiance to all 11.4 men in Aus-
tralia. If memory series Dick Hunt (dead some
years now) was the only man who beat “the Sprig.”
Tom's quiet endurance and manly bearing brought troops
of friends round him, many of whom stuck to the last.
Some years ago the old fellow was giving evidence in
Sidney Supreme Court re the title to some land close by
the family home. Asked by counsel how long he had
known the site. Tom thought awhile, then said, “Well,
I can’t say exactly, but I’ve eaten 60 Christmas puddings
since I first saw the place.” Before my mind’s eye just
now is a “trial” go with the bare-uns prior to one of
Sparkes’s big battles. A well-known successful Sydney
tailor (still in business), who had just previously fought
75 rounds at Cremorne (Port Jackson), was Tom’s oppon-
ent. Things were excessively torrid while the bout
lasted. At the end ’twas found that Tom had put out
one of his knuckles, and it remained that way till the
day he died, but he went on with the fight and won.
“Tim ” Murphy, Victoria’s much-lauded middle-
weight pug., set-to the other night, and writer feels
satisfied “Jack” Conlon or “Mick” Dunn of N.S.W.
would settle his hash. He fights in the same wild style
as his erratic brother, “ Torpedo ” Billy, only more so,
and fills the wing-space with legs and wings as he rushes.
A Sydney sport wanted to back an unknown list, man
against “Tim ” for <£soo a-side, but Tim’s loud-mouthed
admirers, though swearing he is the best of his division
on earth, didn’t see their way clear to plank down a
“monkey” in cold cash. Which calls to mind that
Albury Clifford arrived from Tasmania on Saturday,
looking for fight with Murphy, but latter has a contract
on already with battle-worn Bill Jennings, and more to
follow—Tut Ryan, Jack Conlon, and one or two others
being anxious for the chance.

’Rastlers Lorenz and Maximilian mauled,
tugged, choked, and tripped each other in Brisbane,
Thursday night, for the “Middleweight Championship of
Australasia.” Why? What claim has either to the
title? This was their second match within a couple of
months. A £3O purse and .4.50 a-side formed the induce-
ment. Lorenz won again after a tough tussle. Now
both express anxiety to meet Harry Dunn, and imme-
diately—a desire probably born of the fact that Harry
left Sydney for M.L. last week, arid will not be prepared
to make matches till January, 1900. “ Then let ’em all
come,” says Harry.

Re the recent death of old-time pug. Tom
Sparkes. He was a brother of Bill Sparkes, who, in ’47,
went all the way from Cook’s River (N.S.W.) to England
entirely “ on his own,” and fought Nat Langhatn 67
rounds, occupying 63min. Bill broke the bone of his
right forearm in the 62nd round, otherwise Langham’s
career as a champion would have ended then, thisdespite
the fact that he had height and reach in his favor. Even
with the heavy handicap of the smashed limb Sparkes
came up strong every round, and fought like a tiger.
His seconds had a big task dragging- him out of the ring
—he wanted another “shy.” The Englishman only sur-
vived through “ getting down ” whenever hotly pressed.
Langham afterwards beat Tom Sayers. Pugilistica says
of the Australian : “ A gamer or more fearless fighter
never entered the ring,” and Tom was of the same
kidney.

The difference between the English and Aus-
tralian barracker is that the former throws off inferences,
the latter talks straight to the point. The whistling of
the “ Dead March ” and “ VVe won’t go home till morn-
ing ” is inference, but the Australian barracker’s polite
“Bowl the out” is direct instruction,
leaving no room for doubt as to the gentleman aimed at.
The irate person who called out to Noble in the fourth
test, “ Why don’t you hit ’em, you blanky dog?” was one
of those exceptions which go to prove the rule. A more
truly' Hinglish enquiry, under the circumstances, would
have been lodged in a tone of affected solicitude after
this style—“ Au-pws-tus, are you not afraid of over-
ex-ert-ing .yourself ? ”

The game of bluff. In a Sydney police-court
case, last week, in which a pony-owner and a bookmaker
were concerned, the latter was alleged to have threatened
to punch the former on the nose and give him a good
hiding generally. Under cross-examination, the “book”
said that the pony-owner was a good man with his fists,
and could have beaten him easily. “Then why'threaten
to punch him on the nose?” asked the surprised exami-
ner. “ Oh, just a bit of bluff,” was the reply, “ it’s half
the battle to get in first say.”
The Federation fight has had an extra-
ordinary effect on the different Sydney news-
papers. After washing her hands'of the Reid
Govt, in ’9BS.M. Herald has again taken the
portly Premier to her bosom. The Evening
News, too, has again espoused the Reid cause
after 12 months antagonism, while the Australian
Star has abandoned the Ereetrade Party and is
again attacking the Ministry. The Daily Tele-
graph, having seriously imperilled its soul by its
unscrupulous championship of the lies and schemes
of the Reid regime, has gyrated round to the
Lyne faction, after having made that politician
its butt for a decade.
Dead : Thomas Walsh, who, along with
“Jimmy” Taylor, reorganised Bendigo Stock
Exchange after the awful slump of the seventies.
But though he knew more about Bendigo stocks
and mines than almost any other man on ’Change,
he clung fatuously to three or four chronic call-
makers, which drained him and kept him poor.
The announcement of the death of the
centenarian, Mrs. Casey, at Collector, N.8.W.,
has brought out the names of two others still
living. These are Thos. Johns, of Comelroy
Road (Hawkesbury), who was born at Pennant
Hills in June, 1800. The other is Mrs. Mary Ann
Search, of Bosworth-st., Richmond, who has
passed the century, having been born at Corn-
wallis, Windsor, 26th Jan., 1799. She has a very
large number of grand, g.-grand and g.g.-grand-
children who are scattered over Australia. Her
eldest son is 80, and her youngest daughter (the
mother of 14 children), 56.

advertisement of the Dodd’s Medicine Co. regard-

Mr. A. Sinclair, who has had 34 years
experience of colonial wool-selling and sheep-
stations, is publishing a handy book entitled “A
Clip of Wool from Shearing Shed to Ship,” in
which is given, in minutest detail, an account of
what happens to wool in colonial sale rooms, and
the why and the wherefore of these happenings.
John Andrew and Co., of Bridge-street, Sydney,
are the publishers.*
Mr. W. H. Wigzell, hairdresser, hair-
worker, wigmaker and perfumer, of 110 William-
street, Sydney, has built up a very enviable
reputation in his business. He imports direct
from London, Germany and France, and makes a
speciality of hairworking. No lady need appear
in public without a good head of hair. If she has
one of her own Wigzell will show her how to dress
it; if she has not, then he will make her one that
will serve as well.*

The Breach of Promise Case f
Melbourne•
Ex-Capt. Edwards, S. A., formerly of Lismore and Wood-
burn, in N.S.W., against Adjutant Carpenter, of Head-
quarters Staff, S.A., Melbourne, came on for hearing on
Friday, 25th ult., but was settled privately in court. It
was a'tedious and weary settlement. Acting-Judge John-
son was very patient, and considered that the time lost
would probably be time gained. Such proved to be the
case.
The terms of settlement included the publication of
the following apology
“ I, George Lyndon Carpenter, hereby state that it is
not true that Miss Lily Edwards led me on, and, if at any
time I have said anything which might give that impres-
sion, 1 unreservedly withdraw it.
" Dated this 25th day of August, 1899.
(Signed) “GEO. L. CARPENTER.”
Witness—(Signed! J. U. Maddock. Solicitor. Melbourne

Said that R. S. Smvthe, just now in London, is trying
to induce Rev. Chas. Clark to make another farewell lec-
turing tour through Australasia. If the handsome old
Baptist shepherd should visit us once again, Smythe will

This is certainly a year of Great Discoveries, and Miss
Lili Sharpe, the second-best “phenomenal” soprano
from Bendigo, is a particular case in point. True, she
hasn’t come with a Castles rush, although she is a much
more intelligent artist than the younger damsel. Never-
theless, sinileful Miss Lili amounted to a discovery when
she got her double encores at Melb. Town Hall the other
week. Last year when she sang from the same platform,
under the name of Miss Lily St. Leonards, she wasn’t the
least bit discovered.
Somebody wites from Bendigo, on a postcard, to state
that The Bulletin erred in crediting Miss Lili Sharp with
25 years of age. The Bulletin, by the way, merely sur-
mised from the young lady’s appearance that she might
be not more than 23 to 25, and seeing that the postcard
authority gives her age as “ not 24 until February next,”
he need not have troubled to write. But whilst on the
subject of the Lili Sharp “ discovery,” The Bulletin may
mention that she was presented to Melbourne as a damsel
of “ under 20.”
“T.D.” : Was standing at piano when Amy Castles was
warbling, privately, the other night. Be her methods
ever so uncultured and inartistic, it is still a treat to note
the liquid ease with which she pours out her voice. She
has one essential of the great singer, for she poises her
throat just as a canary or other singing-bird does —and
that is a tip which very few singers will take from nature.
In the programme of Marshall-Hall’s last orchestral
concert he says the Finale of Beethoven’s Symphony in
D Major “ brings upon us a thousand road frolics and
humorsome situations, and dismisses us with merry and
innocent hearts.” Since when has Marshall Hall had a
merry or an innocent heart?
The Howard divorce-suit (Sydney), which resulted in a
decree nisi for the husband, goes to show that a gold-
buttoned ship’s officer is quite equal to any other actor
for “ mashing” purposes. It seems that the respondent,
Mrs. Howard, was a “ fairly wealthy widow ” when she
gave her jewelled hand to comedian Howard, in England,
five years ago. The bride’s health soon required a sea-
voyage, so she took hubby to England per passenger-
steamer. Chief-officer Townsley became very friendly
with the newly-wedded pair, and, after a voyage to and
from Australia by the same boat, Airs. Howard forsook her
lawful spouse, and cleared-out with the theatrical chief-
officer aforesaid. A quite natural indiscretion. The
dandies of the mercantile marine are seasoned stage-
lovers, as a rule, and on their own boards (the quarter-
deck) they have an irresistible attraction for any woman
who inclines her heart to mummers.
Melba, it is printed, will be heard at Covent Garden
next year, after all. She intends to tour Europe this
winter, penetrating as far as Constantinople and St.
Petersburg; but her projected South American tour, for
which she had a princely offer, is postponed for the
present.
The latest about Haddon-Chambers is that he has been
fined £5 for hitting a railway porter with a big stick.
Chambers was (against the regulation) standing on the
footboard of a departing train, talking to a lady, and
having declined to , get. off was forcibly pulled down ;
whereupon he “ incapacitated ” the porter. He offered
no defence and merely pleaded anger.
“Constance Clyde” has been exploring a cheap Syd-
ney show, and comes back in the following frame of
mind: “The record for a cheap and gory treat is to be
found at the Royal Standard Theatre where the Sher-
wood-M‘Gowan Co. are playing ‘Jack Sheppard ’ for Is.,
6d. and 3d. The gallery-hoy who nobly sacrifices his
evening beer in the cause of dramatic art is given full
value for his money, inasmuch as he beholds among
minor horrors an explosion, a house on fire, an execution,
and murders thrown in by the handful. Everybody in
the play gets murdered, and some are obliging enough
to get murdered twice over. One man is killed by a
red tablecloth. That is in scene 113 of Act 95, or there-
abouts, and when the 115th scene rushes down he is still
being killed. The rest of the time is spent mainly in
people discovering they are other people’s lost children.
The plot is the most tangled plot on record, and when it
gets more tangled than usual it is smoothed out by some
of the characters taking documents from their pockets
and saying that these will explain all, and then the re-
maining characters slap their foreheads and say ‘Great
Heavens, ’tis true ! ’ The play is responsible for some his-
torically-histrionic information, notably, in a burglary-
scene, where it is proved that ladies in Jack Sheppard’s
time went to bed with their boots and hats on. There
are, likewise, pathetic touches, and the gallery-boy
chokes on his peanut when the brave highwayman says
something to the effect that if he can only be hanged
with his head on his mother’s breast he will be the hap-
piest hanged man in existence.”
“Poor old Leon Caron,” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, “fresh from a little pleasure trip of fifteen
years conducting in Australia, came to London and
begged but one favor of George Musgrove. Merely .as a
break, a change, a bit of repose, to be permitted to con-
duct the ‘ Belle of New York’ orchestra through some
•little things of his own.’ G.M. consented, and further
got Alfred Plumpton, of the Palace, fifty yards away, to
place his orchestra at Caron’s baton for the same day.
Then for a space of interminable hours the baroneer was
dodging to and fro over cabs and under ’busses from the
Shaftesbury and back working 150 musicians to death
and having" what he termed the ‘ finest holiday he could
have expected.’ ”
Ed. Bulletin.—Was pleased to read your remarks,
12 8 ’99, re the necessity for suppression of the so-called
Sydney “ College of Music.” I believe it to be, as you
say, simply a means for advertising certain music-
teachers arid levying fees. My belief comes from per-
sonal experience. In Feb., this year, J sat for A.Mus.
diploma of this college, at a fee of £3 35., being the only
candidate. Previous to this, after much trouble and
delay, I had been informed that the standard required
would be somewhat similar to the “ Senior Honors.” I
may mention that in June, ’9B, 1 sat for Senior Pass and
Honors of T.C.L., London, and came out with a percent-
age of 88 and 72 marks respectively. With six months’
study on top of this, then, came the A.Mus. papers. Re-
quiring 90 out of 120 to pass, I got 69. And this result
was communicated to me two months after sitting, and
only after repeated application. A request to have the
worked papers returned to me to be sent to England for
examination was refused. Of course, I was not a pupil of
any of the college teachers, and in the letter sent me I
was informed that another setting would be granted me
at reduced fees. —TRAZOM.
Marie Elster, who three years ago made a very good
mark in England as priina-donna of a touring opera co.,
is just now singing a “ turn ” at London Alhambra, and
the critics seem to be hailing her as a recent vocal “ star”
from Australia. Aliss Elster’s success in the early autumn
of her life reminds one of the years when Melbourne
never thought to “ discover ” singers. She and Frances
Saville both warbled deliciously in comic opera at the
Princess’s and elsewhere, but nobody talked of sending
them to Europe. They went “on their own ” without
flourish of trumpets. * Likewise, Lalla Aliranda had to
fairly earn a reputation ere she was reckoned deserving
of a farewell benefit. Saville, Elster, and even the com-
paratively youthful Miranda, not to mention Alelba, were
unlucky in’being born too soon. They ought to have
been contemporaries of Amy Castles.

£3 3s. ; less cost of dinner, ss.—balance, £2 18s.”

“Of current playwrights,” writes “ Alba ” to Tiib
Bulletin, “Jones is the most exasperating. There are
times when one feels that he is about to rise to a power-
ful psychological crisis, as in ‘ The Physician,’ but either
the fear of Mrs. Grundy holds him back, or he hasn’t
the soul to pull it through. Volcanoes rumble in
the keynote of ‘The Physician’ when the great nerve-
specialist hints that he is absorbing the diseases of his
patients. One fancies that he will contract the dipso,
tendencies of the rival whom he magnanimously attempts
to cure —in that direction lay tragedy; but Jones’s volcano
only rumbled a little : he killed his dipso, and married
the"girl to the physician. Query : What shall we do with
our Jones? A similar rumbling happened in ‘Judah’—
his next-best. Wherefore he builds emotional fabrics,
and builds like a tradesman forging steel marionettes
with unutterable labor, hammering with Vulcan strokes
upon the iron of commonplace until the gods
are about to weep; and just when the desired
tear trembles to the cheek up. pops Jones with a sneer,
and down goes the fabric of unutterable sweat. Pinero
is different, his emotional holts are forged with ease, and
when his audience crowd into his palm he holds
them with clenched fist. Yet the grace of poetry
is lacking in this cultured playwright’s scenes.
Never a flower scents the glittering expanse
of dialogue. There is a scene where one of his
heroines looks across the close-packed meadows and sees,
in her imagination, London—London, flash, squalid, a
black ruin with gaslit squares squadroned with jaded
men —London, quick as champagne, repellent as a hedge-
hog ; London —lust, fire, and hypocrisy ! A rarer heroine
than Paula Tanqueray might have leaned from her bal-
cony and felt the eye of Fate burning through the
jewelled—mask of Infinity. Observe how Rostand’s
‘Cyrano de Bergerac’ gluts your sense with a night-
piece ! ‘ Ah, Paris wrapped in night; half nebulous ; the
moonlight streams o’er shadowed roofs ; a lovely frame
for this wild battle-scene : beneath the vapors’ floating
scarves, the Seine, trembling, mysterious, like a magic,
mirror.’ Anon he dwells upon the curse of friendship.
‘ This everlasting friendship which enfolds you is like an
open-laced Italian collar floating round your neck in
woman’s fashion, but . . . hatred, like the stiff ruff worn
by the Spanish, grips like a vyce, hut frames you like a
halo.’ ”
The shriek that W. J. Lyne, if successful
against G. H. Reid on the present occasion, would
be placed in the uncongenial position of haying
to appoint the first Federal Cabinet is mere windy
gasconading. There is every chance that the
Federal Parliament will insist on nominating the
first Federal Premier; at least it certainly ought
to. To let the Governor-General, who knows
nothing of Australian politics “send for ” whom
he pleases, and thereby decide on his own
responsibility who shall rule Australia would be
an insult to every democratic principle.- And if
the Federal Parliament chooses its own leader
then the fact that somebody is or isn’t Premier of
N.S.W. or of any other place can have no possible
bearing on the case. It may select Cnck or
O’Sullivan.
When the Federal address to the Queen
was before N.S.W. Leg. Council, Dr. Creed (who,,
when outside Parliament House, is always annoy-
ingly wide-awake and energetic), moved the in-
sertion of a dissolution clause. Then (mesmerised
by his soporific surroundings) he sunk into a deep
sleep, while the debate raged around him, and
made movements with his hands as one who was
heaving the Bill out in his dreams. The Govern-
ment representative kept an eye on him, and at
the earliest possible moment pushed the matter to
a vote. Everybody expected Creed to call for a
division, but he only slumbered harder, and his
proposition was rejected on the voices. Then,
long afterwards, he awoke and was starting to
complain that the remarks of the honorable one
then discoursing were irrelevant to the amend-
ment, and when lie was told that the amendment
was ancient history by now, and that his country
had been lost while he was having a snooze, he
could only groan.

A little work called “Childbirth in
N.S.W.” just issued by Statistician Coghlan con-
tains information calculated to make the moral
section of the community groan enormous groans.
During the six years 1893-1898, 56,163 women gave
birth each to her first child. Of these 14,779 were
illegitimate ; 13,366 were, judging by dates, the
resultof something that happened be fore marriage ;
and 28,018 or not quite one-half, were perfectly
respectable, according to the canons of conven-
tional morality. This means that nearly one-
third of the women who married did so under dire
necessity to prevent their coming offspring being
illegitimate. The legitimate births have de-
creased from 37,437 in 1893 to 33,353 in 1898 !
During the same period the number of illegitimate
births rose a little, from 2486 to 2497; and the
number of legitimate births which were, appar-
ently, the result of unbridled love before marriage
rose from 1981 to 2408. So, apparently, the births
which were above suspicion were 35,456 in 1893
and only 30,945 in 1898 despite a considerable
increase in population. But the utterly stagger-
ing fact is contained in the apparent revelation
that only about half the women of N.S.W. who
reached what may be vaguely described as the
marriageable age between 1893 and 1898 were
“ respectable ” in the conventional meaning of the
term. Probably, however, a good many people,
especially in small country places, guessed that
much already. The registrar at a country
town once made a cynical remark to the
writer on this subject over a pot of beer.
“This is a quiet place,” he said, thoughtfully
lifting the beer, “but our old cats have
one amusement that keeps them alive. When-
ever a girl gets married every old cat puts
down the date in a book and then they wait to see
when her child will be born. When it is born
they put that date down also, and after that they
meet and cackle.” Here the official thought-
fully surrounded his beer. “ Some of them, he
added, “ have got books covering twenty years,
and you can look through them and find particu-
lars of every young married woman in this town
whose infant was a trifle previous. And you
would be surprised to know how previous they
are.”
N.S.W. Cabinet Ministers charge, the
Premier 3gs., other Ministers 2gs. a day, wliile
travelling in the country on public business. Tim
Ministers themselves define what is public busi-
ness.
Barton was met with a point-blank
refusal by the Reid Govt, when he asked for a
“pair” in the censure division, while away in
Queensland on the eve of the Referendum. When
the House heard that the Federal leader’s absence,
while fighting for Australia, was thus taken ad-
vantage of, ugly comments came even from the
Government side, and Chief Secretary Brunker
hastily telephoned to Barton that the Govern-
ment Whip would pair with him. Barton
was already on his way North, and the message
had to be wired after him. This incident is
eloquent testimony to the hold the Federal idea
has already taken on the body politic.
At time of writing, N.S.W. Opposition was
perfectly sure of ousting the Reid Ministry, and
professed to have discovered interesting facts
which quite accounted for the veering towards
the Government of certain members who entered
Parliament under the Barton aegis. And after the
Ministry has been “slung out?” Well, never
mind, say several of the Premier’s most active
enemies—“ Reid deserves punishment for his
Federation wobblings ; let the future take care
of itself.” The situation is a curious one. One
lot of politicians seeks to expel Reid from office
because he in the first place tried to block Federa-
tion, and these are backed up by another lot who
thirst for his blood because he ultimately decided
to help in bringing Federation about. One thing
is quite certain—the Opposition is united only by
hate of Reid and not by love of Reid’s enemy
Lyne.
At the dictation of David Syme, Vic. Boss
Policeman O’Callaghan (who has eyes on the Chief
Co.onmissionershjp) has started a series of lectures
on “ Police,Duty,” and has arranged for a “ big
house ” beforehand by ordering that all cops below
the rank of sub-inspector shall attend. The result
is that old, grey-headed sergeants who know quite
as much as the great O'Callaghan himself are
brought in from stations 20 miles away to hear
the Age’s protege telling them how to make an
arrest. O'Callaghan’s first lecture was delivered
last week, and in his two hours’ talk he told the
assembled peelers nothing that the average civilian
doesn’t know. The police force that needed such
a lecture ought to be instantly abolished. There
were altogether 335 men present, and fully five of
these derived some benefit from the oration. The
dragging of old-service men to such a discourse is
mere fatuous nonsense. If they have not learnt
their duty by now they never will. For recruits
the idea is an excellent one, but the lecture should
be given while they are on probation, not after
they have banged the inoffensive citizen over the
skull with a baton for asking which is Bourke-
street, and after half have been drafted to pountry
stations. There were only 35 constables appointed
to the Vic. force since last year, and of these 20
were mounted men now scattered in the back-
blocks.
The sudden refund to N.S.W. Treasury
of the .£350 paid to Brigadier-General Neild on
account of his old-age pension report labors is in-
volved in mystery, and as to " ho actually repaid the
amount opinions'differ. All the Ministerial party,
from the Premier to the meanest joint of the tail,
are in turn credited with it. Everyone of
course agrees that Neild himself didn’t hand back
the money. Months ago, when the famous at-
mospheric-gas bag burst, an outcry was raised
against the gallant bombardier, and he was called
upon to refund i>3oo received for commission in
floating the concern. This he declined to do,
urging that if he did so it would amount bo an
admission that he had not honestly earned the
money. Plaint then, by refunding this .£350,
unasked, the Field-Marshal would lay himself
open to the inference that he was not entitled to
the money, and that he had not come by it legiti-
mately, and could not retain it with an approving
conscience. Though the refund may have saved
Neild’s seat in the eyes of the Assembly, stdl it
must have a most damaging effect on the judgment
of the electors outside the House.
At time of going to press the Federal
majority in Queensland was 5525. South Queens-
land outside Brisbane supplied 26,000 votes, and
the anti-Bill majority was only nineteen votes.
Central Queensland gave about 1400 majority for
Federation, and North Queensland nearly 8000.
Only Brisbane was distinctly hostile. Yet that
fine old penny inaccuracy, the Brisbane Tele-
graph, was busy informing the public at latest
advices that all South and Central Queensland
were anti-Federal, and that the only thing to be
done was to cut North Queensland adrift, and let
it go and be a separate State, while the South and
Centre preserved their proud attitude of anti-
Federal isolation, and swapped lies with each
other in their cave about the great danger they
had escaped. If South Queensland dreams of
cutting away the Federally-inclined districts, it
will-'have to saw the province in two just south of
Gym pie, and throw all the goldfields, and copper
and,tin fields, and half the railways, and almost all
thb available public lands overboard. In fact, it
will have to throw overboard everything which
makes Queensland the great province it is, and
go Mjgfcfc under.

Maoriland grandmotherliness is getting a

When Pius IX. died in 1878, the late
Melb. Archbishop Goold got a Pontifical flag
made, and flew it on the bell-tower of St.
Patrick’s Cathedral. After the Requiem Mass
the flag was lowered, and lay hidden away up in
the tower for 21 years, until Archbishop Carr was
to be welcomed back from Rome. Then some old
hand remembered the dusty, time-hallowed flag,
and it was unearthed and run up once more.
Which recalls that when Card. Cullen died in the
early ’7o’s, and Dr. Goold was having a Requiem
Mass for him, he got a Cardinal’s hat made to put
on the sham coffin, which hat was afterwards put
away in a press. And when the Archbishop, and
the sacristan, and old Dean Fitzpatrick, and
Father O’Sullivan, and the rest were all dead and
gone, and the building was finished, the old
sacristy was broken up, and a Cardinal’s -hat dis-
covered therein. Nobody knew anything about
it. The hatter’s name was branded on it right
enough, but the hatter was gone, too. Then some
said that Card. Moran had forgotten it when last
in Melbourne, and would be “calling about a
hat ” presently. And some said Archbishop Carr
had been made a Cardinal and was keeping it
dark. But when the fuss was at its height an Age
scribe solved the problem, and the old hat Ceased
from troubling.
“ Spotter ” to The Bulletin :
How they do thing's in Westralia. In dirty weather the
barque City of York, bound for Fremantle, hove in sight
of Rottnest Lighthouse (12 miles from Fremantle) late in
the evening of July 12. Barque signalled for a pilot, and
was answered from the lighthouse by a “ flare-up” light
—the light which in any civilised land indicates an ap-
proaching tug or pilot-boat. Consequently barque
steered in the direction of the light, expecting to pick
up a tug-boat or pilot, but on approaching close to the
lighthouse found neither in attendance, and discovered
t hat the light was on lane 1 . The vessel had by that time
been lured too close on a lee shore to wear off again, con-
sequently went on a reef close to the lighthouse. The
catastrophe occurred about 7 p.m. on Wednesday even-
ing. Yet news of the disaster did not reach Fremantle
(12 miles off) until midday Thursday. Then the Harbor-
Master, with a fully-equipped and manned steamer, went
off to render assistance, but on arrival at the wreck
found too heavy a sea running to attempt a rescue of the
men on board (the barque’s own boats having been washed
away), so the steamer returned to Fremantle. On the
way back it met the small steamer Dunksley en route to
ihe scene of the wreck. The latter steamer lay to, close
to barque, and the brave skipper put off in the
small boat and rescued seven men from the wreck.
The remainder of the crew, bar those lost, had already
swum or were washed ashore. How does this look for
the lifeboat and pilot service at Rottnest, and the fully-
equipped Govt, steamer Penguin ? The board of enquiry,
which consisted of the Collector of Customs and a P.M.,
found “that the drowned captain was guilty of gross
carelessness in navigating his ship,” &c. The verdict is
strongly resented by nautical men and others, who attri-
bute the cause of the disaster to misleading signals from
the lighthouse instead of carelessness on the part of the
late captain. It was admitted by the officer in charge of
the lighthouse, at the enquiry, that no code of. signals
was supplied him, and that he.could not communicate with
passing vessels. Since the catastrophe, the authorities
have decided that telegraph communication between Rott-
nest and Fremantle is necessary. (The niggers and other
prisoners escape from the island by wading ashore at low
water, and are generally far away before their escape is
reported. The authorities have consequently a long
chase after them, but they make up for the want of tele-
graphic communication by giving them an extra-large
flogging when captured).

A South Melb. man who lately bought a
ferocious-looking watch-dog to look after his
house, returned home the other day to find the
place cleaned out, and the proteotor of his hearth
stolen from the kennel.
In Napier (M.L.) the red blind was the
local sign of Venus, and the honest women there-
abouts were always particularly careful never to
have anything glaring about their windows. One
day a bank-clerk arrived in Napier from the
South, vvhere the red blind was not known, and
immediately he set foot in the city a house-agent
buttonholed him and leased to him for two years
a very comfortable house on the best end of Little
Beach. The worst end of Little Beach is very bad
indeed and every house on it at that time had red
blinds. The bank-clerk’s wife noticed this and,
thinking they looked cheerful, decked her own
residence with lurid window-gear. A delirious
whisper ran round Napier, and quite an army of
males strolled past, and the bank-clerk’s wife was
delighted,:. tp think she had secured a house on
the favoritej promenade. The fact that his new
clerk livediln a house with red blinds was quickly
mentioned 'to the bank manager. That evening
the manager himself strolled by and boldly winked
at the clerk’s wife who was sitting by the open
window. ! It was the time of the half-yearly
balance and the clerk was back at the bank, when
about 9 p.m. the lady was astonished to hear
someone rattling a walking-stick against the front
door. She opened it and a gay young dog stepped
in and oheerfully chucked her under the
chin. He was followed by three other gay
dogs and they all walked into the little drawing-
room, sprawled themselves all over it, called the
lady “ Tottie,” and made mysterious allusions to
English ale. The bewildered lady thought four
lunatics had got loose,-so she smiled sweetly and
asked them to excuse her a few minutes. They
said “Certainly, Duckie ! ” The blushing lady
retired to the kitchen and asked the maid what it
meant. The maid thought hard for a moment,
and then a look of horror came over her face, and
she gasped, “It’s them red blinds!” Then she
explained. Two minutes later the johnnies were as-
tonished to see the lady re-enter the room with two
kettles. One threw her a silk handkerchief, and
received in return a kettle of boiling water. Then
the other kettle flew among them, and the scalded,
startled dudes hurled themselves howling through
the open bay window and made a bee-line for the
nearest chemist’s.
A promising infant of 14 returned to his
home at Brunswick (Melb.) one recent afternoon
and, some trifling matter having annoyed him,
proceeded to make things sultry for his mother
and sisters. Having first expressed his displeasure
at their general behaviour, he started to ad-
minister personal chastisement, whereupon they
escaped to the bedroom and barricaded the door.
Not to be baulked, he then procured the family
axe and made a vigorous onslaught on the door,
and the beseiged women were only saved from
further castigation by the appearance of a con-
stable who had heard a sound of falling timber
and was cautiously reconnoitring. The determi-
nation shown by this youthful native should either
land him at the top of the tree or at the end of a
rope fastened to a limb thereof.
