N THE SUPREME COURT OF QUEENS*
LAND.
Mr. Justice Real.
1899—N0. 106.
In the matter of the Estate of THOMAS
HEN DERSON ANNETT late of George-
street Brisbane in the colony of Queens-
land Civil Engineer deceased intestate
Between
QUEENSLAND TRUSTEES Limited
Plaintiffs and RICHARD CHARLES
FRANCIS ANNETT Defendant.
WHEREAS by a Judgment of our Supreme
Court the following inquiries were directed to be
taken : (1) An inquiry as to who were the next of
kin of the abovenamed intestate living at his death
and whether any of them have since died and if so
who are their respective legal personal representa-
tives. (2) An inquiry whether JAMES JOHN
ANNETT the lawful brother of the said intestate
is living or dead and if dead when he died and
whether he left any and what will and whether he
was ever married and if so when and to whom and
whether there were any children of the
said marriage and who are his legal personal
representatives. And by a further order
of the said Court the said inquiries were
directed to be taken by advertisement. This
is to Notify JAMES JOHN ANNETT if
alive or any persons claiming to be interested in
the abovementioned estate through the said James
John Annett if dead or any other person claiming
to be the next of kin of the said THOMAS HEN-
DERSON ANNETT deceased that they are on
or before MONDAY the thirtieth day of OCTO-
BER 1899 to come in and prove their claims at my
Chambers Supreme Court Brisbane or in default
thereof they will be peremptorily excluded from
the benefit of the said Judgment. Any person or
persons who can supply any information concern-
ing the said JAMES JOHN ANNETT or in any-
wise relating to the inquiries above referred to are
requested to communicate with me before the date
abovementioned. FRIDAY the third day of
NOVEMBER 1899 at eleven o’clock in the fore-
noon at my said Chambers is appointed for hear-
ing and adjudicating upon the claims.
Dated this twenty-sixth day of August A.D. 1899.
(Stamp) J. BLOOD SMYTHE
Registrar.
W. H. Wilson and Hemming
Selborne Chambers
Adelaide-street Brisbane
Solicitors for the Plaintiffs.

APPLICATION for SHARES to bo MADE to O. E. ROBINSON, 81 Queenetreet, Melbourne.
“The hapless Hapsburgs.” In a word,
the “ Mishapsburgs.”
Geo. Henderson, for six or eight years the
mainstay of Illawarra miners’ Labor organisa-
tions, continuing quietly but thoroughly the work
which J. B. Nicholson, now M.P., began, has just
retired from the secretaryship of the Miners’ Asso-
ciation. Henderson’s retirement is a pity, for he
was one of the most cautious, long-headed men in
theN.S.W. movement.
“ Sicovit ” says something :
N.S.W. anti-Federalists, during the recent campaign,
made a loud noise about “ the necessitous States,” and
howled about the beautiful prosperity of N.S.W. Tire
prosperity of N.S.W. ! Where is it? Writer was re-
trenched from the public service of that province five
years ago, and being a professional man, first tried prac-
tising his profession, llesult—couldn’t make salt, for
Govt, does all his class of work. Since then has applied
for, he believes, hundreds of billets in every conceivable
walk of life, and never once received even a reply to an
application. At 12-years-old writer entered for an exant.
in England ; was the youngest competitor, and came out
on top. At 21 went for another exam, in another line,
and was specially complimented by examiners. Since
then whenever he has met his fellows in competition has
held up his end of the log. Has wide experience, and
sheaves of recommendations. If he can only exist —not
live—in this community, what show has a person without
his experience of even doing that, without cast-iron luck?
Writer would bet —if he had the cash—that there are
hundreds of men in Sydney capable of running almost
any business successfull3', if given an opportunity, who
cannot secure any employment whatever in this “pros-
perous ” country.

Something is After You.
Far away, in the Jungle of Central India, a
village lies asleep. Only a solitary human figure
can be seen, following the winding jungle path in
the moonlight. It is early night, and the native
who comes has been delayed on his way home.
But what is that dark shadow that crosses the
path behind him ? Quite oblivious, the man
marches on. He looks neither to right nor left
nor behind him, where crouches that dark shadow.
A few yards more and he will be out of the jungle.
A few minutes more, and he thinks he will be
sleeping in the bosom of his family . But see, the
Shadow moves ! With two noiseiess bounds like
a giant cat the tigress is upon him. One despair-
ing scream and all is quiet. Bitten through the
head, the victim is snatched up and carried by the
grim man-eater to her lair in the hills. Meanwhile
the village slumbers peacefully on.
Was it the man’s fault? Yes, I think it was.
Had he not lingered, he would not have been
caught. The tigress would not have attacked him
in broad daylight. But he was no worse I think
than those in this country who are to-day doing
the very same thing. Thousands and thousands
of English men and women have a shadow in their
path. It is after them. The time will come, if
they do not take heed, when they will feel, like
Mrs Lydia Golding, “as if something had over-
taken” them. Here is a letter from her in which
she tells her experience:—
‘‘All my life I had been a strong healthy woman,
and up to the autumn of 18911 never ailed any-
thing. At this time I began to feel weak, weary,
and tired, and as if something had overtaken me. I
had a foul taste in the mouth, my tongue being
furred, and a sour fluid would rise into my mouth.
I had no appetite to speak of, and the little food I
took gave me no strength. After eating I had a
heavy weight and pain across my chest, and a
gnawing feeling in my stomach. I belched up’ a
deal of frothy fluid, and in the night I awoke with
a suffocating feeling. _ Cold clammy sweats broke
over me, and what with loss of appetite, and not
being able to sleep at night I soon got so weak
that I found it hard work to get about.
“ On December, 23, 1892 (one Sunday morning),
whilst preparing breakfast for my husband, I was
suddenly seized with paralysis, which affected the
whole of my right side. I had no use of my hand
or leg on that side, and a numbness took me on
both left and right sides. My husband got me to
bed, and fetched a doctor from Northfleet, who gave
me medicines. After this I lost my strength
rapidly, and what I suffered I cannot tell.
“I could not rest night or day and I was in and
out of bed every now and again. I got little sleep,
only dozing off for a short time and then starting
up. I was afraid to be left alone, and often in the
night I have been so nervous and frightened I
could scarcely bear it. I took medicines of all
kinds, but was little better for anything until one
day in August, 1893, my husband read in the paper
“Weekly People, ” of Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup, and got me a bottle from Perry and Son,
Chemists, High Street, Gravesend. After taking
it a short time, I began to gain strength, and got
stronger, and stronger until I was free from the
effects of the seizure. I now keep in good health.
You can publish this statement if you wish. ”
Yours truly, (Signed) Mrs. Lydia Golding, 12,
Carter’s Road, Perry Street, Gravesend, May 14th,
1896.
What was this shadow that had crossed Mrs.
Golding’s path? What was it that “overtook”
her and laid her helpless and suffering upon a bed
of sickness? Surely you can recognise the mon-
ster that sprang upon her as the dreaded disorder
Dyspepsia, which attacks you when you are most
defenceless and weakest. Paralysis; yes, Mrs.
Golding had it, and could feel it, but what she
could not feel was what it was that had so acted
upon her nerves and muscles as to cause them to
become so helpless. Nor could she, I suppose,
understand how Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup
cured her so quickly, though we know now that it
was because the dyspepsia was got rid of.
But, as the native was never heard of more, so
there are some who cannot get out of the clutches
of Dyspepsia, even with such help as Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, if the help comes too
late. Hence the best way of all, say I, is to keep
out of the jungle after dark ; and if you must go
walk quickly.

“A Living Example.”
Liver Troubles
and
Indigestion.
All the Credit to
CLEMENTS TONIC
The Case of*
MRS. W. MATTHEWS.
(For the “Newcastle Herald.*)
Mrs. William Matthews, who resides in
a pretty little cottage in Elder Street,
Lambton, is a living example of the efficacy
of Clements Tonic as a representative medi-
cine. Some years back she was a terrible
sufferer with liver complaint and indiges-
tion. Now, for a lady of 64 years of age,
she is hale and hearty and able to walk
about with freedom instead of being bur-
dened with pain.
These facts were mentioned in Newcastle,
and a reporter was despatched to enquire
into the truth of them.
“I shall never forget the benefit I de-
rived from Clements Tonic,” was the lady’s
first remark when the reporter explained
his mission. “ I had often felt so bad that
I really thought I could not live many
months, and yet a few days after I com-
menced taking Clements Tonic I felt re-
freshed.”
“ What were your ailments, Mrs. Mat-
thews ? ”
“ I had many ailments, but I fancy liver
troubles and indigestion occasioned the
most misery at times ; the soreness became
so acute that I could scarcely move about to
do my home work, and I always felt tired—
the first thing in the morning as well as the
last thing at night.”
“ Did you see any medical men ? ”
“ Yes. The doctors told me I wanted a
change. They said the sea air was too
strong for me and suggested that I should
go into the country. ”
“ Did you go ? ”
“I went to Singleton for a few weeks,
and that climate made me feel better in
comparison, but I never felt as well as I
did through taking Clements Tonic.”
‘ ‘ Was your illness of long standing ? ”
“For 3 or 4 years I was very bad with
liver trouble and indigestion. I used to be
attacked with terrible pains in the chest
and between the shoulders, aud at times I
could scarcely move about. My appetite
seemed to have left me. I had no desire
for my meals. I used to eat just to keep
up my strength, and what little I did eat
used to cause me great suffering by reason
of the indigestion.”
“ Could you obtain any rest at nights ? ”
“Many and many a weary night I have
put in. I have often prayed for dayligln
to come, and when I got up I used to have
that same old tired and languid feeling
Life was a perfect drudgery to me, nothing
seemed to do me any good. I could rtol
bear the slightest noise, and the smallest
trifles would have the most distressing
effect upon my nerves.- I was down-hearted
beyond expression.”
“ Who suggested Clements Tonic ? ”
“I think,” added Mrs Matthews, “that
it was one of my sons. I have one an
engine driver on the railways and anothei
is a student at the University—or it may
have been a neighbour that suggested it.
At all events I got a bottle, and I give all
the credit to Clements Tonic for the bene-
ficial change that has been brought about in
my system.”
“How soon did you experience relief ? ”
enquired the newspaper man.
“ Before I finished the first bottle,” was
the grateful lady’s immediate answer.
“ I took three bottles altogether. It
made me feel quite a new woman. I could
eat and sleep afterwards like a growing
girl, and I had no bad after effects. My
liver seemed to have righted itself, and the
indigestion pains gradually passed away.
I am seldom troubled with indigestion now.
I give all the credit for my cure to Clements
Tonic. I hope I shall never want a few
shillings to buy it, because I regard it as a
woman’s friend.”
“ Have you any objection to this testi-
mony being published ? ” enquired the re-
porter, holding up his shorthand notes.
“You can make what use you like of it,”
replied Mrs. Matthews. “Clements Tonic
has saved my life, and I am not afraid to
■own up to it.”

HOVERING
Between
LIFE AND DEATH.
The Nurse’s Remedy.
Mr. Rowe’s
Great Recovery.
How Hospital Nurses
Recommend Clements Tonic.
Reported for the “Redfern Times."
Ifc was a narrow squeak for me, and no
mistake,” said Mr. VVm. Rowe, of 76
Garden Street, Alexandria, Sydney, to the
reporter.
“So I should imagine,” said the news
paper man, who was taking notes of Mr.
Rowe’s remarks.
“ Yes, I didn’t know what was going to
happen when the doctors at the Hospital
decided to operate upon me for the
purpose of removing an abscess in my side.
However, I knew it was no use making a
#uss about it it was do or die; so I let
them do it.”
‘ ‘ And they did ? "
“ Oh, yes ! They removed the abscess all
right, but I was for a time hovering between
life and death. In fact, I was in such a
dangerous condition that when a friend of
mine, who had heard of the danger I was
in, called to have a look at me, the doctors
refused to allow him to approach my bed.
The slightest shock at that time might have
put an end to my existence. Well, to cut a
long story short, my side gradually healed
up, and it at last became possible for me to
be taken home, but in such an exceedingly
weak state that it’s a wonder I ever got my
strength back. I was a mere skeleton. My
skin was drawn tight over my bones. It
was only with great difficulty that I could
move about at all.”
“ You couldn’t work, then?”
Mr. Rowe laughed. “ Why,” said he,
“ if you could have seen me then you would
have wondered how I could muster enough
strength to stand upright. I was a mere
ghost of what I used to be before I went to
the Hospital. I couldn’t eat properly and
my food did no good. I was in a state of
complete sheer weakness.”
“ Well, how did you get your strength
back ? ’’
“Just before I left the Hospital I asked
some of the nurses, who were very attentive
to me, what I should do to put strength
into my enfeebled frame and a bit of flesi-
on my bones.”
“ What did they tell you to do ? ”
“ They said to me confidentially, * You
bake Clements Tonic, and you’ll pick up
your strength soon enough.’”
“ Which you did ? ”
“ Yes ; after I left the Hospital I thought
of their advice and I determined to put it
into practice. I bought a few bottles of
Clements Tonic, and it didn’t take me long
to find that the nurses had recommended me
the proper and a good remedy. From the
time of starting with Clements Tonic I began
to put on flesh and daily to gain a little
strength.”
“ Did you take any other medicines ? ”
“ No; when I started with Clements
Tonic I put aside all other medicines and
stuck to that entirely. I was delighted tc
find that by the use of this remedy ]
continued to very steadily impi I could
soon eat splendidly. I got a fair appetite.
I slept soundly. Previous to taking Clements
Tonic my sleep was very unrefreshing—but
now I always gob a perfect night’s rest. J
felt cheerful and as though there was more
vitality in me. I continued to improve 11
this way till I had completely recovered m.'
lost flesh and was as strong as before n>
illness.” , „ m
“ And this is the result of Clements ion.
°“ Yes; I can say positively, without the
'• east exaggeration, that it did all this for me.
ft built me up from a physical wreck to a
man full of strength and vigour.”
“ I suppose a great many people knqw oi
this ? ”
“ Yes, it’s pretty well known.”
“ And you’ve no objection to our paper
making it still further known ? ”
“ Not the least in the world.

COUNTRY TRIAL ORP€R RESPECTFULLY SOLICITED.
Re-Staged—"For One Night
Only.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hubbard slapped his pipe on the table. “Con-
found it all ! Hear this, Brotnby ! It’s from
Cottenham, up at Labuan. ‘ Dear Alick, this
will be handed to you by the Hon. Percy etcetera
Lushley. He’s a sou of the receutly-inanufactured
Lord Gallonby (beer !). He’s also a hideous snob ;
but, as he wants to reahlly see things, don’t you
know ! the Governor asked me to gi ve the cub a
line to you. Couldn’t well refuse Sir William, old
chap ; and, if you’re anything like the Alick Hub-
bard of old times, I suspect our young friend is
likely to see things with a vengeance.’ . . .
Pretty cool, isn’t it, Billy ?”
Billy Bromby turned over in his hammock.
“Lushley,” he repeated. “Wonder if it’s the
chap that was at Winchester with me ! Don’t
remember his front-name, but his father was in
the beer-line, at all events. More than half gaby,
the fellow was, and I suppose the title’s brought
out the snob-element as well. Where is he, any-
how ? ”
“At the Mission-house, I take it. It was
Father Thibaud’s boy that brought this. Here—
Tabash ! New white man at the Mission, eh ? ”
On Tabash’s cheerful countenance arose a por-
tentous smile. “ Eya, Tuan—yes, long as so”—
with hand held high above his head. “ Much-
hair, like—like api.”
“ Balk , Tabash—good ! I’ll come over. Cursed
nuisance ! ” Hubbard growled then, as he hunted
for a clean suit of linen. “You’ll have to see to
the loading-up of that sago to-morrow, Billy, for I
suppose I’ll have to look after this gilded youth a
bit ”
The cheerful Bromby flopped out on to the floor.
“That’s all right, old man. And perhaps he’s
not so bad after all, though he has hair like api
—whatever that is.”
“It’s fire. Man’s redheaded, I suppose. You’ll
never make a Malay scholar, my abbreviated
young friend,” and Hubbard disappeared, growl-
ing.
In half-an-hour he came back, laughing. “ It’s
your Winchester friend, sure enough, Billy. With
a mighty effort he recollected your insignificant
existence. ‘Vulgah little pusson,’ he says you
were. ‘ Awf’ly vulgah name, too,’ he was good
enough to add.”
Bromby—like many another man of “family ”
was democratic enough, unless you told him so !
His brick-red face turned scarlet.
“ Confound the swipes - selling cad 1 The
Bromby family
“Produced a real whtvO man in Billy of that
ilk ! ” puc in Hubbard. “ Otherwise I wouldn’t
have repeated the puppy’s words. But I’m not
sorry to see you kick, for I want you to lend me a
hand. I’ve a plan for getting rid of this conceited
animal, and taking it down a peg in the process.
Get your boots on. ”
Bromby promptly pulled a pair of very long
boots on a pair of very short legs “ Say on !” he
said —“ thy servant heareth ! ”
“ Well, this fellow can talk nothing but pirates.
He came down the coast in old MacTavish’s
schooner, and that veracious mariner, it appears,
crammed him with horrors until he nearly jumped
overboard. Now that he’s here safe, I suppose his
native insolence is on the mend again.”
“ But there are some pirates in Borneo still,
aren’t there ? ”
“ Yes; and head-hunters too ! —but that sort of
thing has been all knocked on the head hereabouts.
Not but we have an ex-pirate or two amongst us,
yet—Rogash, for instance.”
“ Always did think that old ruffian was no
good ! ”
“ Universal custom, my boy, in his youth !
And you won’t be wise to call him an old ruffian
to his face, for it was to fetch him up here that I
got you to put on your boots. Off you go, now;
and when you come back you’ll hear all about it.”
* * * *
Rogash had lost an eye, an ear, and three fingers
of his left hand. Small-pox had stippled his
countenance, and a sword-cut had made com-
partments of it. But the remaining eye was still
as bright as the wits behind it, and the old man’s
courage had not grown grey with his hairs. He
laughed as he listened to Hubbard.
“A good jest, Tuan ! lam with thee ! It will
mind me of old times ! Leave thou the matter to
me—l will have all things ready to thy word !
Would it were other than play ! ”
“ What a ferocious old tiger-cat it is ! ” Bromby
said —looking after the quondam cut-throat, as
he strutted away, bristling with sanguinary
memories. “ Are you sure he won’t slash the
Honorable Percy over the head in good earnest ? ”
“ Not he ! If the times permitted, he’d take a
man’s life as gaily as a flea’s —but he won’t break
his word. The ruffian and the scoundrel are often
totally distinct animals.”
* * * *
Father Xavier Thibaud was too well-bred to
acknowledge such a horror, even to himself, but
never was host gladder to get rid of guest. A
Frenchman of the best type—the genial little mis-
sionary had nothing in common with an English-
man of the worst —shallow-souled, mangy-minded,
nationally and individually conceited to the last
degree.
Said the priest to Hubbard, “ Your com-
patriot is more than impossible —he is incr-r-
-edible! ”
“ He’s a beast, Father Xavier —that’s what he
is ! —though you’re too kind-hearted to say so.
But we’ll relieve you of him for a day or two, at
all events. Here he is! ’Day, Mr. Lushley!
Will you come down the coast a bit for some
shooting with Bromby and myself ? We can pro-
mise you decent sport.”
The Hon. Percy—tall, fleshy, fiery-haired and
foolish-beaked—might jret have passed muster
decently but for the cultivated superciliousness of
his expression.
“ Oh—yaas !” he drawled with emphasised in-
difference. “I’ll gow if you’ll promise not to baw
me too much. Found all you Bawneo people in-
suff’wable baws, so faw ! ”
“ Insufferable bores ! “ translated Hubbard for
the puzzled priest. “But I hope, Mr. Lushley,
you make an exception of our good Father Xavier
here ! ”
“ Well, you see, yaw good Fathaw, as you
rathaw absurdly call him, is a foreignaw, and
foreignaws are always baws ! ”
“ You hound ! ” thought Hubbard. “ I’ll make
you pay for that, at all events ! ”
* • * *
Breathless equatorial night; starless, above, with
heat-haze ; intermittently brilliant here and there,
below, with swarming fire-flies or with the pale,
phosphoric gleam that shot along the face of every
roller as it curved thundering over on the beach,
swamping with its tremendous fall the thousand
minor voices of the tropic darkness. The lonely
house—a Malay fisherman’s —stood well out from
a jungly point, so that amongst the piles below
the pencil-fishes made merry tracks of greenish
light as they darted to and fro through the inky
water of the slack.
With all which things the wooden-witted Lush-
ley would have been but horribly “ bawed,” if
Hubbard’s boatman had not “the solemn hour
beguiled ” with certain delectable anecdotes of the
orang pumpak— the pirate-men. Djaran had Eng-
lish enough to be intelligible, and though he had,
at Hubbard’s private suggestion, taken the trifling
liberty of post-dating his horrors by about thirty
years, they were circumstantially so vivid that
they thrust conviction on the listening Lushley.
Long time he quaked in silence —not unmarked of
the conspirators—but at last he saw a gleam in
the gloomy recital, and found a husky voice.
“ I’m pleased to heah, at all events, that they do
not genaw’ly kill the white men they kepchaw.”
“No kill?” repeated Djaran, diverging for once
into romance. “ Oh, that ’cause keepat white man
for roast ’im. White man good for eat.”
“ Good Gawd!” and Lushley jumped to his feet,
“ this is puffectly infawnal! Let me get away
from heah.”
“ By Heaven ! ” shouted Bromby, with a huge
affectation of terror, “it’s too late —we’re done
for! Get your gun, Lushley, and die like a man ! ”
Into their faces, from the ridge behind the house,
there burst suddenly out a ghastly bluish glare ;
out of the jungle a score of screeching figures
rushed down into the water and began swarming
up the piles.
“ The bawt, the bawt! ” shouted Lushley, as he
rushed round to the seaward side of the platform,
just in time to encounter a dropping fire of mus-
ketry from the water. A bulletless discharge—but
Lushley didn’t know that, and, as the flashes
partly snatched put of the darkness the outlines of
a big prahu, the luckless Honorable darted into
the shelter of the inner room.
Eor a couple of minutes the din went on outside
—of shots, and shoutings, and metallic clashings.
Then—out of a comparative silence—draggings,
and bumpings, and the sound of smothered laugh-
ter. The listener’s hair would have stood up, if
anybody’s hair ever did stand up. “ The wretches
were actually laughing over their awful work ! ”
Thenalight shone into his corner, and a dozen hands
yanked him out to where stood Rogash—a grue-
some figure, naked to the waist, and gory with the
red paint that further crimsoned his parang and
dripped from the long-dried human head he swung
from his left hand by the hair. With his single
devilish eye the old rascal glared for half-a-minute
on the abject creature that flung itself on its knees
before him.
“I implaw you!”—began the Honorable at
last.
“ Good ! ” interrupted Rogash. “ Yis one white
man much fat 1 Keepat ’im for roast! Take ’way
him! ”
“ Hubbawd ! ” shrieked poor Lushley. “ Brom-
by ! For Gawd’s sake—help ! They’re going to
roast me! ”
“No use call Hubbar’ —he kill! Brommy kill
too ! ” and the ogre pointed to a range of bodies
dimly visible in the torchlight. “No fat ’nough,
dem ! Plenty fat, you ! ” and Rogash gave his
fangs a croeodilean clash that filled up Lushley’s
measure. He dropped.
“ Here, Hubbard ! —have a look at him ! ”
Bromby called. “ Hope to Gad we haven’t over-
done it! —he’s as stiff as a ramrod ! ”
“ Pooh !—only a faint. Sheer funk ! Pretty
sort of animal, this, to give itself airs ! Rogash,
you old villain, you did it splendidly ! Hanged
if I didn’t half believe in it, myself ! Now, be off
with you ! It’ll be pasana turun (ebb tide) in a
few minutes, and, if your blessed old kapsal gets
stuck in the mud hereabouts, it’ll give the whole
show away, and no mistake ! The amas will be
all right! ”
If the idioms were lost upon Rogash, not so the
magic word amas —gold !
“ Balk, Tuan ! ” he said, and disappeared.
• * • *
“ Vauriens / ” said Father Thibaud—“ vauriens,
tons deux ! ”
Bromby, who didn’t know any French, suc-
ceeded in looking lamb-like, but the twinkle in
the priest’s eye was too much for Hubbard. He
owned-up, as coherently as laughing would let
him.
“ So, you see, when he fainted with downright
terror we carried him within sight of the
Mission, and bolted the moment he began to come
round.”
“ Well, my children, he brought it upon him-
self! It is such men'as he who do make your
nation so disliked. Still—it was a le-ee-tle strrong
—the fear of being rroasted ! If you were of my
flock you would have to make him the amende /
But he is gone ! He vent yestairday with Capi-
taine MacTaveesh ! ”
“Yes!—and Mac. took a letter from me to
Cottenham—the biggest newsmonger in the Archi-
pelago—so that when our friend gets to Labuan
he’ll be in for his ‘ roasting,’ after all! ”
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
The father of the present Marquis of Salis-
bury made a specially obnoxious Tory object of
himself, on one occasion, by suggesting that the
poverty-stricken classes in dear old England
should be fed with red herrings if necessary. Any
hungry unemployed was, in his lordship’s opinion,
lucky to be able to get a red herring. The London
mob took an early opportunity to reprove the
noble Marquis. When he was out driving with
his wife in an open carriage, red herrings rained
upon them until they were compelled to seek
shelter in the house of a friend, leaving the coach-
man to get away with a full cargo of dried fish in
the carriage.
London Times seems to have a special
Dreyfus lie-manufactory of its own. It recently
stated that Esterhazy and Henry supplied the
German military attache at Paris at the time of
the first Court-martial .with 160 documents,
including the army-mobilisation plan and divided,
the money received therefor. Well, Esterhazy is
ruined and Henry dead, so they can’t contradict
the story, but we have an Italian attache’s state-
ment that Schwartzkoppen said he wouldn’t pay
more than 10 fr. for a plan of any French fortress.
Also, the mobilisation plan could not have been
any secret. The instructions for carrying it out
must have been in print, and been thoroughly
understood by every battalion-chief, adjutant,
and sergeant-major in the army. Schwartzkoppen
could have got all the information he wanted
about that for a 5 fr. piece, and as for fortresses,
they can’t be locked up in a drawer. Anybody
can see them from the outside and any engineer
worth his salt can get all necessary information
about them for himself. The spy-yarns are mostly
bosh.

Star and Spire.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Spire in the still Night stood dreaming,
And away and above it afar—
Alone in its deep Heaven gleaming—
A white, a mysterious Star.
Was it Fate ? Was it falsehood or madness—
Your eyes, and the lights, and the wine ?
t he mood of our mirth—and its sadness ?
Your secret, my lady, and mine.
Ah ! suns which have burnt into ember
Had better die down in the West,
With a Star and a Spire to remember ;
A kiss, and a clasp, and the rest.
Each day of my life am I learning
This sorrowful lesson by rote—
On Earth stand the Spires of our yearning :
The Stars of our dreams are remote..
Sydney.
E. J. BEADY.
In Hospital.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I r was in a hospital, and I had been ill. They
had just dragged me back from, the awful
things that lurk in the Land of the Shadow. And
in the first glamor of convalescence, I had pro-
posed to Nurse Royl. She had accepted me.
To-morrow I was leaving, and the consciousness
that I had made a monumental ass of myself was
slowly soaking me through and through.
She had nursed me assiduously—had probably
saved my life. Her presence had inspired me like
a benediction, and her touch I had found the most
soothing thing on earth. I had felt towards her
as I suppose a baby feels towards its mother. I
gloried in my helplessness—my absolute depend-
ance on her. Then, in the earliest stages of con-
valescence, when the mere joy of living makes
every triviality a source of endless gratification, I
lay for hours drinking in the faint odors I asso-
ciated with her, gloating over her supple rhythmic
movements and their suggestions of boundless life.
Then—
But it was impossible! She surely could not have
taken me seriously. Royl herself would be the
first to admit my irresponsibility. She would free
me from all obligation. Gratitude I felt—but,
marriage ! —the two were incompatible. Moreover,
it was only her duty to treat me as she had.
Laafe would be well paid for saving me to society.
He knew it, and that was why I was given Royl
to myself. I would explain to her. I was sorry
for Royl, but —she entered.
I sat at the window. After a pause: “You
leave us to-morrow, Greggie ” —and the sentence
died in the faintest of sighs.
“ To morrow,” I said, and gazed fixedly at the
lawn below.
“Do you know it, Greg., we haven’t had one
nice, long talk about our marriage yet.”
She laughed softly.
“Sit beside me and tell me what I must wear.”
She drew me to her. She nestled oosily against
me. I was silent—holding myself up to my own
contempt. I knew that my courage was oozing
from my finger-tips. I mentally put up the
pathos of her position in extenuation.
“Gregory,” she said, “I want to make a con-
fession. No 1 don’t interrupt.” She held my
hand. “ Since the day I first came here, exactly
eight patients have asked me to marry them.
This is one of the consolations of our trade. I
accepted them all with absolute impartiality.
The others, each as he became strong, went back
on his promise. They all made an excuse of a
something. I didn’t mind. But now, Greggie,
even you are going to do likewise ! ”
I was young, very young, and my father’s son.
I saw only the pity of it. There were tell-tale
wrinkles softly lining her eyes and mouth, but
she was at airtimes lovable.
“Roy, Roy, how could you think it!” I pro-
tested.
“Ah! That’s what I wanted, Greg.” She
laughed in relief and kissed me lightly. I was
young. “I am glad you stand by the conse-
quences of your rash act—even if it is only in
obedience to a very youthful sense of duty. But
you, too, must retreat ”
“ Never !” I cried—l think in obstinacy.
“But, my Gregory dear, I accept patients’
offers, matrimonial or otherwise, in a purely pro-
fessional spirit. It hastens their recovery —so I
flatter myself. Then they see with other eyes. A
purely professional spirit. There was one excep-
tion, Greg. But there was a tragic gap between
our ages. I was beginning to fade—l knew it,
and he ” —she rose and went to the mirror over the
fire. “Yes,” she said, “to him I gave freedom
without condition.”
“ You evidently don’t know, Greggie,” she said
at last, “ that my domestic name is Mrs. Roy]
Laafe. Neither did the others. The doctor who
owns this establishment is my husband.”
WILLIAM H. DYSON.
The “ disqualifications ” in Premier Tin-
ner’s proposed Old Age Pension scheme are mor
numerous than are generally imagined. Beside
the unfortunate ivho has been in gaol duringthelast
ten years the man who has been gaoled for five years
at any time stands out, ditto the thirsty soul who
has been five times convicted of drunkenness, and
the malignant who has neglected his wife or
neglected to support his children ; ditto the child
of iniquity of whatever sex who is not of “good
moral character ” or has not been leading “ a sober
and respectable life ” for at least five years. Hon-
will that child-supporting clause “knock” the
veteran who wants a pension mainly because he
cannot support his children ? Who is to apply the
test of “good moral character,” and what con
stitutes “a sober and respectable life”? Under
these latter clauses, as interpreted by an eminently
respectable (ilicial prompted by the W.C.T.U
and the Charity Organisation Society, the man
who qualifies for a pension will be eminently
qualified for a halo and a harp. Strange that the
sober and respectable Government does not insist
on a record of five years’ steady church-going and
regular attendance at prayer-meeting and Bible-
class, the capacity to repeat fifteen chapters of
scripture, and positive proof that the applicant
has shaved his upper lip consistently for at least
seven years.
Reciprocity.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Old George was telling his pathetic story of a
wounded leg and a full hospital with the eloquence
born of many recitals. “An’ I come straight here,”
he said, “an’ I says to the sergeant a decent
bloke he was—l says, ‘ I gives rneself up.’ That’s
right, ain’t it ? ”
“You did,” nodded the watchhouse-keeper;
“you never told a lie in your life, George.”
“Oh, yes, I have,” corrected Old George;
“ I’ve sworn a few into gaol before now. I’m
workin’ for the police, / am,” he added, proudly,
with an explanatory turn towards the reporter.
“ Doing a bit of fizzing ? ’’ suggested the scribe.
“That’s it,” admitted Old George. “ But you
have to be careful. A chap got murdered one
night—Bunt Owens—for doin’ that. They put in
Eddie McKay for two stretch over it, but he
never done it. It was another bloke altogether
that hit Bunt with the bottle.”
“ Perhaps you were there, George ? ” suggested
the watchhouse-keeper.
“ Well” —George stroked his beard with a wise
air—“l mightn’t a-bin very far off.”
The journalist saw “copy ”in George. Had he
been a journalist of fiction or drama he would have
produced a note-book—and stopped the speaker’s
mouth on the spot. But, as he knew his business,
he merely opened his ears wide and asked casually,
“What made you start the game, George? ’’
“Why, it was that son of mine —the dirty
dawg ! Didn’t you know about him ? It was him
that got me 12 months—ain’t that right?” The
watchhouse-keeper nodded. “But I waited for
him all right, an’ I got him two years ; an’ there’s
more ready fur him if he comes back from Tassy.”
“He must be a swine,” sympathetically mur-
nured the reporter.
“Swine! Swine ain’t no name fur it! Go’n
>ut away his poor old dad—74 years of age. He’s
i dawg—that’s what he is ! ”
Old George dumped his swag on the floor of the
station, and bit off the end of a plug of tobacco.
“ Yer see, he was livin’ with me an’ the missus,
an’ never payin’ us a bean ; loafin’ roun’ with the
Rats all day ; so I gets him a billet asphaltin’, at
six bob a day. A-course, I expects him to sling
a few peg'at the end of the week—but not him!
The cow ! —if ever he comes back here agen, I got
somethin’ readied-up fur him. Why, when
he .”
“But what about the asphalting, George?”
< lid the reporter.
“ Yes ; I was goin’ ter tell yer. He never gives
us a bean of the stuff ; so I says to him, ‘ ’Arold,’
I says, ‘ this sorter thing ain’t right, yer know.
You got ter go ! ’ and with that .1 ’its him on the
’ead with a shutter-bar. Me and the missus
didn’t see him agen for a bit. Then, one night, I
hears a tap at the door, an’, when I opens it, there
was a young chap with strapped trousers an’ top-
boots, an’ a whip, as flash as yer like. I didn’t
know him at first, but the missus, she says, ‘Why
it’s our ’Arold!’ An’ so it was. Well, I was
sorry after -I chucked- him out; for blood’s thicker
than water, yer know ; an’ I asks the boy to come
in and settle himself. The scoundrel—the dirty,
mean cow !—I wish I’d bashed his skull in in-
stead ! ” ■
“How’s that, George? What’d ho do next?*'
fisked the watch-house keeper.
“Well, after he’d been there a few days, he
comes to me an’ says, ‘Dad,’ says he, ‘I ain’t
treated yer right, I know.’ ‘ That’s all right,
’Arold, my boy,’ I says, fur I was sorry for the
trouble we’d had. ‘ Well, I ain’t got no money
jus’now, dad,’he says. ‘But ’ere’s a watch an’
chain o’ mine that yer can ’ave as somethin’ off
what I owe yer.’ I felt near blubbin’ at the time,
I tell yer; an’ I says to him, ‘No, my boy, I won’t
take them things from yer.’ But the dirty
dawg ! he made me take ’em—me, his ole father,
74 years old. Then he says good-bye ; and the
very nex’ day up comes Detective Doolan with a
search-warrant. ‘ George,’ says he, ‘we want a
watch an’ chain, an’ we know you’ve got it.’
Like a fool I says first, ‘I ain’t got no watch an’
chain,’ an’ they turns the place over an’ brings it
up. ‘ George,’ says Doolan, ‘ we’ll have to putyou
in over this lot. That there watch was only stolen
las’ week ’-—while ’Arold was in my house, the
dawg!—‘ an’ you’ll be lucky if you get off with
receivin’.’ That’s what I did, too. Twelve
months for receivin’; an’ the meanest thing
of the lot was that the very nex’ day
I was goin’ ter the Agricultural Show at
Warrnambool with a shooting-gallery—a three
days’ job at two quid a day. They all knoo
I was convicted inneroent, an’ they treated
me fair while I was in ‘ stir,’ an’ I come out won-
derin’ how I was to get even with ’Arold. It
was jess two days after I was discharged that I
was going down street when Ikey Cohen
comes outer his shop an’ calls after me. ‘ George,’
he says, ‘ I thought I’d give yer the orfice. Your
son ’Arold he sold me a watch las’ week fur 15s,
an’ I see now it’s on the pawn list—inscription,
number, an’ all. I don’t wanter be hard on the
lad,’he says, ‘ but I promised it to a gent for a
quid. If you’ll let me have the quid you can have
it ?’ What sort of luck was that ? I says to Ikey,
I says, ‘ I’ll see if I can raise it, and then I walks
off. How did I get up that there hill to the
Detective Orfice ! ‘ Detective Doolan in ? ’ says I,
and he cornes out. ‘ I can put yer on to a good
thing,’ says I, and I tole all I knew. A-course,
d’rectly the demons looked into Ikey’s an’ showed
they knoo somethin’, he squealed, an’ ’Arold
was put in same night. I went up to the court to
see him tried.”
“ Blood thicker than water, George? ”
“No dam fear! He was up before Madden.
I was praying ter Gawd ’Odges’d get him, so’s
he’d get life, but I promise yer it was the primest
day’s sport I ever had when I seen him potted.”
“ And since then you’ve taken up fizzing as a
profession ? ” asked the reporter.
“ Yes, an’ I’ve put away thirty or forty o’ ’em.
That’s right, ain’t it ?” he proudly demanded of
the watchhouse-keeper. Then, supposing that
the scribe was a new addition to the ranks of the
“ demons,” he drew him out of the door and
whispered, “I don’t want no money ; but I’ll put
yer on to as much stolen stuff as’d fill a dray, if
ye’ll only say the word.”
